This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe.- lie has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN

SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical lip ir lof N.S. TP. L tie House Sur-
geon t > the (treat Northern Hospital, London
T am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the ti eatmrnt of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in- Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
< (ironic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph , Nov.
6th 7th nnd Sih, 1SOO) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified Hen entered the
field to fight these peats of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from the-'C diseases, who are scattered over all parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which noio exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
My Qualifications I invite .the fullest investigation Into. For some cor. slder able time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of oLservin ; the many phases of the Nervous and
Sexual System, and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatmenfcof these diseases, and when a person consults me he is informed at the
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
if I undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of those diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.
ICLIHUtfiV MEV, M K K»I, K-.lCi l<: l> .19 I.VII VOI VW H ICV
Suffering from Impaired Vitality should send for my Work on Diseases of the Unproductive Organs, which,in addition to explaining
my method of treatment, contains many useful hints as to '■'elf Management, the observance of which is of great use to all, and in many
instances is sufficient t> check the progress of di ease. This Hook will lie forwarded Free on application.
Patients residing at a distance can be treated at their own homes without interruption to their business. Medicines packed and sent
free from observation to all parts of Australasia.
Variocelc quickly and permanent!y cured without operation. Diabetes an I Tumors treated by a totally new mMhod of Cutaneous
Medication. Consultation Hours—From 10 ami. to 4 p.m . daily (except Sundays); and from 7to 9 p.m.on Tuesday and Saturday
evenings. Consultation [either by letter or in person) Free.
Address all Communications
Dr. M. PERRY, M.R.C.S., L.S.A., See., Elmley House, 151 Elizabeth-st., Sydney, N.S.W..

SATURDAY, MARCH 14, 1891.
The Farce of Constitution-Making.
The Bulletin was the first journal in Aus-
tralasia to organise the Australasian senti-
ment ; the first to systematically set itself to
undermine the contemptible provincialism
which had its root and branch, not in the
hearts of the people, but in the petty
jealousies and paltry ambitions of “colonial”
politicians. Through evil report and good
report, The Bulletin has never lost sight
of the ultimate destiny of the Australias :
a Federal Republic. It welcomed every
symptom of the growth of the federal spirit ;
it has by the force of invective, the force of
argument, and the force of satire sought to
stamp out of life and being every tendency
to provincialise the people of each “colony”
and of all the “colonies.” The magnificent
conception of a United Australasia which shall
by the expansive power of its own energies
create a new nation, and a new nation, more-
over, which shall teach the world how to
enrich the national life by ennobling the
quality of its individual manhood, and by
eliminating from the social system the dis-
integrating and degrading agencies of caste,
monopoly and privilege, first received form
and substance in these columns. Other
journals have fluctuated in their grasp of the
Australian principle: at one time, their sense
of the greatness of the Australian ideal has
reached the pinnacle of insane enthusiasm; at
another, it has penetrated, in the strange
declensions of character which witness to the
power of the capitalistic purse over the Aus-
tralian press, to the abysses of Little Pedling-
tonian prejudice. The Bulletin fearlessly
claims that from its first publication to the
present it has issued no number that
has not been consistent (wherever else it
has been weak), to the principle and
to the ideal involved in the phrase
of “ A Free Australia.” There have been
moments in its career when it might have
coined gold by pandering to a “ colony ” and
to a “colonial” “statesman.” Wherever
else it has consciously or unconsciously
swerved from the right and the light, here,
at least, it has been stable and devoted. It
has crystallised the right and focussed the
light until at last it can say without egotism
and without a departure from the truth, that
the Ideal is blending into the Actual.
Wordsworth speaks of an old man who all
his life long had kept before him in the
refectory where ho -broke his fast a picture
of the -Last Supper, and who, by strenuous
gazing, came to believe the pictured repre-
sentation real—
“ And he was fain invSSt
The lifeless thing with life from his own soul.”
“ Here daily do we sit,
And thinking of my brethren, dead, dispersed,
Or changed, or changing, I not seldom gaze
Upon this solemn oompanyyinmoved . .
By shock of circulTtßtffnce or lapse of years,
Until I cannot but believe that they—
They are in truth the substance.”
Within the lifetime of this journal, largely
because of its life-work, the faint imaginings
of the political dreamer have clothed them-
selves with reality. They have merged from
the Imaginary into the Possible, from the
Possible they have advanced to the Pro-
bable, from the Probable they can, if the
men of the Convention mount the hill
of opportunity, grow to-morrow into the
Present.
* * *
Why does The Bulletin thus aggrandise
its own relation to the Federal movement ?
Is it not lowering itself to the ignoble plane
on which a Parkes lives, and moves, and
has his parasitical existence ! Parkes never
lifted his little finger yet to achieve the
paltriest deed for the public that he did not
seek to blazon the significance of his act on
the brassy heavens. The true worker works
for the sake of the work and the sake of the
result. The sham works for the ephemeral
notoriety which any man can earn who is
vulgar enough to cater for the crowd. Why,
then, does The Bulletin assert it s claim to
be recognised as an agent in the Federal
cause ?
* * *
It does so because, from the consistency of
its past, The Bulletin has a right to be
heard as to the future ; and because, in now
raising its voice to censure the work of the
Convention, so far as that body has done any
work, it may not be truly accused of infidelity
to the Federal movement, or to the ideal
based on the Federal principle—a Republic
of the Federated Australias. The Bulletin
would be untrue to itself, and to the people
whose support has enabled it to grapple with
every form of public wrong with perfect
confidence as to the issue, if it did not
declare that, speaking from the heart of its
experience, it cannot bat regard the Conven-
tion as a huge mistake. The Bulletin hoped
almost against hope that when the Conven-
tion assembled in all its majesty as a gather-
ing of the select-men of the Australasian
provinces, it would justify its existence by a
declaration of its faithfulness to the Austra-
lian ideal. It has done nothing of the sort;
it has started with a farce ; it can hardly
help closing its session with a blunder ; it
will be in the mercy of Providence if it
does not consummate a crime.
# # #
Placed in the midst of “ pathless summer
seas” and isolated alike from the tumultuous
West and the “ unchanging East,” Australia
was left practically undiscovered till the Old
World had been partially regenerated by the
most awful of ordeals, and till the New
World of the American States had demon-
strated to the Universe how much greater
the democratic instinct is, as a creative
national force, than the traditions of
monarchies and of empires. In the order of
Providence or in obedience to evolutionary
law, state the fact as you will, this virgin con-
tinent, a paradise in its promise of freedom
and felicity to the groaning millions of
Europe, was defiled consciously and
of malice aforethought defiled—by the
“ greatest nation under the sun.” That
original sin against the destinies of the Aus-
tralias was the first of along, an interminable
series. Prating of liberty to her colonies
always, of progress ever, Britain never con-
ceded a privilege, never abandoned a right
that was not wrested from her. Her classes
—not her people, but her ruling and domi-
nant classes—did not treat this country as
their footstool only because they had used
it as their cesspit. They sent out to
administer her affah’s the bastards or the
toadies of her great men. Their notion of
colonisation was to steal the lands for their
spawn and to find places for their parasites.
Till the gold discoveries this principle of
government operated. Then, a change
took place, but it was one of aspect only ;
the essence of the policy was the same and is
the same to-day. “ The Australias are still
‘our colonies. ’ They are for our exploitation.
We may not crush them ; in spite of all they
have grown too strong for us to do that.
But we can bribe them, emasculate them,
frighten them, tickle them. Bribe them
with our wealth, deprive them of their man-
liness by gratifying their love of show and
vanity, frighten them by telling them that
without us they are at the mercy of Russia
and Germany, tickle them by applauding
their ‘greatness’ and their ‘progress.’” Now
the nation which says this is a nation which
is so rich that millions of her people lack
bread ; which breeds lords, but cannot breed
a man who does not sooner or later rise to
curse her ; which is so strong that one great
naval defeat will debase her to her enemy’s
feet; which is so glorious that the outer skin
of her magnificence envelopes a deadly
loathsomeness like that of the Dead Sea
fruit. It is this class-ruled, caste-ruled
country that seeks to keep the Australias in
subjection. And so long as she does so, so
long will the Australias never attain to the
measure of their national greatness, or a
single son of Australian soil grow to the
height and the dignity of his individual
manhood.
# # #
These are truisms familiar to every man
who has steadily kept his eye on the Austra-
lian ideal. But will you find a solitary
expression of like significance in the volume
of calk already uttered by the members of
the Convention ? Not a syllable ! Nothing
to indicate that a syllable of the kind
will be ever uttered by any delegate !
.Where the delegates, speaking outside or
inside of the Convention, have been led for
a moment to glance at the crux of the
Imperial connection, they have studiously
evaded coming to close quarters with it. The
root-question of Federation is not the
tariff but that of States’-rights What
powers shall the individual States delegate to
the Central Government, and what reserve ?
But inextricably implied with the question
of States’-rights is this : Is an Australian
province, a free and sovereign State '{ The
Bulletin contended months ago that it
essentially is so, though not in name. The
Sydney Morning Herald has just come to
the same conclusion. No other paper that
we know of has made the same admission,
and the members of the Convention have
displayed an equal taciturnity. The dele-
gates’silence may be attributable to ignorance
of the bearing of the question—-or it may be
—we do not mince our terms—to cowardice.
We believe it to be the latter. We do not
think the picked politicians of the provinces
can be so unintelligent as to be in ignorance
of what every superficial student of Federal
institutions must be informed.
For an assertion, implied or direct, of free
and sovereign State-hood involves a Declara-
tion of Independence. A Sovereign State
cannot be a dependency, cannot remain a
“ colony.” The Sovereignty of a State is the
inalienable right of its people. It cannot be
alienated. To establish a “ Federation under
the Crown ” such as every delegate who has
yet spoken has virtually confessed to be the
object of the present Convention, there
must be an alienation of essential Sovereignty,
The Crown is Sovereign, otherwise terms
are meaningless ; and for the Crown—
an outside and exterior power—to hold
the Sovereignty of the Australias is to com-
pel them to continue in a condition of
dependency. Substitute, however, for the
Crown a President elected by the people,
and he becomes the expression of their
Sovereign power. There is no alienation of
the Sovereignty ; there is simply a temporary
delegation, periodically resumed. The sub-
stitution of a President for the Crown
would, by one act, solve the problems and
straighten out the anomalies which must
otherwise entangle the feet of the constitu-
tion-makers at every step of their way. The
delegates have talked for a week on the
power of veto. As scientific legislators they
should adhere to the recognised meaning of
terms and phrases. The power of veto, in
the judgment of every publicist of repute is
essentially a prerogative of the Sovereign—
call the Sovereign President, Queen, or what
you please. The delegates have been discus-
sing it as though it rested solely in the hands
of what, following Parkes’ example, they
choose to call the Senate. Over and above the
Senate is the symbol of Sovereignty, which
is to be, if the Convention proceed on the
lines already laid down, a Governor-General
appointed by the Crown and responsible
only to the Crown. This is what we mean
when we say that the Convention is per-
petrating a blunder, and possibly a crime.
Because its members dare not—if they dare,
what would become of their titles ?—face the
problem, of the Imperial Connection, they are
consenting to an alienation of the sovereignty
of the people who ostensibly chose
them to preserve their national interests.
No people can constitute a nation unless
they are free; no people are free whose
sovereignty is a prerogative of an exterior
power ; and, gloss the matter as one may, a
nation that cannot work out its destiny
without reference to Imperial interests is
not a nation at all. It is merely a colony of
dependants and not of freemen.
How Rents Keep Up.
Mb. Albert Dawson, of Mittagong (N.S.W.).
writes to The Bulletin as follows :
Your leading article “How Rents Go Up,” in your
issue of January 31, is one which I think will commend
itself with more than ordinary force to the bulk of your
readers. It is particularly lucid, and goes right to the
bed-rock of the labour-poverty question, and its reproduc-
tion in, say, three months’ time would meet with approval
from a majority of subscribers. The position taken by you
in “How Rents Go Up” is that increase of population
practically means increase of rent, decrease of wages, and
consequent increase of poverty, and in the year of our
Lord, 1891, the position is good and incontrovertible.
Just so. Now, how docs this square with your persistent
advocacy of Protection ? What would be the result of a
“ useful ” protective policy in New South Wales ? Simply
this. An additional army of 50,000 or so of labourers
would be required in order to make in the province the
articles which are now imported, and for all practical pur-
poses the effect would be—-according to your own argu-
ment—to increase rents, decrease wages, and increase
poverty. Now, would it not be wise, before we rush for
progression (?) and poverty after the manner of other
deluded countries, to work for the abolition of the social
conditions which tack increasing poverty upon so-called
progress. First obtain conditions which will allow in-
crease of population without a more than corresponding
increase of poverty, before we adopt a fiscal policy which
means—if it means anything—an influx of labour ;or the
effect, according to “ How Rents Go Up,” must be in-
jurious, anyhow to the present inhabitants of the country.
The strongest reason that can be urged against Protection
is that its introduction brings a young country more
quickly into conditions of population and poverty which
are found so deplorable in older countries, conditions
which may culminate in destructive revolution ; and
many of your readers are at a loss to reconcile your well-
known efforts and sympathy with the working classes
with your advocacy of Protection.
The above letter expresses accurately the great
argument of the Foreign-trade-mm-Single-tax
organisation in New South Wales, which con-
sists in assuming that because one mistake is
correct, therefore another blunder must be
right. Protection and Socialism, it is asserted,
cannot go together, and though the assertion is
as incapable of proof as the statement that
toothache cannot exist along with the Shorter
Catechism, or that baldness and cats are two
hostile principles, still the assumption does a
lot of service. The position which The Bulle-
tin takes up is certainly that increase of popu-
lation is invariably accompanied by increase of
rents and a general reduction of vv'Ages, but
beyond that it dissents from Mr. Dawson’s
assumptions in tolo .
lii the first place, it does not necessarily
follow that Protection will increase population,
nor is it any part of its business to do so.
There is no special blessing in the multiplica-
tion of inhabitants, and the mere fact of there
being two men where there used to be only one
does nob prove that the country is either richer
or happier or more intelligent for the change.
The object of Protection is to increase the
material prosperity of the nation, but, by some
ironical cussedness on the part of Nature, popu-
lation often multiplies fastest, and families are
largest, where there is least prosperity. When
New South Wales adopts Protection "there will
be work for many hundreds of individuals who
arc at present unemployed, but it is irrational
to suppose that there will be work for 50,000’
imported labourers in addition to those already
in the province. Protection tends to make a
nation self-supporting—to make .the one in illicit
producers of the country produce enough to’
supply the one million consumers, which vir-
tually means that one average producer will
make enough wealth to maintain the average
consumer. To assume, however, that Pro-
tection must bring in a vast influx of
new labourers is to assume that under
the protective system one inhabitant will
be able to consume so much that he must im-
port and hire another inhabitant to keep him
to produce it, consequently they would have to
import two more to help both of them, and an
additional four afterwards to assist the lot. If it
be necessary to bring in 50,0000 labourers to work
for those already in the country, then it would
also be necessary to import another batch to wor k
for these 50,000, and so on ad infinitum. In-
crease of comfort and increase of population do
not go together except by accident. There is
incomparably loss poverty in protected France
than in any other country in Europe, yet the
French nation is not increasing, while the
squalid Chinaman multiplies without ceasing.
The population of the East End of London
grows far faster than that of the West, and the
average family of the costermonger is twice as
large as that of the millionaire. Protection,
however, can neither hinder nor hurry uo this
process, and it doesn’t want to.
* * *
But even supposing that Protection neces-
sarily involved a sudden and violent increase of
population, and admitting the obvious fact that
with increase of population comes increase of
rent and consequent diminution of wages, it is
hopelessly absurd to assume that Freetrade
could reverse the latter part of the process.
The machine works in one direction only—it
positively refuses to go backwards. If popu-
lation increases, rents go up ; if population
remains stationary rents still go up; if it
diminishes rents in most cases go up likewise,
and the only difference is that they go up
more slowly. The population of Ireland
has fallen off nearly 50 per cent, and rents have
risen about 100 per cent. ; that of the North of
Scotland has declined in like manner and with
the same result. By turning a vast extent of
country into grouse and deer preserves an arti-
ficial scarcity of land has been produced, and
the alleged law of demand and supply has done
the rest. Even if there are only twelve men on
a whole continent rents can be kept expanding
by judicious manipulation. The theory that
increase of population should not be encouraged
until we have arrived at a social state which
allows of increase of population without increase
of poverty is good—as a theory, but it is
nothing more, for poverty increases all the
same only a shade less rapidly, and in any case
the increase of population cannot be checked,
except by infanticide or some similar device.
And, further, it must be remembered that the
social revolution cannot be brought about except
by the pressure of humanity ; if keeping down
the population meant keeping down the number
of social oppressors it would be reasonable
enough, but it really means a steady thinning
out of the ranks of that great army which must
one day rise to achieve the freedom of the
human race. Moreover, even if Protection did
“ bring a young country more quickly into con-
ditions of population and poverty which are
found so deplorable in older countries,” it is
only one of many agencies all tending in the
same direction, which cannot, by means of any
human effort, be abolished or postponed till man
learns how to make the best use of them, and it
is not advisable that they should. The sooner
the evil comes, the sooner will the remedy be
found, for the human race is a slow-moving
concern, which travels only under the pressure
of necessity, and never looks for a cure until
the disease is upon it.
* * *
Australia was comparatively happy and pros-
perous in the earlier days, when the population
was very thin and the country very poor, but if
the population had remained as then and the
country as poor as it was then, the landlord
and the monopolist would have come to infest
the land and destroy its happiness all the same.
They came along with the influx of population,,
but if there had been no influx they would have
come by themselves. If the census returns
declined to zero again they would remain not-
withstanding—at all events, they would be the
two last men to abandon this deserted con-
tinent. If Freetrade keeps the country poor
they will be here nevertheless, for no country is
so poor that the landlord cannot extract some-
thing from it; if Protection makes it rich they
will be found hanging round with their accus-
tomed vigour. Some day, however, the
nation will wake up and get rid of them
both, and Australia will belong to the Aus-
tralian people for the first time in its
history, and if the country has grown rich
in the meantime there will be so much the more
for the nation. Then if Protection has created
wealthy monopolies, as its enemies so loudly
assert, the State will confiscate these monopolies
for the public benefit ; whereas, if Freetrade
has kept down monopoly it can only do it by
making the country sfi poor that there is little
for a monopoly to feed upon, and then it is
obvious that there is also nothing for the State
to confiscate, and the public benefit is nil. And
finally, if monopolies, whether created by Pro-
tection or otherwise, should lead to revolution
it is gratifying to think that there will at least
be something to “ revolnshe ” for, and that the
great upheaval will pay a dividend. About the


most lamentable thing on record is a revolution
that is a dead loss to everybody concerned, and
it isn’t worth while for any nation to regain
possession of its native land if its native land
he not worth having.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Promising Freetrade Industry.
About three years ago, when the Park es Pro-
trade Free-tection Cabinet was in an earlier
stage of its wild career, it resolved to apply its
great, though somewhat mixed, principle to the
tobacco-industry, with a view to helping on
that business and boosting the prosperity of the
country ; and the consequences of its action are
now beginning to be apparent. Under the old
regime the duty on imported American leaf was
slightly in advance of that upon the locally-
grown article, and the Parkes Government saw
in this a trace of the nefarious system of Pro-
tection. The duties, therefore, were equalised ;
that is to say, there was no attempt at
establishing Freetrade, but, as the nearest
approach to it, it was resolved to discourage
the Australian industry as much as the foreign,
and then let them take their chance. When a
deputation of tobacco-manufacturers appealed
against this arrangement the Treasurer in-
formed them, with an idiotic candour that no
other Freetrader ever arrived at, that the
alteration was made became they used too much
of the Australian and too little of the American
production, and the deputation, being too much
astonished to reply, fled in confusion. They
had come to point out that the new arrange-
ment would ruin the tobacco-growing industry
in New South Wales, and before they could get
started the small, dried-up importer who ran
the Treasury, blandly informed them —almost
in so many words —that his object was to ruin
the local industry, for the benefit of the im-
porters. They wanted to state that the innova-
tion would divert the trade from Australia to
America, and the disastrous financier broke
them up by explaining that his object was to
divert trade from Australia to America, and
damn the local producer for the sake of the
foreigner. They didn’t even have energy
enough left to ask him why the Parkes Free-
trade tariff was actually heavier than the
Dibbs- Jennings Protective tariff, and if they
had asked him he would probably have been
unable to explain the matter himself; the
situation was too astonishing for argument, and
they simply retreated and left the man who
stated that he wanted to cripple Australian
industry in possession of the field. Since
then the tobacco trade has gone the
way of every other trade in which the
Parkes Government has taken a hand.
The American tobacco, grown in a more favour-
able climate and on a more favourable soil,
cultivated by cheap nigger labour, and brought
to Sydney at a cheap rate of freight, is actually
protected against the Australian article. The
African who is plentiful in the Southern States
of America is so inexpensive, and the charges
for carriage by sea are so low, compared with
the price of Australian labour and the pon-
derous railway charges from the interior, that
the Australian leaf virtually pays a heavy tax
for the benefit of the Virginian planter. At
the present moment there are 2500 tons of un-
saleable tobacco stored in the Tumut, Gundagai
and Murrumbidgee districts. The value of this,
in the days before Parkes’ latest resurrection,
was about £233,000 ; under the new system of
protecting foreign nations and calling it Free-
trade, the value has sunk to about £lOO,OOO ;
and as the actual cost of production is about
£130,000, the produce is stored up, in the hopes
that the Parkes Ministry may die a natural
death, or go mad, or get lost, and give the
country a new lease of existence. Many of the
growers have already collapsed, and the rest
hold on simply because all their money is sunk
in their plantations, and, therefore, to abandon
the struggle means ruin, while to go on cannot
mean more than ruin, and if they can hold out
longer than Parkes the disaster may still be
repaired. Meanwhile, the actual loss to the
planters is about £130,000 per annum; the
tobacco manufacturers are in a bad way ; the
consumer reaps no benefit, for prices are as high
as ever they were ; and the profit goes entirely
to the importers, and does not even remain in
the country, for the importers are themselves
imported, and the dividends go, as a rule, to
the “ head office” in London.
«, _
An Unfinished Insurrection.
The alleged Queensland insurrection has dropped
out of sight as completely as if there had never
been any insurrection at all. The story didn’t
come to any reasonable conclusion ; it merely
tailed off in dismal silence, and the narrative
broke down, and the whole perjury fell dead
for want of something to lean against. The
daily press has given no explanation of this
incoherent ending—it simply gave up the melo-
dramatic gag, and went away, leaving it lying
there. It had got the imaginary insurrectionist
out, and mounted him on an alleged horse, and
started him over the face of the earth in an
armed condition and howling for blood. Then
it got the police and soldiers and horse-artillery
up to meet him, and planted the two parties
face to face, and it pointed out the unre-
lenting ferocity and inhuman fury of the shearer,
and the cold, stern, adamantine resolution of the
troops, with such vigour that it was obvious
neither of them could back down by any
possibility. Consequently, having brought them
together the only possible thing left to telegraph
about was the battle that ensued, and the
carnage and blood and flames, and the shrieks
of the wounded and the reserved attitude of
the dead, finishing up with the siege of Brisbane
and the brutal murder of Sir Samuel Griffith.
But, unhappily, after constructing an imaginary
shearer and an imaginary rebellion, not one of
the capitalist organs had courage enough to
finish the story ; they dropped the whole yarn
when it was in danger of becoming interesting
for the first time, and let all their past asser-
tions slide. Strangely enough, too, nobody
seems surprised at this dismal breaking
off probably because everybody is used
to it. In recognised fiction, when the hero
leaps from a eliff 0000 feet high, he has got to
go right on to the bottom and be smashed,
but iu alleged fact, as it is dished up by the
S.M. Herald and the Courier, he stops short
in the air when he is half-way down, and walks
back, or else he gets lost somewhere on the way
and neither falls nor stops falling—in fact,
nothing more is said about him. This sort of
thing may be interesting, but it is very abrupt
and patchy. Even the Charge of the Light
Brigade wouldn’t have amounted to much if the
famous brigade had neither got to its destina-
tion nor come back, nor survived, nor been
killed, nor anything else—if it had simply
started at a furious gallop, in fact, and then
evaporated, and never been heard of again.
And in like manner, if the history of the
Franco-German campaign broke off in the
middle of the carnage at Gravelotte, and tailed
away unaccountably into a report of an hospital
meeting, and didn’t even mention where either
of the combatants got to, or if they got any-
where, it would probably be regarded as an
eminently unsatisfactory story. Yet the pre-
sent alleged industrial “rising” ends in pre-
cisely that fashion, and so did the last rising,
and the rising before it ; and the next one will
freeze up abjectly in like manner. And when
each supposed “disturbance” conies on the
organs of respectability call on the State to take
a lesson by the last “ disturbance,” quite re-
gardless of the fact that the last disturbance is
still unfinished, for just as the combatants were
rushing at each other with shrieks of awful
ferocity they both vanished somehow, and left
not even a hair behind them. It is ever thus,
and it gets monotonous. One complete dog-
fight would be worth more than six of these
miserable upheavals, which always get stuck
and won’t heave one solitary inch even in the
interest of “spechul edishuns.”

The Fathers of the Church.
Leo XIII, the representative of St. Peter,
has entered into a species of unwritten treaty
with the German Emperor, according to which
the Head of the Church is to lend his assistance
for the suppression of socialism, and as a step in
this direction he is going to issue an encyclical
letter pointing out to all Catholics the necessity
of obedience to any kind of Government that
may chance to be over them. The letter will,
no doubt, come in handy, for the first business
of every Government is to look after the interest
of the landlord and the capitalist; and the
socialist, having no sympathies with either of
these authorities, is bound to be against the
Government, whatever it may be; and it is
better for Leo to prop up a Protestant emperor,
or a Moslem, or a Mormon, or an affluent canni-
bal, than to see a party get uppermost that
wants all men to be free and equal. For the
Pope has everything to lose by socialism, and
when it conies along in earnest the last Head of
the Church may have to go looking for prawns
in Galilee, just as the first Head of the Church
did a good while ago. There are a good many
hundred rooms—or a good many thousand—in
the Vatican, and a good many miles of priceless
pictures and statues, and there is about the
largest library in the world, mostly made up by
hard embezzlement and confiscation ; and there
are several tons of images in gold and silver, and
enough precious stones to fill several good sized
coal-scuttles, and a collection of curiosities that
are worth as much as all the railways in New
South Wales and a lot over. And in the midst
of these there wanders around a rheumatic
patriarch with the biggest income on earth who
is no use to anybody except himself, and who is
very little use to himself either—the same old
shuffling, broken-down spectre that curses Irish
Nationalism, and German Socialism, and
Spanish Republicanism, and who piles his great
spavined anathema on everything that bears any
semblance to human progress. And along with
it goes the anathema of the Archbishop of Canter-
bury, and the Presbyterian Assembly, and the
Greek Patriarch, and the Sheik-ul-Islam, for on
the subject of Socialism the Church is exceed-
ingly unanimous, and it hangs on by its teeth
and its hair to the old cause of the
capitalist who gives about one-eight per
cent, of his plunder to the Lord; also the
principal Brahmin and the head Buddhist are to
be found on the same side, and the boss Mormon,
and the snake-charmer, and the rain-maker,
and the Baptist, and the Obeah-man, and the
Fetish-man, and every other kind of priest that
was ever invented. The only sign of cohcsiveness
among all these creeds arises out of their unani-
mous desire to squash that nefarious doctrine
which asserts that there should be no more
landlords and no more capitalists, and when the
danger grows more imminent at least three-
fourths of them will be prepared to sink all
their old differences and go the whole sacred
hog in the interests of Cash. There are as
many varieties of plutocrats as there are of
creeds and apostles, but they never fail to hang
together. The fashionable pastor grovels to the
principal millionaire in his congregation, and
the mollah of Tunis smoothes the road to
Heaven for the believer who owns a jug-full of
battered copper coinage, and Pope Leo frater-
nises with the German Emperor, and the other
kind of Pope—the greasy pastor of some
obscure Russian village, the Pope who lives in
a hovel instead of a palace—fraternises with the
local policeman, who is the only kind of em-
peror of his acquaintance. And the one thing
that all the different kinds of Popes are sur-
prised about is the irreligious tendency of
modern Socialism. Nearly every church on
earth has thrown out the socialist and damned
him, both before and after they threw him out,
and now they damn him again because he is out
and is likely to remain there.

Some Good Jokes.
A Queensland contractor—let us call him
Wiggle —recently secured a Divisional Board
contract of £9OO, for clearing a road. His
bondsman was (say) Fizzle, of Fizzle and Co.,
who happened to be also a member of the
contract-letting Divisional Board. Fizzle paid
the necessary deposit on Wiggle’s contract.
Fizzle and Co. honoured Wiggle’s cheques,
“ stuck to ” Wiggle for rations, and received
“ progress payments ” on his account from the
Board, Wiggle never handling any of the
Board’s money. The oilier clay Wiggle
appeared as defendant in a Petty Debts Court,
the joint plaintiffs being three labourers,
who sued him for Col 3s. Sd., wages
due. Wiggle nobly admitted his in-
debtedness. That was his little joke. A
telegram was exhibited from the contract-
letting Divisional Board’s clerk, stating that
the balance due to Wiggle -■£3oo -had been
paid to Fizzle and Co. a week before. W iggle,
after thinking awhile, announced that Fizzle
and Co. would probably advance him the
amount of claim on his plant, which could be
then seen from the courthouse in Fizzle and
Co.’s yard. Alternately, he was of opinion
that the plant would realise £l5O at auction.
The Bench thereupon gave a verdict for
plaintiff's ; in default of sufficient levy, one
mouth’s imprisonment. That was the joke
magisterial. When the police tried to levy on
Wiggle’s plant, Fizzle and Co. exhibited
a bill of sale protecting it. That was
the joke capitalistic. The execution order
was returned nulla bona, and W ig-
gle formally bankrupted. The same after-
noon, his plant was auctioned by Fizzle and
Co. “to redeem their bill-of-sale,” and the
greater part of it was knocked down to Fizzle.
That was the third and last joke. The trans-
action shows glaringly the urgent need of a
Lien for Wages Act, to protect workmen from
the combination frauds of bogus contractors and
capitalistic conspirators. Premier Griffith
has more than once attempted to induce the
Queensland Parliament to pass such a measure,
but his intentions have always been frustrated
by that ancient and loyal ally of capitalistic
sharks, the Upper House. If those swindled
Queensland labourers —there were more sum-
monses in anticipation, to the extent of £2O0 —-
were to loot Fizzle and Co.’s premises, the
horror of the righteously-indignant capitalists
and their Press would know no bounds. “ Pro-
putty ” would emit a shriek and call on the
Government for gatling-guns and paid swaddles
to shoot down the “ rebels.” But, because one
of their number has imposed starvation upon
and robbed a crowd of common labourers, capi-
talists merely laugh and refer to it as “ smart
business capacity.” And the law is satisfied.
For, has not Wiggle (jot a month's imprison-
ment —for a £250 scoop ?
Sydney Daily Telegraph (which apparently
wants to help its “ uncle ”) weeps because
dunning creditors cannot by law obtain a
garnishee order on a debtor’s salary when the
debtor is a Civil Servant: —
Why a distinction should be drawn in this respect
between those in the employ ot private firms or indivi-
duals, and those in the service of the State, it is hard to
understand.
The Bulletin also thinks no distinctions of
this kind should be drawn, and for that reason
it asks :—Why should a garnishee order lie
obtainable by duns on any person's salary ? If
a debtor has nothing to live on but his wages,
is it just for the State to enable a creditor to
rob him of his only means of subsistence V
Tradesmen ought to, and generally do, know
the exact risk they are running when they coax
or allow people who are on wages only to
patronise them “on credit.” The abolition of
all legal process for the recovery of debts would,
and will one of these days, cure a big social
cancer. The N.S. W. Civil Service non-garnishee
system sets an excellent example.
The wife of a San Francisco editor lately fell
in love with his friend, a business man, and he
with her. They did not abuse his confidence,
they held no clandestine meetings, simply went
and told him the truth ; he consented to a
divorce, they were married, and he attended
the wedding. The entire affair was conducted
without publicity, without disturbing the
friendly relations existing between the trio.
Those who believe in the inviolability of the
marriage tie will call such proceedings immoral,
but they are, broadly-speaking, as moral as any
arrangements dealing with the relation of the
sexes can be. The business man might have
taken advantage of his friend’s confidence, the
wife betrayed her husband’s honour, the hus-
band killed one or both to vindicate his man-
hood. Straightforwardness and a logical marriage-
law rendered dishonour and death unnecessary.
Blink it as we may, that ’Frisco divorce and re-
marriage show us the goal to which the world is
drifting—the goal (of absolute freedom of con-
tract) towards which civilised mankind began to
drift when it consented to the first divorce.
The S.M. Herald has thrown off its mask, and
has announced itself as the blind slave of the
federated capitalists, and their colleagues in
the present conspiracy to yoke in all protesting
labour. It bewails the alleged “concessions”
made to the dockers by the London Shipping
Federation, as “likely to do more harm than
good,” and accentuates its censure thus (italics
ours): —
This is true, more particularly ot that one in which the
shipowners have cancelled the clause in the Federation’s
ticket which declares that preference shall be given to
non-uniotiists The resolution to give pre-
ference to free labour had been adopted in not unreason-
able retaliation . . and the abandonment of it . . .
will very probably result in preparing “a rod for” the
Federation’s “ own back.” For experience has shown
everywhere . . . that placing free labour and
unionists on exactly an equal platform works entirely
into the hands of the unionists . . . sooner or later
must compel the free labourers to pass over to the ranks of
the union.
The Herald thus absolutely repudiates that
flaunted thing which it had formerly held up as
the sacred backbone of the employers’ creed—
“ freedom of contract.” It now, as shown
above, distinctly advocates preference to black-
legs. Yet it denounces unionists for daring to
agree with it in i-ejecting “ freedom of contract,”
thus showing that the whole question is con-
sidered by the Herald and its colleague em-
ployers as one of personal war, to be fought out
under the ethics of war only. It is an open
secret that the shipowners were so dissatisfied
with the Herald’s attitude in the big strike
that they threatened commercial vengeance.
The Herald’s present position as set forth
above ought to satisfy them.

One of the most formidable and artfully con-
trived hindrances to the natural development
of the Australian Republican sentiment is the
military and naval enthusiasm which is being
fostered here by Imperialistic officials and their
local conspirators and dupes. The baits are
skilfully laid to tickle the vanity of valorous
and aspiring youth. The haughty scions of
British “nobility,” for instance, are pleased to
condescend to receive and to mix with young
men from the kawlinies, and to permit their
enrolment, under certain disabilities,.as attaches
to British regiments, or British men-o’-war.
The youth of Australia are tempted to make
arms their profession and to look to the Im-
perial service as their only avenue of advance-
ment, thus unconsciously habituating them-
selves to regard England always as first and
Australia a poor second. What a lovely crop
of young anti-Nationalist snobs we shall have
when the Anglo-Australian naval cadet system
is in full blast !

Robert P. Porter, head of the U.S. Census
Office, on the M‘Kineey Tariff: —
We look on the Bill as simply one to foster our indus-
tries, and induce wealth to run in one channel (say), the
U.S.', instead of in another (say), foreign countries. . .
So far as England is concerned, I should call it rather a
Bill to promote the emigration of capitalist producers
and experts to give employment to our labour. Many
Englishmen are already availing themselves of the chance,
and you’ll see plenty others will follow I ll
undertake to say the Republic is to-day miles further
away from Freetrade than England is from a reasonable
tariff on articles of luxury The Republican
party favours the reciprocity that does not injure the
home market. That is Blaine’s platform. The recipro-
city which means Freetrade, or free imports of competing
products, no Republican favours. ... If the Demo-
crats were to attempt to upset the country by opposing
new tariff legislation, they would be eternally smashed in
1892. So far as the M‘Finley Bill is concerned, it is good
for 10 years.
And that is the whole matter in a nutshell.
The British Government has reduced the mini-
mum standard for the army to sft. 3in., and the
old difficulty about finding recruits still
remains, for no matter how fast the standard is
reduced the nation decays still faster. There-
fore the time may come when a robust
individual one yard high will be gladly
enrolled as a Grenadier—in fact, that time
must come according to all existing signs, for
the process of diminution seems to be incurable.
There are plenty of long-drawn-out Britons
still in existence —probably as many as there
ever have been—but the proportion of men
who are unfit for service increases every year.
They are mostly narrow in the chest, and
shaky at the knees, and loose and rickety in
the joints, and otherwise gone to seed, for
Britain is rapidly becoming a collection of
gaunt, unwholesome cities planted in an uncul-
tivated desert, and the men who are reared in
cities are a thin, angular, weary race without
either muscle or endurance. And as for the
agricultural population, it has been wiped out
by landlordism and Freetrade—that is to say,
it survived landlordism, but it could not sur-
vive landlordism and Freetrade combined. And
consequently, what was once the tallest and
most muscular nation in Europe now- passes off
any narrow, consumptive spectre of sft. Sin. as
a soldier, and even he is so lamentably scarce
that probably ere long the test of height will be
abandoned altogether, and the recruiting
authorities will enrol any bow-legged dwarf
who can pass an examination in lifting a
quarter-bag of coals, and who is willing to be
dressed up in a glaring red coat for the better
convenience of anybody who is out shooting at
the empire on which the sun never sets.
It is admitted that one of the men lately con-
victed of murder at Melbourne slew his victim
because the latter, who was <lt facto though not
de jure his wife and for whom lie had an ardent
passion, abandoned him to take up with
another. Suppose the woman had been his
wedded wife and had committed adultery and
mocked him as she passed with her man, would
not such an application of provocation to the
primal and ruling passion of the human race
have given him a claim for mercy ? Un-
doubtedly, for such claims have been recognised
a thousand times. Is then hypocrisy still so
strong that because no priestly mummery of
words sanctified their union, a man driven to
frenzy by the most exquisite and mental tortures
is to be butchered in cold blood by the State
—in what must be an utterly vain attempt
to “ deter ” the next man whom a jeering and
heartless woman drives to madness ? The judi-
cial psychology of Anglo-Saxondom is a disgrace
to ‘ ‘ civilisation. ”

The cable states that “the colonisation com-
mittee has lately been inquiring into the severe
distress existing in some of the Highland dis-
tricts in Scotland, and has now submitted a
report recommending that facilities for emigra-
tion should be offered in the distressed districts.”
It is the same old remedy once more—the same
eternal “move on”—the same weary policy of
shifting the responsibility and passing on the
burden. Only one-fifth of Scotland is culti-
vated, and still the agricultural population has
to be shunted, because the landlords have
declared that the other four-fifths shall remain
a wilderness and be used for the production of
deer and grouse, which sell better than human
beings. The average Highlander is tough and
uneatable ; also, he is devoid of gravy, and so
his landlord doesn’t want him. Besides, an
idiotic Government has made it a close season
for Highlanders all the year round, and is
thereby leading to the rapid extinction of the
nation. No doubt if the British sportsman
were allowed to go out on the moors and bag a
M‘Donald or a MTavish the sport would be
much better than shooting a miserable bird or a
shivering quadruped, and under these circum-
stances every effort would be made to preserve
the race. The same system would apply to the
bucolic population in England, and though
their value for sporting purposes would pro-
bably be limited, it would at least be sufficient
to save them from extinction. Some philan-
thropic Tory landlord would do well to look
into this idea. It is a recognised axiom that
nobody has any right to exist in any place
whatsoever unless his presence is remunerative
to his landlord, and evidently the only way to
preserve the country population of Britain is to
make them profitable as game, seeing that they
are not profitable as ordinary inhabitants.

A State Fraud.
The Bulletin withdraws its anathema of last
week, under the above heading, on the N.S.W.
Minister for Public Instruction, Mr. Carru-
thers, and piles it right on to the State and an
insensate Act of Parliament, interpreted and
administered by the Civil Service Board. The
facts relating to the New South Wales system
of Civil Service superannuation—a fraudulent
insurance fund—are correct as stated and apply
to all departments, though its unjust incidence
in regard to females is more apparent in the
Department of Public Instruction than in most
• other branches of the service. Official salaries
are forcibly docked to the extent of 4 per cent,
per annum under an Act passed in 1884. When
an officer reaches the age of 60 years, he or she
may retire upon a pension paid out of the fund
thus provided, which fund is administered by
the Civil Service Board, and belongs properly
to the Colonial Secretary’s Department. The
pension may possibly reach the dimensions of
an amount equal to two-thirds of the salary last
received, but only in the case of an officer who
first of all pays a lump sum, or promises to pay
by instalments such a sum, as would be
equivalent to 4 per cent, on the total of
salaries received by him or her from
the time of joining the service, the en-
forced payments under the Act only starting
from 1884. As a rule, however, the retiring
officer elects to take his or her chance of dying
within a year or two, and does not choose to
pay the amount of “ back dues,” so that the
retiring allowance is usually a small matter,
amounting to half, or less than half, an annual
salary. If any officer leaves the department
prior to attaining 60 years, the fund swallows
up all past payments ; if he or she remain in
the department after attaining 60 years and
die, the State still sticks to its hoarded levies.
There is, however, a pretence at restitution in
the cases of heirs left “ absolutely destitute.”
But the Civil Service Board, as already stated,
interprets the Act, and decides what is and
what is not “ absolute destitution.” It has
been explained that Mr. Carruthers has abso-
lutely nothing to do with the fund, and we
understand that he feels specially hurt in con-
nection with the case of the late Mrs. Lockett
(with which we last week merely illustrated the
system) because he strenuously endeavoured to
have something done to aid the daughters of
the deceased lady. The Civil Service Board,
however, felt it to be their duty not to
exempt this case from the ordinary applica-
tion of the Act, their reason, so far as
we can understand it, being that they
could not conscientiously regard the young
ladies as being “ absolutely destitute,”
they being over the age of 16 years and able to
eaiTi their own living. Equity strongly demands
that officers retiring from the service before
they reach the age of 60 years, should be repaid
the amount which the State has forcibly de-
ducted from their contract wages, and, in the
case of officers dying in the service, their legal
heirs should benefit to that extent. The in-
justice falls with special cruelty upon females
marrying out of, or dying in, the service. The
State simply forces on all its officers an Insur-
ance discipline that is no Insurance at all. The
system is a burden hated by all officers as a
stand-and-deliver robbery under the guise of
philanthropy. An Amending Act is urgently
needed, either making the contributing to this
bogus insurance fund purely optional, or pro-
viding for refunds of impounded payments as
outlined above. Such a measure can only be
introduced by the Premier or the Colonial
Secretary. Here is a chance for Parkes to
make himself sweet with the Civil Service vote
before the next general elections.
The latest report of the London Dock Labourers’
Union throws another interesting side-light on
the much-vaunted decline of British pauperism
under the beneficent influence of Freetrade.
The Union now consists of 45,000 members,
and in December last no less than 30,000 of
these were and had been for some time out of
work. Every one of these 30,000 men were
paupers except for the one circumstance that
the State gave them no relief—they were
paupers barring the fact that the advantages of
pauperism were denied them, and consequently
they appeared in -none of the Government or
parochial returns—in fact, they were tacitly
reckoned as citizens in affluent circumstances.
Including their wives and families they totalled
probably 100,000 destitute - persons in one
branch of industry alone, or an addition in one
single trade anil in one single town, of ten per
cent to the aggregate pauperism of the kingdom.
And their destitution was as utter and complete
as destitution well could be. They lived, and
are mostly still living, on the miserable dole
which the Union was able to scrape up from the
contributions of the 15,000 men who were still
employed—or, in other words, each working
member, 'out of his maximum income of sixpence
an hour, was supposed to support two other
members and their families besides himself and
his family. And at last, when the tremendous
strain became unendurable it was seriously
debated whether this aggregate of about 100,000
human beings should not throw themselves
unanimously upon the poor law, and swarm
bodily into the workhouse, supposing—which is
eminently improbable—that there was room for
even 100 of them, let alone 100,000, in that
receptacle for rags and misery. The scheme
was abandoned by reason of its obvious im-
practicability, and consequently the pauper re-
turns of the prosperous British Isles escaped a
very serious inflation, but the reality is there
just the same. Pauperism decreases in England
because thousands and hundreds of thousands
of poor are supported by the trades-unions
instead of by public taxation, but when one
solitary union in one seaport, maintains 100,000
unemployed out of its funds alone, and when to
this are added the innumerable other unions of
London, and of the rest of the British cities,
the vast mass of the poor who belong to no
union, and the great army of acknowledged
paupers, the total passes comprehension.
Mr. Edmunds, M.L.A., intends, when New
South Wales Parliament meets, to move for all
papers, declarations, &c., re fhe case of one
Henry Myers, who, on 10th September last,
was sentenced to seven years’ penal servitude,
he having been convicted of having, on 3rd
June, stabbed his wife, Mary Myers, in the
breast with a pocket-knife. Two witnesses,
whose evidence assisted to convict him, have
since, it is stated, made statutory declarations
to the effect that they and Mary Myers
committed perjury, and that the woman
stabbed herself in order to bring trouble on her
husband, and bribed them to bear false witness.
The two female witnesses already alluded to
should be gaoled for perjury in any case. If
these witnesses spoke truth at the trial, they
have perjured themselves in the statutory
declarations. If they speak truly in the statu-
tory declarations, then they perjured them-
selves at the trial. What does Attorney-
General Simpson think about it ?

An illustration of the gross injustice and un-
representative results of plural voting occurred
at the recent municipal elections at Mary-
borough (Q.) The voting in this particular in-
stance put in one J. M. Stafford with 544
votes, the scoring record further placing as
they here follow 2, J. T. Murray, 509 ;3,
Mitchell, 502 ; 4, John Norman, 434 ; 5,
Thos. Long, 234. The municipal electoral sys-
tem of Queensland enables the scrutineers after-
wards to analyse the voting, and that sort of
scrutiny showed that more ratepayers had re-
corded votes for Norman, who was fourth, and
only polled 434, than for Stafford, who was
returned, on 544 votes. Stafford’s majority
was made up of about a gross of plural votes,
or dummies, and he now sits as the aldermanic
representative of bricks and mortar and beer-
engines ; while Norman, the man elected by
human beings, is out of the running. The in-
cidence of plural voting is ten times more in-
jurious in Parliamentary polls than in municipal
elections. Which is why the one-man-one vote
is the first step towards the achievement by the
common unpropertied people of a truly free and
full franchise, and the downfall of usurping
dominances.
That portion of The Bulletin which includes
our leading article was at press during the
time Sir George Grey was delivering his
great speech in the Federal Convention, and a
distinct exception from the sweeping condem-
nation of our remarks in that article must be
now made in favour of the noble old Maori-
lander’s attitude to States-rights and the Im-
perial Connection. Differing, as we venture to
do, from Sir George in our . views of the im-
portance of the Empire to these provinces, we
still welcome his utterances as the most notable,
the most admirable in temper and spirit, the
most A ustralian, of those yet enunciated on the
Federal Question either in Parliament or in the
Convention. From the position he therein
defined as the true one to be occupied hv the
Federated States of Australasia it would be but
a step to the Federated Republic of Australia.
Without stress and without strain the Republic
would naturally arise from the Federation—an
Elective Governor-General is but one remove
from an Elective President! The one logical
blot in Sir George’s speech was that, while
he claims for the Federal Government a para-
mount authority and denies the free and sove-
reign statehood of the several states, lie, not-
withstanding, would assure to each individual
state supremacy in the matter of its own
Constitution. Now, an integral element
in a State Constitution is the right to
surrender to the Federal authority certain
State powers. The right of saying which
powers shall be surrendered and which re-
tained is, says Sir George, not to be with
drawn from the individual States. But the act
of selection is itself an assertion of supremacy.
Here, then, Sir George is inconsistent. In one
other respect he is inconsistent. No—we will
correct ourselves : not so much inconsistent as
magnanimous. The Empire he eulogises as the
nurse of freedom and the mother of enterprise
is the same Empire which, when he was guilty
of deeds of far-sighted statesmanship and unique
liberality, condemned him as a “ dangerous
man,” and to this very hour, if we test Imperial
action by its own standards, insults and out-
rages him. Sir George, whose heart, so far as
the public has been able to discover, has never
thrilled to one sordid impulse, is a “ dangerous
man ” because he fought Power, Caste, and
Officialdom. But it is with forces that are in-
capable of aught but revenge, that the Nestor
of Australasia desires us to retain our alliance !
Grey is betrayed by his very greatness.
Like Wagga, the West Coast of JST.Z. has
gained an evil pre-eminence in murders. This is
ascribed, by a local journal, to insanity caused by
the excessive use of doctored tea.
Mr. Charles Arthur Moresby Billyard, of
Sydney, who is about to marry a wealthy Tas-
manian lady named Leake, gives notice of his
intention to change bis name to Leake, in accord-
ance with the will of the testator from whom the
lady derives her fortune. Thus Charles in marry-
ing loses his maiden name.
According to the Sydney Workman, the
inmates of the local Home for Fallen Women
have amusements about twice a week “in the
shape of ‘ musical chairs ’ and ‘ blindman’s-buff ’
after tea.” The director “is the only gentleman
present.” We further read that of the 27 inmates
fully five are pianists, one of whom got honors in
the Royal College of Music.

“Colleen Bawn” ran very smoothly at
Sydney Her Majesty’s on first night to a good
house, but, probably, the most lasting impression
conveyed by it to the Australian mind will be the
remarkable sameness of Irish scenery. Following
another Hibernian play, folks in front mixed up
appellations after the fashion of Irish stew and
talked freely between acts of Arrah Bawn, Myles
the Post, and Danny Feeney. As the Colleen, a
character that is pulled by one string only, M aggie
Moore does not obtain the all-round show she had
with Arrah, and her apparent failure on compari-
son is due simply to the weakness of the one
character made more manifest by its following so
closely on the other. The Myles-na-Coppaleen of
Jack Sweeney is a good rough-and-ready perform-
ance, butwantingin theslynessandthe physicaland
mental light-heelednessthat isMyles’characteristic
Harry Jewett’s Danny Mann is very ably handled
and to be excelled only by that of one built for the
part, cradled in it and schooled in all its traditions.
The Ann Chute of Roland Watts-Phillips is, per-
haps, the worst thing she has ever given us, her
brogue doesn’t fit and she lacks the easy dash,
abandon and careless grace necessary for Ann’s in-
terpretation. By the way, the Colleen Ruadh is
usually played with an auburn wig. Arthur
Greenaway also lacked levity—in fact, the ten-
dency of the whole show is towards ponderosity.
The Hardress Cregan of J. W. Hazlitt is just
supportable, the Father Tom of Stirling Whyte,
safe, the Corrigan of Frank Harcourt best in re-
pose, and the Mrs. Cregan of Mabel Tracey
suggestive of the high heels and pearl-grey tights
of pantomime. When we say that “Colleen
Bawn ” will probably run until it is turned off,
we are not speaking rashly, for George Rignold,
who lias retired for a well-earned spell, seriously
worsted in his encounter with the Irish language,
has so many good things in store that he doesn’t
know what to do first.
¥£ TT?
“ Master and Man” has fairly caught on
at Sydney Royal, despite the fact that a good
deal of tommyrot is sandwiched in with stuff that
might be more fitly mated. For instance, folks
are bored for about ten minutes by the ambitious
Jack Walton, described as “ a man of brains and
education,” and his wife, with some drivel con-
cerning Farls, Farlesses and Earlets, after the
fashion of Johnny Sheridan’s “He’ll be a count
and I’ll be a counter,” that is positively depres-
sing. The baby—not Lohr’s heiress, it seems—
takes kindly to stage-business, but, flushed with
success, has lately dropped into a Snazelley
fashion of gagging that is likely to mar its
future usefulness. Yet the audience seem to like
its inconsequential remarks, and some night we
expect to see their appreciation take the practical
shape of feeding-bottles and soothing syrup. This
idea is suggested by a rumour that the “gods” have
serious thoughts of visiting the abbatoirs in quest
of liver for Beelzebub, who is getting a trifle podgy
for active business. It is proposed to send a re-
quisition behind requesting Albert Norman to
refrain from saying “ Ameriker,” and someone
with a genius for figures has calculated that the
word “ damn it occurs seven times in each act
is the best laughter-getting line in the play.
Arthur Lawrence is rapidly losing the adiposity
which he is so proud under the weight of
Logan’s hump, but everything points to a pros-
perous season while there is a shred of him left.
Our illustrations of the show on another page are
calculated to give folks who “ never visit the
theatre on principle, my dear,” a good idea of
what it is like. Premier Parkes has invited the
Federal Convention to the Royal on Saturday
next.
* * * Hi
“ The Jilt,” preceded on the last evening
by “Honour Bound,” was replaced at Sydney
Criterion by “ School ” on Tuesday evening. Its
chief feature was the return of George Anson and
Emma Temple and the induction of Miss Romer
as the instructress into the goloshes vacated by
Mrs. Bolton. All the other parts are filled as on
the prior occasion, and (at the time of writing we
hadn’t seen the show) probably in the same good,
old style. Maggie Moore will make her first
appearance at this house in “The Schoolmistress,”
by Pinero, at Easter.
m * * *
At Sydney Garrick the tuneful opera of
“ Mignon,” by Ambroise Thomas, Annis Monta-
gue’s instructor, is excellently staged, and on the
whole, capably dealt with. The opera is at all times
well worth hearing, simply on its own merits,
and apparently could hold the stage during the
rest of the season. Annis Montague, in the name-
part, on first night was in good voice and made
the most of evei’y opportunity, and the new
singer, Edith Moore (a pupil of Lucy Chambers),
also a soprano, as Filina, sang with a finish and
ease not often exhibited by a debutante. Charles
Turner’s cold is on the mend, and he was all right
as Wilhelm Meister, while Warwick Gainor’s
Lotario, with quieter music than he has lately
handled, was, perhaps, the best thing he has yet
shown us. Cicely Staunton, as Frederick, made
a small hit, and her “Here am I in Beauty’s
Room ” got a big call. Percy Shannon played
Laertes, but was chiefly remarkable for his mou-
stache, which would have done credit to Buffalo
Bill and gladdened the heart of any bottle-washer.
Houses are keeping up. “Fra Diavolo” is an-
nounced for Saturday, and “Faust,” “ Lucia di
Lammermoor,” and “Martha,” follow at brief
intervals. Turner has also two new operas, Van
Brnhl’s “ Golden Cross,” and Goldmark’s “ Queen
of Sheba,” in reserve.

The Melbourne Cyclorama people are
about to build another building in Bonrke-street
for the reception of the Siege of Paris, but Sydney
folks are quite satisfied to get along with Gettys-
burg.
* -* *
The Alathea with Arthur Gordon’s Con-
cert company on board made two successful trips
down Sydney harbour on Sunday last. She had a
big cargo in the afternoon, and at night was
crowded from stem to stern though there was lots
of room on deck. Harry Rickards, who sang in
the evening, was rapturously encored (he appears
again next Sunday afternoon), and Arthur Gordon
made a hit with the “ Dream of Eugene Aram,”
which, we need hardly say, is one of the finest
things for recitation ever written.
*# * «
Fernandez, one of the pluckiest aeronauts
that lives, made his sixth or seventh air-jump at
Bondi on Saturday last, and alighted about two
miles off, near Waverley. The professor is going
to travel, but may be induced “to do it again”
before he skips.
** * *
The School of Arts Vaudeville Company
continues to attract good audiences principally
through the efforts of Charles Fanning and Dan.
Tracey, who are now assisted by “the great Mel-
bourne favourite,” George Williamson, a gentle-
man hitherto unknown to us, but supposed to
have come over as a delegate to the Federal Con-
vention.
m* * *
At Wild America last Saturday Dr. Carver
smashed up 201 balls in 4min. 43sec., according to
the tally of a committee of journalists, and ten
just-caught wild bullocks were ridden in the arena
at one time by cowboys, Indians, Mexicans and
Australians. As one steer had cleared the camp
on the previous evening, knocking down tepes
and hoisting up benches, things were a bit lively.
Next week the Doctor smashes 500 balls.

a match is pending between Griffo and Murphy.
On March 21, George Walker, Mr. Larmour’s
factotum, takes a benefit, when a big programme
will be got through.
** * *
The Dual Bathing Club continues to boom
along at Sydney Natatorium where the pellucidity
of the waters is only excelled by the snowy white-
ness of the ladies’ toe and finger tips. A Cabinet
Minister is said to be a candidate for election, but
will only be admitted if his career will bear
feminine investigation.
“ Devil Caresfoot,” a comedy recently
tried in Brisbane with apparent success, was-re-
ceived with a respectful yawn at Melbourne Bijou
on Saturday night. There is little action and no
humour in the story, and all parties concerned
are more or less objectionable, with the exception
of a young man in knickerbockers who goes away
at the end of the first act and doesn’t return until
the audience is dropping into a gentle doze. Dur-
ing the long absence of this estimable but colour-
less young person his girl is worried and tricked
into marrying a libertine, on the understanding
that he is about to die and will relinquish all con-
nubial rights in return for the privilege of making
over his estates to his bride’s father. The delicate
maiden puts these private conditions on half a
sheet of paper, which the “ Devil ” duly signs, and
after the marriage ceremony she retires to her
own apartments and set ks comfort in the supposi-
tion that her liege lord is dying as fast as possible.
Whereupon the good young man turns up
with scorn in one eye and disgust in
the other, and curses her before the wedded
virgin has time to explain that the other
fellow is an unimportant circumstance, who will
shortly become food for worms. Then the vir-
tuous lover clears out, and the moribund bride-
groom comes sneaking in with a view to breaking
his solemn agreement, to which end he begins
chasing his lawful spouse round the sofa. How-
ever, the lady’s father intervenes at this interest-
ing stage, because he had a vague suspicion that
his son-in-law might crown a career of villainy by
attempting to embrace his own wife. The young
man also returns once more, though it is quite
evident that he has no business there, and enjoys
the satisfaction of seeing the “Devil” roll dead
from his chair. The excitement of pursuing Mrs.
Caresfoot on their wedding night has been too
much for the enfeebled reprobate. Everybody
cries “Dead!” and the curtain falls, leaving
a tired congregation to wonder whether
there will be an inquest on the body
and spicy details in the newspapers, or
whether the affair will be discreetly covered
up like the death of Sculptor Boehm. A four-act
play which is at once dull, unpleasant, and devoid
of moral purpose, will scarcely do much good for
the Bijou exchequer. Nor is Miss Janet Achurch
likely to increase her number of admirers by ap-
pearing in the tedious, unsympathetic character
of Angela Caresfoot. She is frequently ungrace-
ful in gait and gesture, harsh in utterance, and
bluestockmgy in style. Her acting throughout
is powerful rather than pleasing, save when she
moves the house to enthusiasm by a very womanly
cling to her departing lover. Charrington’s
elaborated and Irvingesque performance of the
name-part has a certain air of being stronger than
it is, and his make-up strikes the eye. Herbert
Flemming is nice as usual, especially about the
stockings, and the others have few opportunities
to seem of much account. Special exception may
be taken to a hat worn by Miss Lillie Bryer, who
picks up a hand-glass and positively expresses ad-
miration for this hideous head-gear. “ Devil
Caresfoot ” is elegantly staged, and may last for
a second week. On the other hand it may not.
** * *
At the Opera House there was only a
moderate attendance for the opening night of
“Kindred Souls,” a show which suggests a lemon-
squash spiced with cayenne pepper. A company
including Snazelle, Gourlay, John Forde, Wallace,
Hodson, Fanny Liddiard, Aggie Kelton, and
others, cannot fail to give some dignity to this
series of marital complications, and the fooling
has at least a polish on it. Nevertheless “Kindred
Souls ” is no more than a glorified music-hall farce,
and if the people engaged were to come on
separately and give “ turns ” they would go a good
deal further and probably fare no worse.

“The Fortune of War,” as adapted from
the French and produced for the first time at
Melbourne Royal, is a gorgeous military pano-
rama appealing to an old-fashioned sense of rever-
ence for national flags. The story, which goes
back to the gory times of Buonaparte, tells how
two French soldiers in particular, and the army
in general, are dying for love of a cantinibre’s
daughter, a sweet young creature who travels
round with the troops and contrives to remain
innocent all the while. The eantinierc promises
her girl to the suitor who gets his promotion
first, and then they all tear away and fire squibs
at the Austrians until it appears that one of the
rivals has saved the Flag. Consequently the
Commander-in-Chief promotes this hero and puts
him in charge of the Flag, and then the fair bone
of contention chooses the other soldier as a
matter of course. Under these circumstances
the promoted person naturally curses the
sex, gets drunk, and wants to fight, so
the Commander-in-Chief deprives him of his
Flag, and hands it over to the young woman's
other mash. Some more battle occurs after this,
and the Austrians are on the verge of getting the
Flag when it is providentially grabbed by the
cantiniere’s daughter, who puts it under her shawl
and thanks Heaven that France has still got its
stupid bit of bunting. But the other lover (who
has gone over to the Austrians in the meantime)
comes along and catches the girl. They struggle
together until the maiden inadvertently discloses
the precious Flag to view. The sight of this
sacred emblem is too much for the man who has
taken up with the enemy, and he repents on the
spot. Then the Austrian commander clanks on
•and wants the Flag from the girl, and when she
says she hasn’t got it they stick her up against a
cardboard cross and shoot at her. But the re-
pentant warrior gets in the lino of fire
and dies in place of the girl, after which
the French Army swoops upon the scene,
and the maiden produces the Flag, and it is
unfurled amidst demonstrations of joy. The
Lord only knows what would have happened if
the Austrians had got hold of that flag, because
France had no money to waste upon a new one at
the time. Such human interest as the drama con-
tains is confined to the three people who mono-
polise the Flag, but the scenery and opposing
armies are laid on regardless of expense and may
keep “The Fortune of War” going for awhile.
The violence with which several warriors tumble
down a ravine after being slain by fireworks is
thoroughly inspiring, and quite worth the money
charged for admittance. Charles Cartwright is
vigorous and full of dramatic effect as the soldier
who goes wrong and repents, albeit his monotone,
so appropriate in the part of Cyrus Blenkarn,
is now rather wearisome. Lawrence Cautley
plays the successful lover in an inoffen-
sive fashion, whilst Sass, Harold Rus-
sell, Oily Deering and a lot of other capable
people contrive to look more or less heroic in
regimentals. The comic element, as represented
by Greville and George Walton, is weak and
washy, but the supers seem pretty bloodthirsty,
considering how little they get for keeping up
their courage. There are but two female charac-
ters. Miss Jenny Watt-Tanner represents the
elderly eantinierc in the garb of everyday life,
which seems against all stage tradition, and Miss
Olga Nethersole undertakes the impossible r6le of
the artless daughter in an ultra-emotional manner.

At the Adelaide Royal “ My Sweetheart ”
has taken the place of “Hans the Boatman,” and
the Stacey Company have scored another success.
On Saturday the “ Silver King ” Company, headed
by G. R. Ireland, opened to good business.
Wybert Reeve, who shortly takes over this
theatre from the “phirm,” is now in Melbournein
search of attractive novelties for the coming
season. By the way, a tremendous dust-storm
last Saturday evening so darkened the auditorium
that an alarm of fire was raised, and, in the
general stampede, a man’s leg was broken. Quiet
eventually restored, the performance was con-
tinued, George Rignold was badly wanted, how-
ever, to allay the panic by singing “Rule
Britannia.”
