They will no doubt scon be here—lrving and Terry.
They are getting played-out in London, for “Raven's-
wood,” a good original play, luxuriously mounted, well
acted, was frosty, not kindly, houses paperhangered. The
old set appears quite to have moved on from the Lyceum,
(he public crave for j 7 outh, and it is just as it was with
Mr. and Mrs. Kean, and is getting to be with Mr. and Mrs.
Kendal, who don’t act at all in London now, hut are
ripening up for Australia to pluck the autumn fat, fair,
and forty fruit. Enthuse, enthuse!
It seems to be with the actresses nowadays as with the
cricketers. They must be young, and this is horribly
painful for a woman approaching the shivery age of 30,
just when Balzac says they 7 are at their nicest, with a pre-
ference tor the other side—his Femme de Trente Ans.
As soon as her shoulders begin to round, antiquity gently
snowing flesh on them, as it were, she is “ a goner,”
Alfred Oellier, after submitting his new comic opera to
J. O. Williamson, and without result, has taken the
manuscript back to Londbn, where Nellie Stewart may,
after all, make her first “ home ” appearance in the prin-
cipal character.
William Elton surprised Thespiana by announcing
his Melbourne farewell benefit. Elton is a real loss to
London, for in every impersonation he wraps himself in a
character, and is not a mere comic lecturer like Greville or
Toole. Take his Dr. Cupid, and parts in “ Erminie,”
“ Pepita,” “ Dorothy,” “ Marjorie,” “ The Gondoliers,”
and “ Hands Across the Sea.”
The grand hit in the “Miner’s Right,” Melbourne
Alexandra, is the reference to Eureka Stockade. It re-
mains now for a dramatist to make this the centre of a
whole piece, boldly introducing Lalor and Co., and the
Australian flag. Give ’em the fight, and glorious triumph
of the Red Lobsters, with the policeman swarming up the
pole, and tearing down the Southern Cross flag before
jumping on it.
A London critic writes on the advance of the stage from
“Formosa” to “The Dancing Girl,” for, when “Formosa”
came out, mighty was the howl. But just compare it
with the “ Dancing Girl,” which is high art. Fish, fish,
how art thou fleshifled ! France conquers, and they will
stand “ Hero and Leander.”
One Ike Elwell, well-known as a circus clown in Eng"
land, died of poverty last Christmas w r eek. He couldn’t
hold out long enough to get a fresh engagement and be
called “ Mr. Merryman ” again.
A good yarn is told about a lengthy performance of
“ Monte Cristo ” at one of the London theatres, about
20 years ago. The show 7 had dragged on until 1 a.m., when
George Belmore was interrupted in the middle of a long
soliloquy, by the enquiry “ Shall you be long, sir?” from
a polite sufferer in the pit.
John Brunton will be scenic artist at Melbourne Royal
for Coppin & Co., Limited.
As a young man, the late Cardinal Newman actually
composed a comic opera. It couldn’t have been very
comic. True piety chiefly consists in lack of humour.
One of the latest London sensations was the pan"
tomime of “ Beauty and the Beast ” at Drury Lane. Lady
Dunlo, as Beauty, wasabig draw, but the real excitement
was the Beast. Miss Vesta Tilley was transformed into that
unhappy animal on the middle of the stage ; she is walk-
ing across it in human shape, and suddenly she turns into
the Beast in full view of the audience, and no one can
guess how the thing is managed. This show has
caused a lot of speculation, but, after all, it isn’t quite
unique. For example, we knew an Australian girl
once who married a beautiful young nobleman from
“ Home,” and he also turned into a beast in full view of
the audience and with very little notice. Moreover, he
remains a beast to this day. The transformation was
brought about by hard drinking, we believe.
“Mrs. Langtry,” says a Yankee writer, “is nothing if
not daring. Even in her playfulness she is often
audacious. Physically, the Jersey Lily is about as near
perfection as women grow. True, she has not the sinuous
grace and ophidian suggestiveness of the Bernhardt; but
her limbs are models of symmetry, and her feet, although
too large, are as shapely as those of the Venus de Medici,

and as soft and pink as a baby’s; hence they look nice
bare in sandals, the obligato style of foot-gear when one
plays the wild and wilful Cleopatra, who had a playful
habit of kicking the bedclothes around in a lively fashion
when Anthony stayed out late at nights. But if there are
parts of the Jersey Lily more beautiful than others, they
are her shoulders* They are—and I speak from autopti-
cal experience—sublimely splendid, especially when
viewed together with the arms, for these are not only
finely shaped, but they make junction with the shoulders
in the highest style of workmanship known to nature.
Now, having these’transcendent merits, it is not, I opine,
at all astonishing that the Jersey Lily, should yearn to
show to the world just how beautiful she is. This is how
she has done it: In one act, her jewelled bodice, which,
of course, is decollete, has very slight shoulder-straps, so
that when her arms are raised the eye gets the full, mag-
nificent sweep across her shoulders and out along the
upper arm. In order to display the symmetry of the
junction of her arms to her trunk, she cuts such long
armholes that the lower edge is on the level with the
bust-line. The effect may be imagined. It is positively
startling, especially when those beautiful arms, large,
full, round, and white, flash angrily in the air as she pours
forth her hot indignation or paints the agony of her
jealousy. But be calm, I beg of you. The Jersey Lily
will ere long bring her Cleopatra, then you may see for
yourselves. What I am waiting to see is whether our
swell girls, ever ready to copy the toilets of actresses,
will dare to adopt Cleopatra armholes.” Whereupon Tub

concert in Sydney on April 8. All the leading musicians
have volunteered. He goes to Italy, so it is said.
The Frances Saville farewell concert takes place in
Sydney March 14.

amateur, who recently passed successfully at the College

Texas Jack, while endeavouring to cut down a hung
horse-thief with a rifle bullet inHarmston’s “ Wild West
at Brisbane, damaged a boy’s eyesight. The lad, unfor-
tunately for the Harmstons, was the son of a local lawyer,
who has served them with a writ for £5OOO. Unable to
find bail, they have been arrested.
Mrs. Lyne-Stephens—Marie Louise Duvernay —a British
high-kicking female who made a pile out of her legs some
40 years ago, and then married a wealthy husband, and
who has since thrown her limbs about only in the paths
of calm domestic bliss, has endowed a church at Cam-
bridge to the extent of £lOO,OOO. And now, we presume,
they will hang up her seven-league tights in the taber-
nacle, as a holy, though somewhat abbreviated relic, and it
will be told in after years how the exhibition of her ex-
tremities was indirectly the means of leading many
sinners to repentance, and how her legs were a bright and
shining light generally.
“ Good parts make good actors,” is a favourite saying
on the Point, and, cully, there’s a lot in it. Now look at
Charles Cartwright, who electrifies Sydne3'andMelbourne
in “The Middleman,” and ask his comrade Sass how honest
Cartwright stood in London when he left. Was he
reckoned “ in it ” with Warner and Terriss ? “ Virtue can-
not so inoculate us out of the old stock,” unless a Middle-
man part comes along. See how Willard was kept under
by an infinitely inferior actor, Wilson Barrett, until luck
came in his way. So don’t let your spirits go down,
Brown.
Says the leading Dublin weekly : —“ Henry Irving w; s
lately presented with a sheep (probably the frozen Aus-
tralian, sent by Johnny Toole), and with characteristic
generosity he had it cut up and distributed among the
poor and hungry of the East End of London.” The feast
of the loaves and fishes was nothing to this, for—
Sims Reeves gets more for singing now than when he
was at his best, consequently he is working the “fare-
well ” business to death.
Santley sang four songs, all chestnuts, “ Maid of
Athens,” “ To Anthea,” “ The Erl King,” and another, at
his first reappearance at the London Monday “Pops.”
Judges say that his voice has less sonorousness than of
yore and that he strains more at his top-notes. Santley
must nave made a fortune, and, as he married one with
his second wife, why doesn’t he sit back and give “ the
boys ” a show ?
According to a Calcutta paper Mrs. Potter is a big draw
in “ Camille.” She gets all the jam, and Bellew, who
doubles as Duval pere et Ms, is let clow n with a few lines.
Clement Scott’s second reply to Sydney Grundy, which
will appear in The Theatre, will be headed “ A Bull in
the China Shop.”
Sarah Bernhardt has sailed from Paris with forty-five
large trunks containing 100 costumes valued at £12,000, and
with 30 performers. Capoul and Gamier, however, do not
come with her. She takes no risks, but receives a lump
sum for her two years’ tour in the States, Australia (so ’tis
said), Mexico, Cuba. Then she returns to Europe, and,
without performing, embarks at Liverpool for South
America.
The Sydnay Art Society’s last smoke-concert was very
successful. Messrs. Donovan, Young, Bowler and Craven
sang. Herman Newton violined, and Signor Lardelli
accompanied. Messrs. Spence, Soutar and Mahony did
some lightning sketching.
Pettitt, the dramatist, whose share in “Master and
Man,” judging from style, is not very meritorious,
recently met an old schoolfellow. “By the way,” said
his old friend, “are you any relation to Henry Pettitt,
the dramatist?” “I am he,” replied Pettitt. “Ah, but
I mean the great Henry Pettitt, who writes plays for
Drury Lane.” “Yes,” said Henry, modestly, “that’s
me.” “ Well,” said his friend, with disgust, “ you always
were a liar, Pettitt.” Moral: If you would be a successful
playwright begin intelligent lying early in life.
In Jerome K. Jerome’s “ Stageland,” you miss the
Dresser. Sometimes he button-holes us, or she does, with
magical souvenirs of Brooke, Montgomery, Ellen Mortyn,
Fanny Cathcart, Jefferson ; but the Dresser always has
some hero or heroine that the world never knew, some-
body who could knock your Edmund Kean or Mrs.
Siddons into a cocked hat. We found one whose idol was
the Macbeth of Charley Pope, an actor from America, in
Australia some years ago.
Another commentary on the fact that real life imitates
fiction, rather than fiction real life, is afforded by the
strange lucky hit made by H. A. Jones, in his new play
“ The Dancing Girl,” where the Duke of Guisebury (not
Qoosebury) speculates on what the world would say if he
committed suicide, and almost while the play was being
acted, the Duke of Bedford went and did it, as a splendid
advertisement for Beerbohm-Tree and the Haymarket.
Talking of the runs of pieces, one may glance at the
runs of actors in particular characters. Jefferson leads
with Rip, about 4000 times, and Sothern acted Dundreary,
say, nearly 4000 times, matinees being taken into these
calculations, while Emmett’s “ Fritz ” will foot up to
3500. The other longest lists of performances have in-
cluded Matilda Heron in “Camille,” Toole in “ Ici on
Parle Francais,” Neville in the “ Ticket-of-Leave Man,”
Hawtrey in the “Private Secretary,” Wilson Barrett in
“Claudian,” D. James (Belasco)in “Our Boys,” Raymond
as Col. Mulberry Sellers, Florence in the “Mighty Dollar,”
Minnie Palmer in “My Sweetheart,” Jennie Lee in “ Jo,”
and Clarence Holt in “New Babylon.”
Daughter of music and mirth and song,
With eyes like the light of the morning star,
Whose teeth are like pearls the rooks among
Down where the shrimps and the damp whales are,
Whose hair has borrowed the richest hue
Of the gems in some fathomless cavern sunk ;
The thing that we most do adore in you
Is the way your arms are fixed to your trunk.
Once, in the years that are buried now,
We worshipped for one brief summer day
At the shrine of a maid as fair as thou ;
Then the vision passed, like the mists, away,
And we said, “ This world is an empty show,”
And we swamped our griefs in a tearing drunk,
For, alas ! her arms, as we got to know,
Didn’t join on gracefully to her trunk.
Angel or seraph on airy wings,
This world is not what it seems to be ;
The earth is hollow', and earthly things
Are apt to burst up most dismally,
Yet truth survives; when popes are dust,
And king and cardinal, czar and monk
Are forgotten, they’ll print on a marble bust—
“ Her arms were neatly affixed to her trunk.”
Five loaves and two fishes fed five thousand men
(At least so the Biblicans tell us),
But this little miracle now is eclipsed,
For Irving’s must make them all jealous.
To the poor of East London he gave a rich feast
(Not a man of them could be a glutton),
He divided amongst some two millions of men
One really magnificent mutton.

The daily papers print an . absurd cable-
gram to the effect that a’ British plunger won
£700,000 at Monte Carlo and broke the bank.
When details come to hand, the amount will
possibly be £70,000. A thousand to one it will
be 700,000 francs, or less than £35,000, which is
only the half of £70,000 and only the twentieth of
£700,000. We don’t think anyone, ever won even
£lOO,OOO at Monte Carlo; when anybody .wins
£lO,OOO the event is thought wonderful enough to
paragraph in half the European papers.

Descriptive Circular Free on Application
Tales of Old Sydney.
No. II.— Marie Antoinette’s Fandango.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When we described the formation by the
most distinguished Emancipists of Sydney of the
“Pure Merinoes’ Ball Smash-up Fund”* we
simply sketched the outline of the proceedings,
any allusion to which, for many a long day subse-
quently, bit into the feelings of the First Families
as acutely as the pinch of Colonel Foveaux’s
branding-irons, of old time, tortured his men.
That typical First Family man had, indeed, never
succeeded in scarring the bodies of his proteges as
incurably as the Emancipists, otherwise the
Ancients, wounded the Pure Merinoes’ pride
when they introduced into the exclusive, unadul-
terated, supremely tip-toppy company at Chester-
dale House that characterless young lady, Ruth
Fenwick—No. 119 on the manifest of the female
convict-ship, Lord Warden. Time would cicatrise
the physical injury. For the wound to the spirit
there was no healing.
How the wound came to be inflicted you
have been told in outline. This tale fills in the
picture—which consists of a centre-piece and four
panels.
# * #
Panel the first—Mrs. Kent’s dressing-
room. Present —Mrs. Kent, in all the grace of a
negligeable costume, or, rather, no costume, and
Miss Ruth Fenwick, transport, governess by
repute, pickpocket and light o’ love by profession.
The younger lady speaks.
“ And, ma’am, I’ve mixed with the very best
society— ”
“Yes, yes, I know,” said the lady, impatiently
—“ I know all about that, and that you were in
company with a marquis under very improper
circumstances when you were arrested. But the
point is not your past associations, but can you
act at the ball with your then ones—as if you
belonged to their class ?”
“ If I could pass muster,” rejoined the insulted
girl, bridling disdainfully, “in Bath Pump
Room and assemblies as a gernmen’s—l mean
a gentleman’s—lady, I ain’t a bit worser ’n—l
mean I’m equal to—the best of your Nobility ;so
there now!”
“Oh, Fenwick, I’m sure I—l didn’t mean you
weren’t,” said Mrs. Kent, for the first time becom-
ing despondent as to her plan, “ but don’t you see
it’s quite essential that they—that you—shall act
so as not to undeceive ’em till supper-time?”
“ Oh, stow your gab, Missus ! ” and the girl
flung, not ungracefully, her tall, lithe, well-shaped
figure on Mrs. Kent’s pet couch, “ I’ll come the
fine lady-lay all right when it’s the game. ”
“ But —but ” Airs. Kent was terrified. She
had had Ruth Fenwick in charge for a week as
her specially-assigned maid, and never yet had the
girl so revealed the base habits of speech and
thought which had been overlain by the veneer of
refinement picked up in the course of her dubious
relationships to “ fine gentlemen.”
“ But, but what ? ” mimicked the transport.
“D’ye mean my lingo? Are you afraid the
coves I dance with ’ll drop to me too soon. Speak
it out, old lady.”
Now, Mrs. Kent was not old. She was still
fair, was not fat, and was just forty. Therefore
to be termed “ old lady,” and by her own assigned
servant, was too much, and at the risk of upset-
ting the plan by which Messieurs the Mancipists
proposed to smash up Airs. Colonel Chester’s en-
tertainment, she resolved to assert her position.
“ You impudent thing, you ! How dare you
insult your mistress ! Don’t you know I can send
you to the Factory, and have all that hair of
yours off for your insolence ? ”
The transport rose from the couch and moved
towards the enraged lady with a menacing gesture
which frightened Mrs. Kent, but not nearly so
much as her words.
“ Oh, you’re a pretty one, ain’t you, to go on
this way? Now, look here, Mrs. Kent, I ain’t
been a first-rate moocher in London for nothin’,
and I ain’t been in your house a week for nothin’,
neither. How ’bout all those letters from young
Stephens to you in your dressin’-oase, eh? ”
“ What! ” shrieked the chatelaine of the Kent
household.
“ Or, rather, what ’bout those letters which
ain’t in your dressin’-case ? ”
“Oh, what —do —you—mean?” This with a
gasp, and a very obvious attempt at hysteria.
“What do I mean? I mean that you’re not
an old lady at all. You’re simply an old rip
a-carryin’ on with the son of your old man’s
partner, and you the mother of a grown-up girl.
An’ I mean I’ve got those letters. An’ I’m goin’
to make you pay to get ’em back. An’ if you
don’t take care I’ll bust you up instead of the
Nobility’s ball.”
“Oh, Fenwick! Oh, Ruth! how did .”
And Mrs. Kent here genuinely broke down.
Considered as a woman and not as a professional
female-transport, Ruth Fenwick was not all bad.
She’d got the whip-hand of Airs. Kent and she
knew it; but she was not wishful to ply the crop
unnecessarily. In vyhioh characteristic Aliss Fen-
wick, alias Mrs. This, That and the Other, simply
proved that she had not been brought up in the
first circles of Sydney society.
“ Here, dry up, and I’ll give you back your
letters. All except one—l’ll keep one, then I
know you’ll have to stick to me after the ball
affair.”
“ Oh—how did you get them ?”
“Lud, after Dressin’-case Bess was lagged—by
the way, ma’am, you’ve never run against her
here, have you, she was sent to the Bay ?—well,
after she was lagged, I was known as the best
hand at dressin’-cases an’ travellin’-bags in the
trade. I never saw but two cases I couldn’t open,
an’ yours weren’t one of ’em. But goin’ back to
what we started from, I’ll play brag at the ball
till I get some fizz into me.”
“ Fizz ! Do you mean champagne ?”
“Yes, o’ course. I won’t answer for what I say
or do when I take that wine.”
“But you won’t ask for any till supper, surely?
And after supper—after you’ve unmasked—”
“You don’t care what happens ? Oh, I under-
stand, missus. I’m not a fool by any means. But,
don’t forget—old lady! (Miss Fenwick would have
been more than human had she forgone that shot)
—that I’m goin’ to keep one of young Stephens’
letters. ”
Certainly Ruth Fenwick, No. 119 on ship’s list
per Lord Warden, was anything but a fool. Only
a fortnight in Sydney, she had already become
seized of what Chief Justice Francis Forbes (in a
letter which we will give to the world some day)
said was the central fact of Old Sydney life.
“Sydney society was born in corruption, and moved
on wheels of bribery, blackmail, and hush-money.’’
* * *
Panel the Second.—Madame De Voleure’s
fitting-on room in George-street. Aladame had
quite the best business in the .colony. Lady
Darling herself patronised her, and the wonderful
Queen Elizabeth dress in which the stately hostess
of Chesterdale would receive her guests on the
night of the ball had already been sent home, after
eliciting volumes of admiration from all who had
had the privilege of seeing it. “Do the thing in
style,” had said ’Mancipist Paul Kent, and there-
fore Mrs. Kent resolved to solicit Madame’s aid.
But she visited Madame (with Ruth Fenwick in
attendance) in the stillness and shadow of the
evening.
“I've been a good customer to you, Madame?”
“Oh, vary, Madame! A coostomar, good
always.” Madame (originally from Kent) had
an idea that the pure Parisian accent lay in
broadening of the vowels, and the idiom in bring-
ing her qualifying terms to the ends of her sen-
tences.
“ Then you must oblige me in a little thing. You
must get a ball costume ready for this —young
lady (she pointed to the veiled figure of Ruth) on
the quiet. It’s to be an expensive one, and over
and above your bill the sum of ten pounds will be
paid you, Madame, to —to .”
“Vat say you, Anglash ladies? Your tongue
to hold ?”
“Yes, Madame, that’s what I meant to say.
No one must know who the dress has been ordered
by, or for whom it is intended.”
“ Certainement, Madame. Mam’selle, then .”
Madame De Voleure stopped, aghast for a
moment. For Miss Fenwick had lifted her veil,
and was most inexplicably (as Mrs. Kent would
have thought) winking. Madame was recalled to
herself by a slight cough from Miss Fenwick.
“Mam’selle then is to ze ball goin’ on—vat
say you?—on ze sly ?”
THE OBVIOUS ALTERNATIVE.
MRS. FITZBOOSE : “Thank you very much, but I never take tea.”
AMIABLE HOSTESS ( sweetly ): “Dear me! I am so sorry I haven’t got any whisky.”
“That is it,” said Mrs. Kent, both relieved and
distressed to find that the acute-witted costumUre
had so quickly read her game. “ What character
would you suggest ?”
Madame De Voleure meditated for a full second
with finger-tip resting on her artistically-rouged
cheek, and then replied :
“ Oh, Mam’selle must to ze ball go as thatmees-
fertoonate queen vary, Marie Antoinette. Mam’-
selle is too long—vat you say too tall, a leetle;
but, zen, Mam’selle would ze queen ra-present
parfectly. She has ze beauty, an’ she has ze grace,
an’ she has ze—ze vartue,”
“ Mam’selle ” looked to the wall. Mrs. Kent
thought she had turned her head to blush unob-
served. Not she ; she had glanced aside to laugh.
“ Oh, Marie Antoinette ’ll do nicely. Don’t
you think so, Fen —dear ?”
“ Mam’selle ” couldn’t trust herself to reply so
near was she to choking with laughter, so she
nodded assent.
“ Zen, Madame, if so good you vill be as to
take some, vat you say rest one leetle, mam’-
selle I vill make some mazures from in zis room
’ere. An’, p’r’ps, Madame, you vill like zis book
to oversee. ” As she spoke the costumiere opened the
door of an inner room, into which she bowed
Miss Ruth Fenwick, and followed the action by
handing to her patroness a volume of coloured
prints of fancy-dress costumes. Mrs. Kent sank
into the voluptuous velvet of a couch to glance
over the plates, sending an inquisitive look now
and again into the apartment where the De
Voleure plied inch-tapes and pins industriously
in the registration of “ Mam’selle’s ” finely-
proportioned contour. Almost as industriously
she plied her tongue. But Mrs. Kent heard only
villainous English and professional French. She
did not know that the dressmaker was displaying,
in whispered interpolations, evidence that in her
exile she had lost none of her old-time proficiency
in thieves’ argot.
“ Mam’selle vill ’ave a costoome of Versailles,
or of ze ex-eoooshun, vot you say period! Oh,
Polly, who’d ’a’ thought of seein’you ’ere?” (This
last in a whisper).
“ And, Bess, when I saw you, old pal, from
’neath my flimsy, you could have knocked me
down—Oh, Versailles, of course—with a feather-
no executions for me, Betsy, laggin’s ’nuff,”
“ Zen we vill ’ave one robe broche, I vud say
vit trimmed bod ce an’ toonic in front—what yer
lagged for, Poll! —an’ vith flounces of broche an’
lace of gold ? ”
“ Ten years for nabbing my omee’sf yellow-
boys—as you please, Madame ! —but, Lud, to
think o’ Dressin’-case Bess a-doin’ the duds-lay.
Where’s your ruffler, Bess? Warn’t he lagged
same time as you ? ”
“ Ve vill ’ave ze bodee ’igh in ze back —yes, but
he got stretched for paddin’ the toby, they call it
bushrangin’ ’ere—an’ in ze front—’ow vill Madame
like Mam’selle’s bodee —Mam’selle’s cha-arms best
vill be deesplayed by one bodee ske-vare ? —What’s
yer name, Poll ? ”
Mrs. Kent, appealed to, answered that she
would leave the point of cut to Madame
De Voleure’s well-known taste. “Mam’selle”
acknowledged the compliment to her charms, and
the query by a dig in the dressmaker’s ribs, and :
“Bess, you donny, you were alius glib in the
gab. But ‘ stubble your whids ’J or you’ll put me
away. I ain’t Mrs. Flipperton now; I’m Ruth
Fenwick.”
“ ’Sh! Zen ve vill ’ave ze bod eese ske-vare.
An’ vot jooils vill Madame ’ave ze desire zat
Ma’m’selle shall vear ?”
“What would the De Voleure suggest?” was
asked from the outer room. Madame of Kent
was indifferent; her iewel-case was to be at
her young friend’s disposal. Ma’m’selle, on the
other hand, was of the opinion that pearls would
suit her best, all things considered.
“They would go best with robe and coiffure,
would they not, Mrs. Kent ?” she said to the
“old lady.” To the De Voleure, alias' “ Dressing-
case Bess,” she remarked, sotto voce (with another
dig into Madarae’s side): “Pearls is most appro-
priate to the virtuous, Bessy, ain’t they ? Where
d’ye get your lingo, Bess?”
“Shut up! I’m French. Mats, Madame
Kent —Mam’selle is as a queen goin’ to ze ball, an’
di-monds is for ze Majesty, ze—ze best, vary !
Poll, you’member Sparkler Ned? He’s ’ere.”§
“Certainly, Madame De Voleure, I quite agree
with you. I can afford the young lady the use
of some brilliants. ”
“And Sparkler Ned here! I nubble to your
game, Bess. If I get the stones he’ll foist the
dummies ?”
“ That’s the lay, Poll. Ah, zat is kind vary —
of Madame Kent. Allans, Mam’selle, I vill not
you trouble more at ze present. We vill, eef
Madame pleases, at ze vat you say, ma-tee-rial,
now look. ”
Mrs. Kent, so willing, was speedily immersed
in the contemplation of sundry silks and fal-de-
rals, out of which were to be compounded the
costume which was to manifest the De Voleure’s
skill, to display the Fenwick’s charms in con-
cealing them, and to ravish, to the point of burst-
ing at the Chesterdale party, the hearts of Mrs.
Colonel Chester’s masculine guests.
And while the wife of ’Mancipist Kent was sc
engaged, Ruth Fenwick, otherwise Poll Flipperton
and Mrs. This, That and the Other, and her old-
time crony, Madame De Voleure, familiar as
“Dressing-case Bess ” to the London police, found
opportunities to exchange mutual sympathies and
to renew ancient alliances.
“Ze ball is on ze of Mai ze zixteen. Mam’selle
shall call in one week before, to vat you call lit on,
an’ ze costoome vill be ready two free day ze ball
before. I vill mineself bring it to Aladame’s.”
(This to Mrs. Kent.) But unto Mrs. Kent’s
amiable protege, as she held the door open and
bowed them to their carriage, this : “ I’ll bring
Ned’s fiilyll with me, Poll, as ’sistant. You ’ave
the sparklers ready, and she’ll ’ave the wax.”
And, to anticipate shortly, we may here remark
that, while Madame Voleure occupied, on the
occasion of her taking home Marie Antoinette’s
dress, Mrs. Kent’s attention with a deeply-in-
teresting account of the numerous costumes she
had created for the ball —“An’ none of zem,
Madame, vill vot you say eke-val Mam’selle’s ” —•
her assistant was hastily but accurately taking an
impression of Mrs. Kent’s diamonds. Transport
Ruth Fenwick, per Lord Warden, had as facile a
knack at opening jewel-boxes as in the manipula-
tion of dressing-cases,
* # *
Panel the third—the dining-room at Mr.
Harrington’s villaon theeveof theChesterdale ball.
Mr. Harold Harrington, at the foot of the table,
nerving himself for an unpleasant task. His wife
speaks from the head.
“Nell and I will be ready, dear, at nine o’clock.
S’pose we say the carriage at half-past ? ”
Mr. Harrington (of the Treasury) looks down
on his fruit-plate and toys with his nut-crackers,
but gives no sign that he has heard the voice of the
wife of his bosom.
“Harold, did you not hear me? I wish the
carriage to be ordered by half-past nine.”
“Eh, what, dear ? Oh, yes, the carriage. But,
look here, I ”
“You what? You’ve been having too much
wine, Mr. Harrington.”
“Well, this, dear, and you, Nelly. I—l- .”
“For heaven’s sake, Mr. H., what is it? Aren’t
we going to the ball ?”
Mr. Harrington jerked out his reply in an
agitated fashion.
“ Well—no —not—all of us !”
“ What!” shrieked his womankind in startled
chorus.
“ The fact is—Nell—you can’t go !”
“ What! Harold, you’re drunk !”
“Pa! ma ! Oh! —oh!” (Preliminaries to
hysterics.)
“ It’s not a bit of use going on in this way!”
In his desperation Mr. Harrington found his
words ran away with him, “ Damn the Chester
ball say I, damn the old minx for insulting the
’Mancipists, and damn—damn— you!”
“What !”
“Yes, you! It’s all your own fault—if a
woman will gratify her mean spite she must
suffer.”
“ Will yon, if you haven’t quite lost your senses,
be good enough to explain, sir.” (Never' before
had Mrs. Harrington felt the necessity to play
with severity the combined role of the Injured
Mother and the Outraged Wife.)
“ Didn’t Mrs. Chester issue her card for to-night
with a letter that she wouldn’t receive members
of the ’Mancipist families ? Didn’t Nell there, who
is shamming to faint (a moan from Nell !) go and
make the insult personal by showing her card to
the Kent women ? Answer me that, my ladies !”
“ Well, and what then? That’s no reason why
you should go mad ! ”
“Isn’t it? Well, if you must know all—l’m
under Kent’s thumb. He can ruin me to-morrow.”
. “Horrible!”
“Pa!”
“ Oh, you’re better, are you, Miss ? I’m glad of
it—glad to know, too, that you know at last that
your silly girl’s trick has induced Kent, in order
to get revenge, to buy up all my bills and to take
over from Quaker Tawell the mortgage on this
property, and that he has threatened me to press
for every penny at once, unless ”
“Oh ! Unless what ? ”
“ I take his daughter to the ball in place of
Nell there.”
Nell’s hysterics came now with a vengeance.
But the father and the mother let her cry. “ It’ll
do her good,” said the former. He was a man,
you see, and, therefore, never being in the habit of
giving way to bursts of unreasonable passion, had
no sympathy with the nervous demonstrations of
his women-folk. “ It’s too horrible !” gasped the
latter, who was less shocked at the imminence of
ruin than at the supremacy in her husband’s affaii-s
of one of the cursed ’Mancipist brood. “ It’s too
horrible !”
“It’s bad, but it might be worse. Kent pro-
mises if I do this, he’ll release me from some of
my bills. ”
“ Why did you give him the bills ? Why don’t
you let him sue? He can’t recover in Court,
being a ’Mancipist,”** sobbed Mrs. Harrington,
who evidently thought it was high time she, too,
broke down.
“ Talk sense, wife, do !” ejaculated the badgered
Harrington. “Kent is a C.P. man, and not a
ticket-of-leave ; And, even if he were, all that
he’s got to do is to assign his interests to a free-on-
arrival or free-by-servitude man and get a ver-
dict.”
“The officers won’t give a verdict against a
Treasury man.”
“Won’t they ? They won’t in most ’Mancipist
cases, but there isn’t a panel now in Sydney who
would dare to give a verdict against Paul Kent,
or Pete Stephens, or Sam T , or three or four
other big ’Mancipists. No; I’ve simply got to
pay up—which I ean’t —or take old Kent’s
daughter.”

(Now you will remember Paul Kent had never
stipulated that Harrington should take his [Kent’s]
daughter to the ball. He had done no more than
insist that the Treasury dignitary should escort
“a lady” to the Chesterdale festival instead o,
his own girl, Nell.)
“ Then, unless Nell is to go—and our dresses all
ready, too !—I shan’t go ! 1 won’t chaperone an
old lag’s chit. And there’ll be a pretty row when
they unmask at supper 1”
“ No, there won’t. Kent has promised to have
his trap outside the gate by 12.30, and I’m to see
the girl to it then.”
“ He’s promised, has he ? Mark my words he
will do nothing of the kind! His trap! Yes—
I don’t doubt it —the whole thing’s a trap, that s
what it is ! ”
“Pooh, pooh !” rejoined Harrington, greatly
relieved that he had got the trouble over (though
there was a sweet savour of sal volatile and burnt
feathers from the adjoining room.) “Hell not
lay a trap to expose ids own daughter to insult.
Don’t you see he’s only anxious to enable his girl
to show Nell that, after all, it was she who
went to the ball— she, the ’Manoipist’s daughter—
and that she went, too, on the card of invitation
sent to the daughter of the big man at the
Treasury. ”
But Mrs. Harrington being a woman, and,
therefore, distinctly unamenable to masculine
reasoning, persisted that she could not see it,
would not see it, should not see it.
“ What apology shall I make for you, my dear,
to Mr. Chester ?” _
“ What you please, Mr. Harrington. Tell her
what you like, because you’re iinposing on her hos-
pitality and insulting herself and her guests”
and boo-hoos —more singed feathers —more sal
volatile completed Mrs. Harrington’s sentence.
At nine-thirty, however, as Harold Harrington,
Esquire (of the Treasury), stepped in the not
altogether inappropriate disguise of a Bandit of
the Apennines from his hall steps into his car-
riage, and at the very instant that he opened his
lips to instruct his coachman to call at the Kent
mansion, Mrs. Harrington spoke her mind once
more from the doorway. _ _
“Mark my words, Mr. Harrington, it’s a trap ! '
Which remark was the text from which many a
domestic sermon was to be preached in the near
future.
* * *
Panel the fourth—Mrs. Kent’s double
drawing-room. In the ante-portion Paul Kent,
Emancipist and “ Ancient,” and Harold Harring-
ton, Esquire, member of the Colonial Aristocracy
and First Family man. In the inner portion
behind the folding-doors (left slightly a-jar) Mrs.
Kent and Mademoiselle Kent —and Ruth Fen-
wick. Not that you would have recognised Miss
Ruth in the fascinating creature who stood just
within the rim of light thrown by the shaded
lamp, and smiled in all her purchased —and pur-
chaseable—graces. It was quite evident that the
Transport, per Lord Warden, who was numbered
119 on ship’s list was going to enjoy herself who-
ever else did not at Mrs. Chester’s masquerade.
In the outer drawing-room Mr. Harrington
speaks. In the inner, three feminine heads bend,
on the first syllable, nearer to the interstice of the
folding-doois.
“ Well, I’m here, Mr. Kent, to carry out my
part of the bargain. Is the young lady ready ? ”
“ The young lady is ready, and I, also, am ready
to perform my share of the agreement. I have
your overdue and protested bills here for £327 Us.
fed. sterling in favour of Mr. Samuel T ——. Will
you glance at them to see if they are in order?
No, sir, they can’t go out of my possession yet.
You can look at them in my hand.”
“ Sir ! ”
“ Now, pray, don’t be indignant, Mr. Harring-
ton ! ” The old man was asthmatic, but be con-
trived to infuse a fine irony into his next wheeze.
“You are a public servant, sir, hence are not to
be trusted with another person’s property.”
Harrington flamed to rose-red passion, but ho
knew that he had met his match.
“ These bills are my property till——.”
“The girl’s in my carriage. That was your
statement.”
“Exactly; you have a capital memory for
spoken words—better than your written ones.”
And the ’Mancipist flaunted the dishonoured
notes under the other’s very nose with one hand,
while with the other he rang a bell.
“James,” he said to the footman (a burglar
with violence) who appeared in answer to the
tingle, “ announce to the ladies that Mr. Harring-
ton’s carriage is waiting. And, James, see the
young lady to the carriage—Mr. Harrington will
be down presently.”
Three minutes later Mr. Harrington was down.
He had got his bills the moment the gentleman of
burglarious and violent tendencies had reported
that the young lady was “ waiting Mr. Harring-
ton’s pleasure ” —he had responded to Mr. Kent’s
enquiries as to “whether they were all right by
a sullen “Yes !” he had first torn the notes into
a hundred pieces and then ignited the fragments
by the flame of a waxen candle —and he had
marched out—furious and revengeful. He made
no salutation to the ’Mancipist, nor did he deign
to indicate that he heard Mr. Kent’s words; “I
shall be at Chesterdale gate at 12.30, Mr. Harring-
ton, with my carryall.” He was too busy to do
either one thing or the other, inasmuch as his
whole powers were devoted to framing the most
elaborate maledictions on the ’Mancipist tribe. _
It was to be somewhat regretted in his own in-
terests that he was so pro-occupied. Otherwise it
is possible that he might have overheard Mr.
Kent’s remarks to Mrs. and Miss Kent as those
ladies emerged into the front drawing-room,
“ Don’t forget, my dears, that you saw and
heard he accepted those bills as tlie ones he had
made.”
“Why, Paul ? ” questioned the matron.
“ Because, Jane, those were not the bills he
made. Those are of old Pounce’s manufacture.
“ Forgeries, Paul ! ”
“ lorgenes, Jane ! I have the originals still,
They may come in useful, yet. And the old
rogue chuckled in a way that would have been
merry if he had not been quite so asthmatic,
Old Pounce (twice oapitally-condemned former)
one of the chiefest institutions or Ulcl
Sydney. It is difficult to see how Old Sydney life
could have been what it was but for Old Pounce,
Everybody used him from high Government
officials to new y-arnyed transports who, not due
fora ticket-of-leave for three or four years, yet
were anxious to possess one a couple of months or
so after landing. Particularly useful party, Old
Pounce ! • „

The centre-piece—Mrs. Colonel Chesters
supper-table. The “brilliant galaxy of guests”—
the words are Mr. Robert Howe’s, of the Sydney
Gazette —“filed to their places to add the
beauties of feminine grace and masculine vigour to
the magnificently-spread and elegantly-adorned
tables.” At the cross-table—where were grouped
the seats of honour —the unmasked hostess, an
Imperial Elizabeth in black velvet, filled the
centre-chair. On her right, unmasked also, in his
General’s uniform, Governor Sir Ralph Darling;
0 n her left, robed as a Judge of Assize, Chief
Justice Forbes, who, notwithstanding his well-
known fondness “ for doing justice to the ’Manci-
pists,” was too powerful to be socially slighted by
the First Families. Three chairs away from Lady
Darling (“ Marie Theresa ”), who sat next to the
noble Chief Justice, was a Marie Antoinette
assiduously waited upon by an officer whose
domino did not hide the uniform of the 57th
Regiment. It was “a table of Sovereign beau-
ties,” as Mr. Robert Howe wrote.?! But, of
course, the beauties were only partially discernible

as yet: the hour had not yet come for general
unmasking. With the first toast, the guests were
to unmask.
Twenty chairs distant from the cross-table a
Bandit of the Apennines, who had, in his agita-
tion, left his silk face-piece in Mr. Kent’s drawing-
room, was doing his best to drown his fears by
looking upon some red Portugese wine.
Brilliant among the beautiful, Marie Antoinette
had enjoyed herself to the utmost point of
rapture. Harrington, paying his devoirs to
Queen Elizabeth on his arrival in the ball-room,
and being without his mask, had, of course, been
recognised. And her Majesty of England had
been gracious enough to touch with her lips her
sweet sister of France. “How your daughter is
growing, Mr. Harrington. I’ll forgive you, you
naughty man you, for coming without your mask,
because you have brought so magnificent a
costume. But where is Mrs. Harrington ? Now
that I know you and your daughter, may not I
know what character Mrs. Harrington has as-
sumed?”
“ Am deeply—ah—sorry, Mrs. Chester, but my
wife was suddenly—l mean, she has an exces-
sively bad headache to-night— ”
“ Oh, so grieved ! But, nevertheless, lam sure
the lady brought by Mr. Harrington will carry
off the major honours to-night. To have had a
second member of the family would have been for
you to monopolise all.”
This gratifying reception was merely the pre-
lude to the triumphs of the evening.
First of all, Marie Antoinette, with Lieutenant
Charles Darling (who by-and-bye was to dance
another sort of figure in Victorian history),
formed bottom couple in the first set of the Stale
Quadrilles. In Le Pantalon, her partner thought
her pretty; in L’Ete, beautiful; in La Poulc ,
fascinating; in La Trenisc, entrancing; and in
the grand rond of La Finale, a goddess ! Mane
Antoinette had not mixed with the best West End
society for nothing. Her conduct and her speech
were unexceptionable. As yet she had not tasted
a mouthful of champagne.
Young 'Chester himself, in whose and whose
bride’s honour the ball was given, danced the first
waltz with her. And with the finesse, so character-
istic of a First Family scion, he thereafter assured
his x>retty, lifeless young English wife, “ that she
was a stick at dancing compared with that
Harrington girl,” For, tire news that the Mane
was Nell Harrington had spread throughout the
great ballroom, through the paradise of the conser-
vatories and out in the glades of the shrubberies.
People wondered where Harrington’s daughter
had got her esprit and grace from all at once.
For every dance she found a difficulty in select-
ing her partners, she was besieged so numerously.
But it was Lieutenant Charles she favored most
frequently, and to whom, in a coy whisper, she
granted permission to take her into supper, and
whose aid she invoked for the after-supper-minuet.
Which was never danced.
* * *
The little rift, which by-and-bye was to
make mute the music of the Band of the 39th
Regiment which acted as Mrs. Chester’s orchestra,
was visible after Maria Antoinette’s first glass of
champagne. Lieutenant Charles poured it out
for her. She modestly demurred—“ vowing she
would ne’er consent, consented ” —sipped—drank.
The effect was electric.
“Never been at Bath Park-room, sir? And
this is almost as ‘rorty’ as that. Who’d ’a thought
this was Botany Bay !”
“ W-h-at —your Majesty? I—l’m afraid I did
not quite catch what you said. ’
“ Oh. never mind ! twice boiled cabbitch an. •
no good. Here, tip ns some more fizz, cully.”
Blindly, not believing his own ears, Lieutenant
Charles refilled her glass. She absorbed the con-
tents in a most unqueenly fashion. As she put
down her glass she caught a remark, dropped
by a venerable gentleman in the costume of the
Ancien Regime.
“As I was saying, we treat the assigned
pris’ners too liberally. Look at their clothes, for
instance. It’s an extravagant allowance foi
assigned men. For assigned women it’s simply
waste.”
“ Why, sir?” Marie Antoinette raised her voter
so that the old gentleman (one Mr. John Mac
arthur) should hear her. “What do the wonm;
get ?” _
Capt’n John rattled the list off glibly. You
couldn’t beat Capt’n John when it was a matter
of reciting regulations unless you put up his sou
James against him.
“Annual dress allowance to convict women—
one cotton gown, two bed-jackets, three shifts,
two flannel petticoats, two stuff petticoats, two
pair shoes, two calico caps, three pairs stockings,
two'neck ’kerchiefs, one check .”
“Lud, don’t I get no more things than them ?”
# * #
Then happened the beginning of the end.
Her voice raised excitedly, Marie Antoinette’s
words carved out a broad area of shocked silence.
Just as she spoke, however-, the toast-master from
behind Sir Ralph Darling’s chair called out —
“Ladies and gentlemen will please unmask while
I give, ladies and gentlemen all, ‘The King’;
God bless him.”
There was a rustle as the masks came off. At
the lower end of the great room—you can still see
its gables from the Liverpool Road—the tinkle of
laughter, and the moving of chairs back, as guests
rose to welcome the toast; but in the neighbour-
hood of the table of dignity—silence.
Which was broken by a woman’s voice exclaiming
in decidedly hiccupish accents —
“The King! I’ll s-show you—ladies an’ gents
—’ovv me an’ the King—God blesh ’im ! —danced
the fandango at Brighton.”
Before her cavalier could stop her she had
rested a hand on his shoulder, and with that
support mounted to the table. Balancing herself
for a moment while she picked up a couple of
spoons, she began the slow movement of the
fandango. With an aptness that told that the
performance was not the first under like condi-
tions, she played the spoons in castanet fashion,
and i-apidly, before the astounded crowd, deve-
loped the voluptuous rapidity of the dance, her
diamonds glittering gloriously in the light of two
hundred candles.
“ I want a partner; get up, Cully !” she shouted
out, once.
But Lieutenant Charles, although he heard her
voice distinctly over the crash of silver and glass
which she cleared from the table in the sweep of
her silk-shod feet, declined the invitation.
Three minutes she might have danced before the
appalled though fascinated guests, when the whim
seized her to unmask. She flung the piece of
stiffened silk to the floor.
Mr. Seragge, Barrack-master (he was attired as
a Greek pirate), saw her face then, identified her,
and shouted so that above the ring of the castanets
his exclamation was heard by all:
“By Gad! It’s Ruth Fenwick, convict per
Lord Warden,”
Ruth Fenwick, otherwise Mrs. Polly Flipperton,
otherwise Marie Antoinette, stopped exhausted
in her wild evolutions and heard him.
“ Lud, pal, ’ave you only just found that out! ”
was her retort. And another voice confirmed the
news.
“ I have to apologise for this intrusion, Mrs,
Chester, and I was hopeful to arrive before any
scene happened. Unfortunately I see I’m too
late. Constable, that’s the woman—my assigned
servant, Ruth Fenwick.”
Mr. Paul Kent, J.P. (with a constable in hia
company) was the speaker. As the constable
advanced to arrest Marie Antoinette —he had an
awful work getting her to Charlotte Place watch-
house—Mr. Kent made a further explanation to
Colonel Chester.
“ I heard, sir, that the woman had been induced
to leave my house to-night by some officer of the
Civil Establishment, and making further enquiries
found she had been driven here. lat once feared
she had been brought to make a scene. I can’t
say how sorry I am this has happened.”
As we happen to know that the Pure
Merinoes’ Ball Smash-up Fund had been originated
by Mr. Kent, and that he had himself subscribed
100 guineas to it, we take the liberty of doubting
Mr. Kent’s veracity.
PRICE WARUNG.
If Wrote, but did not publish. The Press Censor struck
the phrase out.
Saved from Misery.
Mrs. Fauny Dent, wife of a gentleman employed
in the Lambton colleries, kindly allows ns to
publish the following facts of her interesting case
and wonderful recovery, which we gather from
her correspondence, and which we shall be glad
to allow anyone to peruse who wishes to do so.
Mrs. Dent has been in a state of extreme debility
for twelve years, frequently quite unable to do
any work, the lightest kind causing great fatigue,
with severe pains in the limbs, back, and left side.
Ho severe was the pain that she would have to sit
or lie down frequently. Walking exercise was
very trying and she could only walk very slowly.
She had long seen Clements Tonic advertised, but
like many more people, her husband was prejudiced
against “ patent medicines,” but (now' we use her
own words) “I knew some one who had tried
Clements Tonic, and I decided to get a bottle on
the quiet and say nothing about it if it did me no
good. The effect I thought something wonderful.
I felt better after the first dose, and by the time I
had taken half a bottle I was a different woman.
My husband, friends and neighbours soon noticed
the change in me, and they could not make it out.
I had to tell the truth that it was Clements Tonic
that was making me strong. I could not keep the
secret, I was only too glad to give the credit to
Clements Tonic. After finishing the bottle, Avork
was no trouble to me, but rather a pleasure, and
after doing a heavy day’s washing I could go for a
walk instead of having to go to bed, as always
happened before taking Clements Tonic. , One of
my neighbours said to me last week —Why, Mrs.
Dent, you are dou% wonders this week, how is it
you are so strong now?” She knew I had done
more that week than I had been able to do in 12
years before (she had known me this time, for I
am an old resident of this locality, having lived
here for 15 years.) I replied : ‘Yes, I feel a
wonder to myself, so I’ll tell you the secret; I
have been taking Clements Tonic, and I cannot
tell you the benefit I have derived from it. You
know what my sufferings have been, and now I
have a genuine taste of good health.’” Mrs.
Dent wrote us those particulars of her remarkable
cure in March. She wisely, however, decided to
continue the medicine to “clinch the cure” as it
were. Some time afterwards she wrote as follows :
“ Waratah Commonage, N.S.W., —Dear sir,—lt
is with great pleasure that I pen these few lines.
1 have been as you know a great sufferer from
weakness and debility for years. I was under
several doctors, but they never did any good. I
despaired of ever being better, but as I stated in
ray last letter I saw Clements Tonic advertised. I
tried a botfle and its effect ivas something wonder-
ful. I felt better almost immediately, and was a
new woman after finishing the first bottle. Before-
taking Clements Tonic it was a great trouble even
to do a little house-Avork, but afterwards my work
was a pleasure, and my friends and neighbours
were surprised at the change in me. I cannot say
too much in praise of it, and can only gratefully
describe my case and strongly advise all Avho
suffer from Aveakness, backache, pain in the side,
ringing noises in the ears, and shortness of breath
to take Clements Tonic. I feel sure it Avill do
them as much good as it has done, me.—Yours
gratefully, Fanny Dent.” Wo have great pleasure'
in thanking this lady for allowing us to publish
tins case. " We constantly hear of similar ones
which people object to have published through a
false delicacy. We only want to publish them in
the interests of suffering humanity. Surely it is
not wrong for people to acknowledge the genuine-
ness and reliability of an article, Avhen they have
proved it to possess the properties Ave claim for it.

Society
lb is to be hoped that Australia will soon
see the very last of the vice-regal postilion non-
sense. This is a country where even the horses
must have democratic privileges. Any Governor
who has his horses ridden while they are also
being driven should be prosecuted by the Society
for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals. Any-
how, it is cruel to make a noble rnoke figure in a
show which in a really sporting country only
excites the disgust and derision of the populace.
# # *
At the Federation dinner, Mrs. Mayoress
Burdekin was sandwiched between Lady Jersey
and Lady Parkes. Real strategy would have been
shown by sandwiching Lady P. between Mrs. B.
and Lady J.
# * *
Gov. J ersey is said to be the most obliging
man south of the line. When he has to speak at
a gathering at which Premier Parkes also orates,
he makes a point of dropping his finals, and says
“nothin’,” “havin’,” “doin’,” &c., in the most
courteous manner. He has not yet, however,
achieved the Parkesian orthographical vanity of
substituting a “k” fora “g” in such words as
“ nothink,” but he has ’opes of accomplishing even
that feat shortly.
# # *
On the second day of the Convention there
appeared on the flagstaffmver N.S. W. Parliament
House the Union Jack, and it has waved there
evei’y day since. This flag does not flutter in the
breeze when the N.S.W. Parliament is in session
—except on opening day—then why should it be
hoisted over the Convention ? Why, indeed,
except to demonstrate that Parkes is grovelling at
the feet of Royalty. “No lip-service loyalty
about me,” said Parkes at the Federation Confer-
ence in Melbourne. We wish we could bring it
home to the deluded old man’s imagination with a
dull cold bash that there are a large number of
reasons why he will not get the hereditary mark of
Royal favour for his Federation “hefforts.” If
he could once thoroughly comprehend his position
with regard to the Peerage he might easily be
induced to dye his hair vermillion —like the tail of
the Cyclorama’s advertising horse —and howl for
the Republic.
# # #
After all, it would seem that Lady Jersey
does not always wear the—er, governing garb.
She attended the Federal Convention the other
day with her guide and beacon, and at the con-
clusion of the sitting jumped up with something
like pleasurable excitement to shake hands with
some of the delegates. Jersey, however, just
suppressed her into her seat with a gesture, which
said as plainly as words, “My dear, you must
contain yourself. When you are to have your
little innings I’ll let you know. I’m running this
show.” She went down without a word.
* # *
Vice-regality seems to be desperately fond
of the gentlemen who can supply it with “the
straight griffin.” Carington used to worship the
late James W'dte, espec’ally when his horses were
winning; Kintore’s affection for the owner of
Carbine is pathetic in its tenderness, and now
Hopetoun may be seen clinging to one of the
Millers, if the stable happens (as it usually does)
to have a “ good thing ” going “over the sticks.”
# * #
Yarrangobilly, N.S.W., has been re-
christened “Jersey.” How proud of their native
country these Australians are !
* # #
The Federal Convention has gathered
together a lot of fossils in Sydney, including a
hash-house keeper from Tasmania who landed at
a big coffee-palace with his boxes blazoned all over
thusly—“ Hezekiah Mutton, Esq., Mayor of
Goose Green Gully.” The Mayor went out walk-
ing one Sunday morning intending to paralyse the
town, and when he returned his door was placarded
with letters a foot long setting forth that the
“ Mayor of Goose-Green Gully,” slept within.
He never wilted, simply took it as one of the
attentions paid to distinguished visitors, and so
“the boys” determined to go one better. They
did, for they promptly sent him an invitation,
purporting to come from the Mayor of Bondi,
who requested the honour of the visiting digni-
tary’s presence to dinner at his (Bondi’s) town-
residence, House. Goose-Green Gully bit,
and marched round to House arrayed in his
most frayed and best suit. After standing on
the doorstep for some time, getting his collar and
eyebrows straight, he knocked timidly, and on
asking for the Mayor of Bondi, was rudely re-
pulsed by the showy young female who opened
the door as an idiotic old hayseed, who ought to
know better.
* # *
The other day there stepped into a Sydney
tramcar a most elegantly-dressed and fetching
woman. In the same compartment there hap-
pened to be a member of Parliament whose eye
lingered long and lovingly on the enchantress.
Presently there came along the ticket-collector,
apparently a new man, for he knew not the mem-
ber, and so demanded a ticket instead of allowing
the potentate to pass unchallenged. When the
conductor had departed the eminent statesman
proceeded to address the lady: “No doubt,
Madam er you—er —haw —may be—er —ah —
somewhat surprised at my not having paid my—er
—fare, but the—aw—fact is that (here holding out
his pass) my—er —pass as a —ah-Statesman,
renders paying superfluous !” “ Indeed !” frigidly
returned the fair creature, resuming her book.
This is the great man who on one occasion
got hold of a newspaper-reporter at Parliament
House and asked the latter his opinion as to the
most dignified way in which to write his (the
statesman’s) name—the way in which it would
look best in print. “Which would you prefer,
were you I, dear boy?” he enquired, proceeding
to write on the blotting-pad :
Mr. R. Bagley Jumpup, M.P.
R. Bagley Jumpup, Esq., M.P.
Mr. Richard Bagley Jumpup, M.L.A.
Richard B. Jumpup, Esq., M.L.A.
Mr. R. B. Jumpup, M.P.
and so on for half-a-column. The reporter said
he didn’t quite know, but he was sure the news-
paper-men would gladly print the great states-
man’s name in any form the great statesman
selected. Mr. R. Bagley Jumpup, M.P., finally
fixed upon a “style and title ” and commended the
same to the attention of the press, but the scribes
laugh at him and make a point of alluding to him
A “MAN OF MARK”—OR, THE MODERN AUSTRALIAN BOOK-AGENT.
shortly as “ Mr. Jumpup.” That is the one great
sorrow of the great statesman’s life.
# * *
Geo. Sutherland, D.D., through the
medium of the Sydney press, requested that the
Federal Convention should be opened with prayer.
It was opened with a corkscrew, and probably
will be closed with nux vomica and spirits of
ammonia.
* * #
At Springwood, Blue Mountains, on
Monday week, a very shabbily-dressed person,
wet to the skin, got into the train, seated himself
in a corner of the carriage, and as soon as the
train moved off, stood up and began to undress.
As he removed each wet article, he threw it out
of the window. At last he stood there in a state
of nudity; then, taking a towel from the bag he
was carrying, he rubbed himself down. After
this he dressed himself in clean, dry clothes.
From the time he entered the carriage until he
was rehabited he never spoke a word, but
afterwards publicly explained that he had had to
walk a mile to the train in the wet, and that his
umbrella and mackintosh being in town he had
brought a change. That is the kind of man who
will get on in this world.

There are at least three divorced men
sitting in the New South Wales Legislative
Assembly.
# * *
Justice Webb ("Melb.) says the woman’s
oath is enough for affiliation. We are sorry to
hear this, for judges should be men of the world
and Judge Webb evidently knows nothing about
women. We must leave the question to be settled
between the partisans of the credulous Victorian
Judge and the incredulous Australian Chief
Justice who says privately (when he has taken six
large whiskies) that ‘' all men are liars and all
women are d liars.” Women, we must say,
have a good time in the Australian law-courts, for
there no man ever gets full justice against a
woman.
# * *
When Primrose Day comes round, Lady
Jersey will realise that she is indeed in a foreign
country. No statue of Beaconsfield have we, nor,
had we one, is there on the whole continent a single
silver-mounted ladder up wlrch her Ladyship
might climb as in days of yore to ornament with
flowers Dizzy’s effigy.
# # *•
The English Freemasons, headed by the
Prince of Wales, have formally excommunicated
Sir Robert Stout of Maoriland from their ranks
on the ground that he belongs to the Grand Orient
Lodge of France, a purely democratic institution
which exacts no religious tests. English Free-
masonry, of course, is entirely devoted to the House
of Hanover and is among the chief means by which
that glorious dynasty is shored up. Stout’s weak-
ness in accepting a knighthood at the hands of the
very Court whose coming head has jumped upon
him with both feet, places him in a most invidious
position at the present juncture. The moral is
obvious.
# * *
The secretary of Melbourne Y.M.C.A. is
endeavouring to form a Commercial Travellers’
Christian Association. This will supply a long-
felt want, and if the secretary is successful in
bringingthe bagmen into the fold, the Y.M.C.A.
will be the best place in Melbourne to hear
amusing stories of the sort you don’t see printed
except in countries where they have suspended the
Obscene Publications Act.
* * *
According to our horse-reporter, the
Ladies Dorothea and Estella Hope, at Flem-
ington last week, wore pale-blue gowns,
which, although warranted to wash, looked
as if they had been designed at some
distant period and afterwards cut short in
front so as to be quite a la mode and display the
fetlocks. The whole result was distinctly dread-
ful. Two dainty little feet, tripping gaily into
full view from beneath a poem by Worth, of
Paris, might possibly incite a jockey to fall down
and worship the very ground they send a thrill
through. But a pair of turned-in tootsies over-
shadowed by a skirt which stands out from the
waist at an angle of 45 all round, bring little con-
solation to the man who has come a cropper over
the last race. Lord Hopetoun’s crushed-strawberry
blonde relatives attracted the particular sacri-
legious bookmaker, who wanted to take odds that
Lady Dorothea was the long ’un, and Lady
Estella the short ’un, or vice versa —just as anyone
cared to bet. A staunch courtier looked wither-
ingly at this disgusting person, and asked him
how he could possibly discover which lady was
which. The skinner of lambs promptly explained.
He would, if necessary, go right up to ’em and
shriek “Dorry-t/ie-a !” and the first one as turned
round would be ’er, and he would pay out ac-
cordin’.
# * *
It was generally reported at Flemington
that Hopetoun had no fancy for Blackwood’s
Vengeance.
* * *
The story that little Lord Dudley is
“ engaged ” to one of the Prince of Wales’
daughters, though probably all moonshine, would
account for Tummy’s parental attitude to the
young man since he was arrested in a gambling-
hell. Nevertheless, it is barely conceivable that
an out-and-out Juggins with a “gone’’leg is to
be dragged into the Guelph circle for the sake of
his family diamonds, most of which may already
be glittering up the spout. Dudley, according to
the very latest accounts, is still making a deplor-
able object of himself, having started a sort of
Lucullus Club, where members invite their friends
to dine at 80 guineas ahead, and reckon the little
feed a failure, bai Jove ! if it costs less than £5OO.
The state of Tummy’s liver doesn’t admit of his
entering keenly into these intellectual enjoyments,
consequently he can’t want Dudley as a son-in-law
for the sake of getting peacocks’ brain soup gratis.
Nor is Albert Edward too dense to understand
that his young friend’s mania for baccarat is likely
to break out again worse than ever as soon as he
gets thoroughly accustomed to the glare of the
royal eye. Where, then, could be the object in
marrying Maud to this unreliable little gorger,
the degenerate son of a cranky father, whose one
great achievement was that historical visit to the
Sultan of Turkey’s harem, which cost him his ears
and qualitied the late Duke of Wellington to run
for the Geldings’ Plate, as old Squire Western
would have put it? However, if Maud hankers
after her Dudley, and has pointed him out to
mamma as the young man who alone can make
her happy, she may as well have him. Love, ac-
cording to Tolstoi, is the inevitable result of high
living, so it would be against Nature for Maud
not to desire a husband of some sort.
* * *
Mr. Johnny Bruce, of Melbourne, has
graciously cabled the announcement that somebody
(name, Johnny?) provided him with a farewell
banquet at the Hotel Metropole, previous to h’s re-
shipment for Australia. Also that J. B. Pa< tersou
celebrated the occasion with a speech remarkable
for its “patriotic fervour.” “Potty ” Bruce had just
such a farewell dinner before he left Melbourne,
and then the same old Jingo, if we r. member
rightly, delivered the same old eulogium upon
Victoria Regina and her faithful animal iho
British Lion. It makes us very tired.
#.* # '
A certain Melbourne suburb had civically
decided to have itself proclaimed -a city. The
kind of reception to be accorded the Governor on
the occasion of his official visit was being dis-
cussed when one of the members (chairman of a
Board of Advice under the Education Act) pro-
posed|; “ That a tribunal arch be erected on the
bridge, another tribunal arch at Brown’s Hill, and
yet another tribunal arch at the Town Hall.”
# # *
Perhaps the largest amount ever made in
Australia through the sale of one single building
was netted by Mr. Tom Curran, the Sydney wine-
merchant, who one morning was offered Temple
Court, Sydney (corner of King and Elizabeth
streets), and, after a little haggling, bought it
right away for £18,250. A year or so afterwards
he was crossing the street and was bailed up by
“Ginger” Clarke, who asked him if he wanted
to sell Temple Court. “I don’t,” replied Curran,
“ I’m quite content to keep it—it’s paying me 10
per cent. nett.” But what is your price?” asked
Clarke. “ £50,000,” replied Curran. “ I’ll get it
for you to-morrow morning,” replied “Ginger.”
And he did.

When a daily newspaper speaks of “ that
well-known citizen, Mr. Blank,” it often means
that Mr. Blank is “ better known than trusted.” -
* * *
Breach of promise case. Plaintiff’s barrister
was hurriedly instructed, but made a great point
by melting the jury down, as he pointed to hislovely
client. Defendant’s barrister : “My learped friend
is not aware that she has a wooden leg.”
# # #
They have just borrowed, in Melbourne
Public Library, a sensible custom from the Sydney
Library, It is now a rule that readers must leave
their books on the table, instead of replacing them
on the shelves. Numbers of books are reported
stolen, which afterwards turn up on the wrong
shelves ; for instance, Balzac’s “ Contes Drola-
tiques ” were found, only the other day, thrust
into the midst of the Theology, which Mephis-
tophelian act could hardly have been pure accident.
Another reform wanted is to engage a smart young
librarian, athletic, to swarm up and down the
ladders for books for the young ladies.
* * *
A “ popular ” N.S. W. Town Clerk is now
wanted for embezzlement. The Town Council
allowed him to be popular on £4 a week.
* # #
An honest bank-clerk is the scarcest work
of God.

rests on a secure foundation. Gillies is about the
only man in Australia who manages to be both fat
and bony at the same time, and he has a shiny
look like the outside of an oiled dictionary, and
altogether he would be improved by a little hair.
Hopetoun’s beard, on the other hand, is a dead
failure, and the man who would grow such a thing
in the light of day is reckless enough to have a
shower-bath in the middle of Bourke-street right
on the tram-line. It is glaring and scrubby, and
looks like a squashed currant, or a quantity of
