# * #
Adelaide is a lucky city. It has but one
public statue, and that is an ugly, dirty, almost
crumbled monument to Colonel Light, erected in
classic Light-square, which is to Adelaide what
Pimlico is to London.
# * *
Charles Lowenthal, sentenced to death in
Adelaide last week for the murder of his wife, is
an old soldier, and served on the German side in
the Franco-Prussian War. He appears insane,
and the authorities will in the end probably arrive
at that conclusion.
* * *
The Melbourne bank-clerks, apeing the
University-Students, took anight at Melbourne
Princess’s last week, having hired the amphi-
theatre for this gorgeous occasion. They macje
several laudable attempts to be funny between the
acts, but distinctly missed a point in not yelling
for the management to shut up the sliding-roof
and reduce their overdraft. On the other hand
they introduced a certain amount of pathos into
the proceedings by dangling a flag inscribed,
‘ ‘ What price bank-clerks —£60 a year ?” which
must have sent a cold shudder through several of
their female adorers down below.
* * *
That hereditary guinea-pig, the new Duke
of Manchester, is also visiting Australia for the
benefit of his health, and will doubtless make an
inspection of our comic-opera girls. In his pre-
vious capacity of Lord Kimbolton, the Duke was
a familiar, very familiar, character behind the
scenes. In fact, he was generally known there as
“ Uncle Kim.” Now, we want to know if Bessie
Bellwood is coming with him. He’ll need her in
his little rows with cabmen.
# * *
A Melbourne paper points out two interest-
ing facts. (1) That George Labertouche, charged
at Sydney Water Police Court with a num-
ber of forgeries, was allowed a seat at the
solicitors’ table. In the same court a clerk, out
of work, charged with forging one cheque for a
pound, was placed behind the spikes. What has
the Minister of Justice to say to this ? (2) That a
subscription-list, in aid of the hard-up Sydney
Carmelite nuns, was started by the Stale Foul
proprietary. Ye gods !
* # »
We are told that in Sydney a scheme of
Juvenile Immigration is being darkly formulated
in most influential circles. In plain words, the
alleged society seeks to get hold of cheap boy and
cheaper girl labour for the immediate profit of
capitalists, under the avowed gospel that the
scheme is for the future benefit of the children
themselves. The object is to make willing
drudges out of pliable material. The servant-girl
difficulty is at the bottom of the scheme.
# # • #
A certain Blue-ribbon Mayor from up
North was seated at the Federation banquet next
to a burly Parliamentary official who is a most
vigorous diner. Now, the Mayor “ never touches
it, ” so the burly guzzler cottoned to him amazingly
and drank the share of both. Bottle after bottle
of gold-top disappeared “all the same way,” and
the capable Bacchanalian “never seemed to get
any forurder.” The way he “ put it down ” was
only equalled by the way he “put it up” on
the Mayor. “Now, then, Queenslander,” he
would boisterously remark, “we must celebrate
that last sentiment with another. Here, waiter !
Another bottle of fizz for me and the Queens-
lander /”
# # *
Governor Kintore is now on the road to
Port Darwin, prior to overlanding it to Adelaide.
The “gag” is that he is only to be accompanied
by Dr. Stirling and a naturalist, but the S. A. Post-
master-General, Surveyor-General and Commis-
sioner of Police have all commanded their
departmental officers stationed on the chosen
route to properly receive and entertain the expe-
dition, which will be as well shepherded as if it
were lying in splints in the Children’s Hospital.
Roast emu with scorpion sauce, and spiced hog-
weed, will probably find their way into the
expense vouchers for the next three months of the
overland telegraph-clerks. We can recommend
camel-rasher fried in “ gohanna ”-fat as a soothing
sleep-producer when the mosquitoes are over-
persistent. Boiled muscaria, let stand till it sours
and ferments, makes a passable brain-paralyser,
and will make an explorer see hundreds of
curiosities that would otherwise remain invisible
We await Kintore’s “report to her Majesty’s
Government” with a curiosity as to the cypher-
code and the ruby country.
# # #
Sir John Robertson once went up country
and came across ablackfellow who had lost a leg.
Filled with compassion, and cold water, Jack
instructed the local superintendent of police to lose
no time in securing a wooden limb for poor old
Tarpot. In due time the leg was strapped on to
Tarpot and the account for it was sent to Jack at
the Lands Department. Sydney, whence it was
duly forwarded to the Treasurer. The Treasury
returned it saying “ there was no vote for wooden
legs ’* and the voucher was bandied between the
two departments several times. At last one day,
while Jack was cogitating over the matter, a lead-
ing officer came in with a batch of papers. “ Here,
Mr. So and So,” said old man Robertson, “ I
ordered a wooden leg for a blackfellow and the
Colonial Treasurer is too blanky miserable to settle
the bill. What’s to be done?” “Oh,” said the
offic'al, coolly rubbing his hands, “shove it down to
the furniture-vote.” And it was shoved down.
Likewise, it was paid for out of the furniture-vote.
*■ * *
The S. M. Herald has made itself
supremely ridiculous by getting out a second
edition containing a column of hysterics about
the thousands of wicked, blood-thirsty shearers
up North, who had gone to upset a pub-
lican’s buggy and were kept at bay by
one heroic mounted police-sergeant and two
foot constables, who ultimately dispersed
them with great slaughter. Ye gods ! By the
way, the present writer believes that he knows
one of the gallant policemen mentioned in this
connection. Years ago, in Ear Northern Queens-
land, the brave officer referred to had in
his charge, at a diggings lockup, a well-
known horse-thief under committal, who, finding
time hang heavily on his hands, kindly offered to
assist his custodian in cleaning out a certain
flooded well. The prisoner went down for a spell,
and, after working for a few hours, came up say-
ing he felt very cold ; whereupon the “peeler,”
who bore his charge only official enmity, was
lowered to take up the running. The horse-thief,
when he found his friend safely at the bottom of
the well, cut the rope from the windlass, and then,
taking the constable’s horse, revolver, rifle, and a
fair amount of tucker, cleared out to the ranges.
It is pointed out that numbers of N.S.W.,
station-masters and railway night-officers have been
working continuously for three years without
holiday.
The Bulletin wishes to make a sugges-
tion for the benefit of the distracted bank-directors
of Australia and the countries round about.
Hitherto this harassed race has systematically
appointed all manner of sincere Christians, good
young men, and holy snufflebusters of every de-
scription, to take charge of the cash, and it has
been “left ” by this moral crowd every time. The
apostle who was bursting with texts has escaped
over the brim of the horizon and disappeared with
the entire contents of the office. The mild, con-
sumptive youth who absolutely wallowed in prayer
has bashed the safe into shivers with a sledge-
hammer and hooked everything out of it. The
solemn, spectacled Christian in the bill depart-
ment has feloniously discounted bad paper for his
low, carnal uncle, and stood in on the proceeds.
The dreamy, faraway-looking Y.M.C.A. person
has skedaddled with the loot, and the rest of the
preachers have gone the whole hog generally.
And because of these evil results The Bulletin
desires, as already mentioned, to make a sug-
gestion. It proposes that a new bank should be
started with a howling atheist as manager and a
HE KNEW.
INSTRUCTOR : “By what do you know your Major?"
RECRUIT: “By the way he rides, sir.”
e54yw
profane and hard-drinking youth for chief account-
ant. The ledger-keeper will be an immoral person
who has been in gaol for bigamy, and two insol-
vent bookmakers will work under him. ihe
teller will be suspected of murder if possible, and
if that can’t be arranged he will at least be utterly
unprincipled; and as for the branch managers,
inspectors, and the like, nobody will be eligible
who has any kind of a character from a clergyman,
or the slightest reputation for benevolence or
piety or anything else. Any individual found
snuffling over a good book will be promptly
sacked ; anybody attempting to wear a heavenly
expression will be sacked instantly ; any person
developing a look of holy resignation will be
sacked faster even than either of the other two.
The official who rigs himself up in a white tie and
a sacerdotal aspect will be banged right out into
the gutter for the first offence, and if it is dis-
covered that any clerk in the bank’s employ has
been born of poor but honest parents he shall be
discharged relentlessly. If a relative of any
officer goes into the ministry that officer shall be
immediately removed to another branch, and an
employe who is seen attempting to manifest good
principles will go out the instant he can find his
hat and his month’s salary will be thrown to him
through the window, for employes who manifest
pood principles have broken more banks than can
be counted. And, above all things, if anybody is
found praying, or humming a little hymn, or
carrying a tract in his pocket, or wearing a rapt,
seraphic air, the cash will be immediately counted
to see that nothing has gone astray, and when
this ceremony is completed the offender will be
bounced out by the back door ; and the person
who is discovered giving a donation to a church
will have a fair chance to break his neck in his
hurried flight down the front steps. Last, but
far from least, anybody connected with the in-
stitution who attempts to Do Good in any way,
l anre or small, shall be firmly but sternly ejected.
There will be no defalcations or mysterious short-
ages in that bank either, and the reserve fund will
speedily rise to £11,000,000. Also the dividend
will be 581 per cent.
POLITICAL POINTS
The cost of the Federation banquet will be
something between one or two thousand ponn a.
The individual who so kindly and unquestioning y
“ pays for all ” will settle the bill.
The Canadian general elections have
resulted in a “ victory ” for the Government iy a
majority of 24 votes, a result which must strum
terror into the Imperialistic party.
The first thing is to get into Parliament,
and the next is to get appointed as member o a
Special Commission of Enquiry into i lungs in
General.- That’s when you feel all right.
The Victoria capitalistic party is ‘ judi-
ciously ” stuffing the electoral rolls, and a mine
will have to be sprung on the Munro Government
ere any One-man-one-vote Bill can be made law.
Some attempt has been made to conciliate
Playford, and he supported Parkes’ nomination
for President of the Convention. Nevertheless,
Tom has much bitterness in his heart, and an evil
leer in his eye.
One-man-one-vote has just been rejected
in the Commons by a majority of 102 votes. Up
to date it has been adopted by only one ot tne
Australasias—New Zealand —and all the Govern
ments are fencing it.
Premier Ballance, of N.Z., prophesies
that most of the future Governors of that colony
will be chosen from among the native-born. -Ims
may be taken as a promise that the Ballance
Government intend to support Sir George Grey s
Elective Governors’ measure.
In a late Times’ article, the writer asks —
Is the negro willing to go ?
Can the negro be dispensed with ?
How can he he removed ?
Whither can he be sent ?
Someone cynically suggests that the word “negro”
be dropped and that of “unemployed” substituted.
Parnell has been stung into declaring, as
he did long ago in America, that his aim has
always been the national independence of Ireland.
Just so. It comes to the old “ Robert Emmet ”
of free Ireland, for the rift between English and
Irish is racial, ineffaceable. Salisbury and
Chamberlain see this squarely enough, if Glad-
stone doesn't. Home Rule is only a step.
Premier Munro complained in public
t’other day that he was a managing director of
three Coffee Palaces—the Grand, Federal, and
Victoria—andnoneof them were booms. Theseand
the Federal Bank, the Federal Building Society,
and his stations at King Sound, Cambridge Gulf,
and Derby just about fill nine-tenths of his broad
Scotch mind, leaving a fag-end with which he
rules a nation.
There is a Young England Conservative
party favourable to the strikers. The fact is that
Liberalism is the worst foe to Radicalism, just as
the good is ever the bitter enemy to the best, and
the proletariat may squeeze as much out of
Salisbury as it will get from Gladstone. Ran-
dolph Churchill’s physic, not quite senseless, is to
crush the bourgeoisie between the upper and lower
jaw with Tory-Democracy.
E. Steinfeld, who has been everything
political by turns, has induced the Victorian
Chamber of Manufactures to declare that in its
opinion Parliament should have power to impose
differential duties in favour of Great Britain and
British possessions. What is Somersault Stein-
feld—mark you, Steinfeld the German-Australian,
not Steinfeld the Englishman or English-
Australian—giving us? To whom or what is
Steinfeld loyal ?
Queen Victoria has been upon the throne
about 54 years, during which time she has received
from a grateful country an average of, say,
£500,000 per annum. At compound interest this
sum figures out to about £150,000,000. Now, is it
worth it? A paper tells us that the Prince of
Wales during the 49 years of his life has drawn
£3,300,000 from the public Treasury, and his debts
now amount to £1,600,000. At compound interest
he has had, say, £50,000,000. Now, is it worth it ?
Premier Munro’s idea that the right of
appeal to the British Privy Council should be
maintained even after the Constitution of an Aus-
tralian Federal Court of Appeal, is certainly a
lovely idea. Munro’s proposal amounts to this :
that the man with a long purse shall be enabled
to carry his case through three expensive courts
instead of two. Apropos, would Munro give
Money a Privy Council appeal and deny such an
appeal to a citizen sentenced to death ?
J, P. Garvan, who was to have been a
delegate but was shunted in favour of Dibbs, has
once or twice put in an appearance on a back seat
among: the spectators at the Federal Convention.
Mr. Garvan is a gloomy-looking man and his
favourite preliminary words in speech-making are
“ I have a solemn duty to perform.” He was
only known to smile emphatically on one occasion.
He was presiding at a colossal insurance banquet,
and his colleagues told him that if he didn’t smile
he’d “ burst the whole show.”
Sir Samuel Griffith told the Federation
banqueters that he is and ever shall be indebted
“ for any success that he may have been able to
attain, or that he may attain in the future, to the
distinguished professors of the University of
Sydney” in his college days. He thus alleges
that he is an artificial man, without originality
and devoid of native braininess. In contrast,
Parkes is not a University man; neither was-
te come to Sir Samuel’s profession—the late Sir
James Martin. Neither is Chief Justice Lilley,
of Queensland.
Speaker Lee Steere, of W.A. Assembly, is
a stickler for ceremony. Hecently, he gravely
informed the members of the Assembly that he
considered that some difference should be made
in the manner of receiving messages from the
Legislative Council and from the Governor. As
a mark of respect to the latter, he suggested that
the bearer of the vice-regal message should be
permitted to traverse the Chamber and hand the
message to the Speaker in person ; while all
messages from the “other place” should be
delivered at the bar of the House. Suggestion
accepted unanimously.
Why Parkes’ tilt against “ professional ”
Governors in his banquet-speech ? This is how
the milk got into the cocoa-nut. Some years ago
Sir Hercules Robinson said at a London public
dinner something to the effect that Sir George
Grey was the only man in the Southern Hemi-
sphere to whom people could look for an authori-
tative opinion on the question of Australian
Federation, and this was reported in the papers
at the time. Parkes, knowing that ex-“ profes-
sional ” Governor Grey was coming to the Con-
ference, had a dig at the older and greater man by
sneering at the “ class ” to which Grey belonged.
Take this as your axiom : Transportation
always is, and always was, wrong. Every country
must consume its own smoke, nor c*an reference
be tolerated to the convict legislators, squatters,
parsons’ fathers, mothers, of any place on earth.
They cost too much, and the steam-roller has to
be trundled over them too lone:, there has to be
too much grinding in the sugar-pan to get out the
stalks. Let England consume its own smoke —
heaps of room for settlement on the antres wild
and deserts idle of the Scotch deer forests, with
all the advantage of the rich philanthropists being
able to take a run up and see their poor proteges.
But no, they want to shuffle them out of sight.
Put down a few pounds and cart the rubbish
clean out of the country to some other.
There can be no such possibility as all-
round “freedom of contract” in any country
which recognises private property in land. All
the country’s wealth comes from the land (or the
waters). The land-owner having got possession
of some of the wealth-producing commodity him-
self, has absolutely enslaved the landless man, who
has thus no “freedom of contract.” The land-
owner enjoys to the full “ freedom of contract ” to
employ labour at as low a wage as it will accept,
or not to employ it and personally wrest produce
and wealth from the land. The landless man has
the empty “ freedom of contract ” to accept work
for whatever the land-owner will give, or to clear
off the land and starve. This kind of “freedom
of contract” is the freedom of “no compulsion,
only you must.”
Extract from a Continental Socialistic
“ Catechism of a Woman of the People ” ;—
Q. What are you ?
A. I am a woman of the people.
q. What is your mission in life ?
A. To work and to suffer.
q. Why only to work and to suffer?
A. Because I am the wife of a slave.
Q. But you have children, who should be your joy and
consolation ?
A. The wife of the master has children, who are her joy
and pride. For me, I have no children ; they belong to
the master.
Q. Why do your children not belong to you ?
A. When they are 12, the factory and the mine take
them. No ; I have no children.
Q. But you have a husband to comfort and love you?
A. My husband does not belong to me ; he belongs tc
the master.
q. Why does your husband not belong to you ?
A. He leaves me at 4 in the morning; I don’t see him
again till 7 at night. Worn out with fatigue, he takes his
supper and goes to bed, to begin again next day. No, I
have no husband ; my husband belongs to the master.
And so on. That is how the Socialists educate
their women in politics.

At the invitation of Crack-Shot Carver, all the Vernon
boys, under the mgis of Captain Neitenstein, visited Wild
America on 4th inst. There was a very good throng on the
grandstand, including “Churaley ” and Secretary Goschen
from Government House; and Frank Cates, in a lovely
lavender suit, was there accompanied by a bewchus lady
in a palm-leaf fan. The show went splendidly, as usual.
Lord and Lady Jersey have been dining the Federal
delegates and some of their feminine belongings at Go-
vernment House.
The marriage of Miss Harriet D’Aroy Irvine, daughter
of the Rev. Canon D’Arcy Irvine, of North Goulburn, to
Mr. Edward M. Zouch, son of the late Captain Zouch, of
Goulburn, took place at Goulburn on 4th inst. in the
presence of a crowd of people. The wedding was very
quiet. The bride wore silver-grey relieved with silk of a
darker tint and huge bouquet, her one bridesmaid in
white and silver grey. The church was beautifully
decorated with roses.
It is rumoured that Mayor Bardekin intends giving a
grand fancy ball prior to his departure for Yurop.
Amongst the passengers from Sydney by the Japan
mail steamer, which left Svdney for Japan last week, was
Mr. John Smcdley, the well-known architect and amateur
artist, who goes for a trip.
Governor Jersey will attend a dinner to celebrate the re-
opening of the Sydney Athenseum Club (lately damaged
by fire) on 11th April.
The Cup Blend, a delicious tea,
School-Inspector Barker (England) has
been furnishing a report on some clever little
rustic girls he lately encountered officially. The
school-ma’am instructed one of them on verbs and
their tenses, and asked for an example of the verb
To Be. “ Past tense, I was a baby ; future tense,
I shall have a baby,” replied the ingenuous infant.
The original genuine article —Wolfe’s Schnapps.
One Nunn was charged at Sydney Central
last week with sawing wood on the Sawbath, but
it was found that the “stocks ” was the appointed
penalty for this horrible crime, and as the
“stocks,” like the pillory, have long passed into
oblivion, Nunn was discharged. The whipping-
post and the hangman’s noose still remain with
us.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, March 11,1891-
Mt Dear Moorabinda,—
A great throng of all sorts of people attended the
first reception given by Lady Jersey at Government
House on 3rd inst., and however the Countess managed
the corners of her mouth while some of the grotesque
objects were bobbing and ducking in front of her in their
awful frocks and bonnets is a puzzle, for, unlike most
women, Lady Jersey seems to have a keen sense of the
comical. The arrangements differed slightly from that of
the Carington regime—the names being announced twice,
Captain “ Chumley ” , calling them from the door and
brother Rupert No. 2 repeating them to the Countess,
who received at the foot of the dais in the ballroom, a
line of artillerymen holding a cord down the room to
keep the visitants from straying in the wrong direction.
No attempt whatever had been made to decorate the red
drawing-room, which looked as if the family were remov-
ing, and it was noticeable that all small articles of vertu
were looked out of harm’s way. The piazza was covered
in with awnings, and there tea and coffee were served to
the crowd, who ate everything on the tables and smashed
a little crockery as well.
There was very little dressing; hundreds of the visitors
were perfect shudders from their heels to their bonnet-
strings. Lady Jersey (who, unlike Lady Carington, re-
ceived without a hat) wore a gown of pink silky material
with a spot of the same colour made with a round skirt
gathered very full on the hips and trimmed with two
bands running down the hem and up the front and back
of the skirt, of black lace insertion ; the bodice made
with full front and puffed sleeves at the top, with bands
of the lace insertion going three times round the arm and
once down the front of the bodice; collar with fine lines o f
black jet fastened by a diamond brooch; tan gloves and a
gold bangle. The countess held a large bouquet which
harmonized with her gown. Mrs. Sydney Burdekin, in
apricot striped canvas cloth trimmed with cream Spanish
lace and bars of sage-green silk, flat hat hi biscuit straw
trimmed with apricot silk gauze and blowaways; Mrs.
Aronson wore apretty gown in India-red cloth with whipped
flounce round the feet of black lace basque of black lace
and small black bonnet; Mrs. Alfred Cape, fawn poplin
with black lace visite ; Signora Majeroni, who looked
very ill, bronze-green silk skirt and bronze-green velvet
bodice, toque hat en suite ; Mrs. Gray, black poplin with
bands of black cord guipure over white silk, bonnet in
black jet and feathers ; Mrs. J. H. Carruthcrs, white silk
with chiffon ruffles, white feathered hat; Mrs. Alfred de
Lissa, floral gown with relOvements of caruhia satin, toque
of scarlet poppies; Miss Burdett-Smith, grey; Miss
Nellie Hill, fawn cloth combined with broken checks en
suite, and dozens more. There were lots of men present
(one unhappy creature, haunted, perhaps, by thoughts of
the Governor’s levee, came in full evening dress), and it
was lovely to see the groundlings nudge one another
when the captain of the German warship in port kissed
Lady Jersey’s hand in foreign style.
Why do the “ galloway ” women take so frantically to
fashions which are death to them ? At the Jersey recep-
tion the other day some misguided females, about four
feet nothing longitudinally, came out confidently in the
new Long-Tom paletdt which leaves off half-way to one’s
knees, and makes anybody but a. lady lamp-post look like
a walking trunk. No short woman should wear a long
jacket, no matter how modish she thinks it to be, for,
after all, what is it we most admire? Not fashion
but fashion when it becomes the wearer. Short women
shouldn’t wear lace basques either, which are the ne plus
ultra of style at present, unless they are dying to look
like the fictitious Irish gentleman who wore his two legs
down to his knees.
Speaking of styles, it was remarked at Government
House that all the best-dressed people wore black head-
gear, no matter how gay their frocks were. There is
magic in such a topping-off, for a gown that seems to be
too garish for words is sobered down directly one gets on
a black bonnet. I was glad to see, too, that hair was very
discreetly dressed, and that torn-out birdsnests were only
observed on the crania of the Awful Brigade.
It reflected somewhat on the hospitality extended to
visitors at the recent Government House reception that a
great number of women present, after leaving the cere-
mony, made a bee-line for the ice-cream and coffee
down town. The Jerseys have yet to learn what a hungry,
lantern-jawed locust is the fat Sydney Government ’Ouse
haunter in the nodding bonnet and the hand-bag.
Is it true, the whisper so industriously circulated just
now, that the wife of a certain magnate is anxious to get
him afloat on the briny, so that he may be out of hear-
ing of cork-popping ? I have heard corks pop even on
a mail-steamer.
Apropos to voyages, I am told that while on the
journey out it was remarked that Lady Jersey was ex'
tremely attentive to her children, so much so that at first
some of the passenger's mistook her for a maid. Indeed,
one day early in the passage, as she was bringing a glass
of water to one of the girls, a fellow-passenger (who was
doubtless of the opinion that real countesses spend most
of their time in casting mediaeval sneers from under
diamond coronets at their serfs) spoke to her in the
familiar style in which it is probably his wont to address
maids. Nobody ever knew what answer he got, but he
was observed suddenly to turn the colour of boiled beet.
At the Federal banquet the other evening Lady Jersey
wore a trained gown of dark-green velvet embroidered in
gold with gold passementerie on the bodice and gorgeous
liamonds—the same gown that she wore qn her unhappy
visit to the Queen at Balmoral, when she had to stand in
the night air with uncovered shoulders (while Her
Majesty cooled off) and so caught the cold antecedent to
aer late severe illness.
A Sydney Women’s Union (not to be too precise) has
broken out in a fresh place. For instance, the presi-
dentess had been so terribly shocked at an advertisement
inserted in one of the papers by “two young ladies
wanting situations as barmaids,” that she immediately
wrote them a letter asking them to “ consider well before
they entered upon such a career.” This is all very
pious, of course, but uneducated women must earn their
living somehow, and I presume that the danger of the
barmaid’s calling depends very much upon the sort of
woman who engages in it. lam doubtful whether the
Union would carry its enthusiasm so far in the good cause
as to procure remunerative work of another kind for the
women in whom it takes such a loving, pen-and-ink
interest. But if society will peddle with sinful and
worldly “ads.,” it might leave the hounded-down Ilebes
alone and exert itself on behalf of the “ young widows’
who “ would receive one gentleman boarder,” and the
bachelors who are in need of “domesticated housekeepers
under 30.”
The ladies of the Melbourne Austral Salon were “at
home” on 27th ultimo, and received many visitors, in-
cluding quite a crowd of men. Snazelle was there in
full blast, and all sorts of people turned up, including
Lady Davies and Victor Daley. Mrs. Macdonald was the
receiving hostess of the occasion. The next excuse the
Salon has for indulging in another outbreak is a benefit
to Matilda Ashton, a blind girl who recently matriculated
and is now anxious to gain a degree.
Women are not at all enthusiastic over the Federation
Convention. Those of them who know anything about
the matter are afraid of losing Governors and Governors’
wives of the present pattern and getting instead one
grand Panjandrum of a Governor-General. This would
be all very well for those living at the Capitol, but
how would society “ hum ” for those who didn’t ?
Only one guest turned up at the Federation feed in un_
dress costume, and that was the member for Paddington.
Said a notoriously hard-up swell who seemed hurt at the
absence of boiled shirt in Alfred’s appearance: “You
can’t g 6 into sassiety much if you haven’t a dress-soot.”
“ But at any rate this one is paid for,” retorted the Pad-
dington poet.
I have often wondered how travellers by train, unfor-
tunate enough to get into the same compartment as the
honeymoon couple who are made targets for old boots
and things from the platform, managed to escape so fre-
quently without a black-eye and their ears full of rice.
They do not always get off scot-free, for I remember one
such occasion when a large and irascible male passenger
with no sympathy for the joyousness of the proceedings
and who had received a cut from the heel of a hurtling
shoe, stood at the window and shook both fists at the
hilarious wedding-part} 7 , vowing he’d bring an action for
battery against the whole of them. It is probably in
the interest of law and order that the Mudgee station-
master has put his foot down and declined to allow wed-
ding guests to pelt things at the ’appy pair in the train,
and so the floating railway population has no need to
seek refuge under the seat when a wedding-party comes
aboard at that particular stopping-place.
It must be a true saying that a woman is just as old as
she feels. If not, how could this happen ? A maiden
lady on the shady side of 70, residing at North Shore,
invited a young lad who lived next door to help her nail
up a creeper on the verandah-post. The business done,
the boy was, in the most natural manner, following the
lady into the house, when the latter, in stern tones,
turned upon him with —“ No, Robert, not a step further.
People will talk, you know !”
Apropos of advts. : An old-clo'lady announces in the
dailies that she is the purchaser of “ old artificial teeth.’
These, of course, she polishes up and soils to the dentists
at a “ sacrifice,” and then the sassiety lady, who has her
fangs torn out because they are so uneven, my dear, gets
a nice new set which were originally the property cf a
Goulburn-street typhoid-fever corpse. Ugh !
On dit that the next marriageable daughter in a Sydney
family of girls who, from a society point of view, have
been very fortunate matrimonially, has received an offer
from a titled aide-de-camp attached to a neighbouring
vice-regal “ court.” The matter is being kept very much
under the rose by the parties concerned, and some say
that the bold girl has had the rashness to refuse him.
A characteristically Australian incident happened at
the Jersey reception. When the paid artillerymen on
duty in the ball-room were set at liberty they were seen
shaking hands with and conversing with one or two of the
ladies present. They were probably sisters or sweethearts
or something of our bold stipendiary defenders; but
what must Lady Jersey have thought of them, coming as
she does, fresh from a land where no respectable woman
is ever seen on the same side of the street as Thomas
Atkins ! Vide Rudyard Kipling on this subject.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Nature and art conspired to make the Sydney Lieder-
taefel’s serenade of Governor and Lady Jersey the other
evening a thing to remember. The night was cool, starlit,
calm and balmy, and the singers seemed to have drunk
deep of its beauty and become permeated with its spirit
ere unburdening their souls of melody. The only fly in
the ointment was the extraordinarily fulsome speech
made by that enthusiastic courtier, Sir Patrick Jennings.
Lord Jersey’s reply was brief, manly, and modest.
Charles Huenerbein is again to the fore with three
morceaux characterisques : “ Consolation,” a tender
wistful arpeggio melody ; “ The Dream,” a lulling,
plaintive strain with soft slumbrous chords for the right
hand, with the theme for the left. “ Regret” is another
of Mr. Huenerbein’s compositions, and so is “Gavotte”
to be “ dedicated to my favourite pupil.” They will be
published shortly, and are sure to have a great drawing-
room run.
The marriage of Miss Mabel Kirby, step-daughter of the
late Mr. E. T. Waters, sometime editor of the EvKXiyo
News, with Mr. Carter, of the 8.N.Z., took place at St.
James’, Sydney, on 4th inst. The bride wore a travelling-
dress of old rose cashmere trimmed with Oriental em-
broidery, small bonnet en suite, and was attended by her
sister, Miss Grade Kirby, in cornflower blue. The break-
fast was held at the residence of the bride’s uncle,
Woollahra.
Mayor and Mrs. Burdekin have secured cabins in the
Orizaba, which leaves on 13th April.
A Turf Conundrum.
The Bulletin must plead guilty to the rather
common crime of publishing hopelessly bad tips for the
Newmarket Handicap and Australian Cup. To pick the
winner of a six-furlong scramble from a field of thirty-six
is, indeed, a job so difficult that it excuses failure,
especially when the attempt has to be made several days
before the event. But in dealing with a race like this
year’s Australian Cup, a “prophet,” having a quantity of
past facts and figures to work upon, should be able to
give some specious explanation of a ghastly error. The
Bulletin named Enuc as a likely winner, and also went
so far as to include Vengeance amongst a lot that had no
show, “on paper,” of doing the trick. Such conduct
calls for an apology. Vengeance, several times beaten in
moderate company, had won but one, race-viz., the
Caulfield Cup—previous to this last Flemington meeting.
At Caulfield he was receiving two pounds from a com-
moner like Loyalstone, who ran him to a short head. It
was not a brilliant victory. The two horses laboured
down the straight, stride for stride, and at the finish they
were being rapidly overtaken by little Enuc, who had
seemed altogether out of the fun 250 yards from the
judge’s box. On that Caulfield Cup performance The
Bulletin assumed that neither Vengeance nor Loyalstone
(equal on “form”) was likely to win the Melbourne Cup
over an extra half-mile of ground, and in the longer race,
as everybody knows, they were both handsomely beaten
by Enuc, who then finished on the quarters of Correze,
and about a length and a half behind Highborn—the lot
being donkey-licked by Carbine.
# * *
Four days later Vengeance ran again in the
V.K.C; Handicap of IJmiles, meeting Correze and Enuc
on their Melbourne Cup terms, or thereabouts. The two
three-year-olds confirmed the previous “form” by run-
ning first and second, Vengeance finishing fourth. Up to
then there was nothing more evident than that Vengeance
had no pretensions to be called a “good Tin,” and cer-
tainly no claim to be regarded as a stayer. As a five-year-
old he would scarcely develops much bone and sinew
within the next four months, whilst the youngsters, in
the natural course of things, would improve at least a
stone.
# * *
As the Caulfield Cup was run, it seemed that
Vengeance was lucky to beat Enuc at all, at a difference
of five pounds in the latter’s favour. So much for the
apparent relative merits of the two horses over an easy
14 miles. Over 2 miles (Melbourne Cup) Euue gets 17
pounds from the other and loses him. Again over
miles Enuc receives 141 b. and beats Vengeance
by some three* lengths or so the Enuc and
Correze running in the two races being noticeably
consistent. These youngsters have four months in
which to acquire more muscle and stamina before meet-
ing the already matured Vengeance over a 2 j-mile course,
and they have much the better of the handicap with
their old opponent. Sticking to the case of Enuc we find
that he receives 91b. in the Australian Cup, or only 511).
less than Vengeance so completely failed to give him in
the V.K.C. Handicap of last spring.
# #
But neither Enuc nor Correze has a ghost of
look-in at any point of the Australian Cup, whilst Ven-
geance gets heme from Highborn. Vengeance is meet-
ing the runner-up to Carbine with an advantage of
231 b, as compared with the Melbourne Cup weights,
consequently they run somewhat according to “form,”
or what would have seemed like “ form ” had Vengeance
been already a proved stayer. But this fact merely com-
plicates the puzzle. Highborn, who is also a five-year-old
and past his growing days, meets Enuc and Correze on
151 b. and 121 b. worse terms than he did when they
finished right up against him in November. In short,
Highborn runs quite a 2 stone better horse than one
three-year-old who was third in the Melbourne Cup, and
another who was third in (he Caulfield Cup and Legcr.
We have seen Carbine give Highborn 4 stone (all but
31b.) and a tremendous bearing. How, then, are Correze
and Enuc to be handicapped with Carbine next season ?
Melbourne Herald's racing reporter was carried
away by his feelings last Thursday, when lie-wired : “The
geiu'ation of the day was the winning of the Autumn
Handicap in such a sennaiwnal manner by such a sensa-
tional horse as Marvel.” But m particular sensation was
experienced by the general public, who, for the most pait,
didn’t hear these all /gcd offers of 100 to 2 against the
long-boltled-up animal, and failed lo perceive that he
“ overpowered his jockey.” In truth, the whole business
seemed legitimate enough this time. Clever people
started by .airing 10 to 1 about Marvel, the price grajna. y
shortening to “ sixes.” Any subsequent knocking-cut of
the horse was apparently caused by one or two book-
makers thinking they knew something when they rca'ly
knew nothing. In the race Man el was allowed to make
tlie best of his way round, fairly close to the lads all the
time, and when he had gallantly led the procession past
the winning-post there seemed to be plenty of hackers’
tickets presented for payment.
# # *
Everything smells pure auel whole'ome in any
race that Carbine starts for, and the very punters who
have invested a trifle on one ot his opponents, just on the
off-chance, are ready with a magnanimous cheer when
Donald Wallace’s wonder comes in as usual. The demon-
stration on Champion day w as a repetition, mi a-smaller
scale, of the Melbourne Cup furore, and th aeing world
wore a bright, happy, almost honest aspect as gord old
Carbine returned to scale. You didn’t bear anyone
cursing his luck because be had wasted it sovereign over
The Admiral or Wbimbrel. The “ books ” accept the
\ ictories of “ Old Bay-over ” (as they call the great horse)
in a pleasant sort of way, for they have left off gambling
against him in weight-for-age races. A halo of sanctity
shines around Carbine’s not very handsome head. He
has never been “ milked” on the betting-market. If lie
isn’t intended to run for a race be is scratched at the
earliest opportunity, and be has never been the hero of
“ mysterious movements,” or a question of the money
being all right. He runs to win, and does w n, as many
as sixteen times in succession. It is supposed that a
jockey who attempted to “ pull” Carbine would imme-
diately fall dead from the saddle, and that the horse
would then pick up the corpse with his teeth and get
there all the same. He represents all that is excellent,
honest and plucky in horseflesh and Turf politics. He
will be the golden legend of the racing future, and-well,
God bless him, and take care of his c-cracked heel ! The
writer has broken down under his load of genuine emo-
tion.

A delay in hoisting tho “ all right ” flag after the
Australian Cup caused some innocents to suppose that
the V.R.C. stewards were politely requesting the ow ner of
Vengeance to give some explanation of that animal’s in-
and-out performances. Not that anyone supposes the
owner has much to do with such matters. Nor the trainer
nor jockey. They merely back the horse when he is
“well,” and tell their friends to do likewise, and the
horse—barring Ins Melbourne Cup failure—has always
won on those occasions.
# # *
This season’s three-year-olds must be a bad lot.
The Admiral, after beating Megaphone at Caulfield, just
manages to “do” Magic Circle in the V.K.C. Derby.
Then an unknown mare named Litigant heats Magic
Circle easily. Anon, Gibraltar beats The Admiral, after
which Gatling, w ho once ran Gibraltar to a head, comes
in a poor third for a Maiden Plate. Finally Megaphone,
who on previous handicap running, is the best youngster
of his year, gets beaten at nearly level weights hy a horse
(Highborn) that couldn’t touch Carbine at 3st. 111 b.
Trident, Carlyon, or even Dreadnought, would have
romped in for last Australian Cup as three-year-olds
carrying Megaphone’s weight.
* * *
How can punters hope to do any good when
many of the “books” are ignorant as to the unmistak-
able'“cronkness” of a prominent/aroimfe? For one of
the races at Flemington a certain horse w f as backed (by
the unfortunate public) until most of the ready-money
ringmen would lay no more. They went so far as to back
the horse “in” with brethren who had a little “ Xxx
money ” left on their volumes. Meanwhile, the trainer of
the horse in question was quietly shaking his head at.
friends who enquired by signs as to whether “it was all
right,” and candidly telling acquaintances that he didn’t
fancy anything. When the flag fell Xxx, whose jockey
had never moved in any of tho false starts, “ got badly
away,” and not till then did the “ books,” or most of
them, know that they had been afraid of a regular
“ stilt ’un.”

and the man who invests on the totalisator gets a chance
for second and third money, while the bookmaker gives
no second or third money. Mo wonder jthe influential

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid fur. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
S, Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated,
ii. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred ).
h. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, espccialli
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
he pre 'erred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of (he paper, and folded, not rolled. '1 he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not mereb on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will he made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

S. M. Herald declares piously and apoplec -
tically that the sudden death of “Dr. Keatinge ’’
in gaol “offers a lurid warning of the ruinous
consequences of a life in conflict with the moral
law and the legal code of society.” Bosh.
Keatinge simply had a run of ill-luck. For every
Keatinge that dies in the clutches of the law,
there are a hundred Bluebeards who die in their
beds and the odour of sanctity, after having for
years been respected subscribers to and advertisers
in papers like the Herald.

Miss Billee Barlow acted principal boy in last Christina
Edinburgh pantomime, and, on the morning after Boxing
Night, asked a German man-servant of the hotel where
she stayed, to bring her up a Scotsman to see how the
show went. The German brought her up the Coots,
remarking, “ Dis is de first Scotchman I find.”

A Tasmanian Mayor, who aspires to Par-
liament in the Freetrade interest, has pledged
himself to support freedom of contract between
whites and Chinese, and to aid in having the
Chinese poll-tax abolished. That such a man
should be a Mayor, let alone a possible M.P.,
shows how far Tasmania is politically behind the
times.

Personal Items.
David Christie Murray seems to be play-
ing himself pretty low down.
“ Angora” Parkes is the nom de plume of
Sir Henry in (South Australia,
Nob one of the speakers at the Federation
banquet was an Australian native !
The Playford Cabinet contains two Eng-
lishmen, a Yankee, a German, and two Austra-
lians.
Victorian Railway Commissioner Speight
has lost six inches in height, besides his antici-
pated rise in salary, since bachelor friend Gillies
got the “ run.”
Wiles, the sewing-machine man, who was
killed at Bathurst last week, was an ex-policeman
and took part in the fight with Moonlight’s gang
at Wantabadgery.
A grim printer’s error comes to us from
Queensland. It was written that Henry George
“ had a touch of aphasia. The printer made it
“ a touch of Aspasia.”
John Lucas, M.L.C., had, according to the
plan, two seats reserved for him at the Federal
Convention dinner. Wonder whether they laid
two dinners for the old gentleman ?
F. M. Mansel, private secretary to Gover-
nor Kintore, is a cousin of Mr. Henry Labouchere,
of Truth. He returns to England next May, and
is ambitious to embark in British politics.
The world’s boss embezzler was one Walter
Watts, a London clerk of the Globe Insurance
Co., for, with a salary of a little under £2OO a
year, he forked in £700,000. Go thou and—but
you can’t.
J. G. Jenkins, the new S. A. Minister of
Education, is generally known in that province as
the “member for America.” Jenkins first startled
Adelaide some years ago by canvassing for a book
on “ George Washington.”
The father of Mortimer Menpes, the now
almost famous artist, was a councillor years ago
at Port Adelaide, where Mortimer was born.
Mortimer was chiefly known during his youth as
a precocious office-boy, who delighted in carica-
ture.
Fronde, in his Australian travels, was at
a provincial town, where the Mayor was doubtful
as to the pronunciation of his name, and asked
the great man himself whether it was Frowd or
Frood. The Historian ; “Well, you know, many
people call me Fraud.”
Langworthy, the wealthy man whose
treatment of his wife and child created so great a
sensation in England a few years ago, died re-
cently in a lunatic-asylum in the Argentine Re-
public, leaving a magnificent property which
descends to his little daughter.
George Coppin (Vic.), who is believed to
be the only comedian who ever sat in Parliament,
once had his election for Richmond spoilt by an
Age reporter, who represented him as commencing
his hustings speech with the professional clown’s
greeting : “ Here we are again !”
When Architect Wardell showed Lord
Canterbury the plans of Government House,
Melbourne, with its 100 rooms, he asked ; “ Now,
your Excellency, is there anything you can
suggest ?” Excellency : “ Yes, I should like a
quiet little place, just to eat a chop in.”
The proprietor of the Inverell (N.S.W.)
Argus, being threatened with a libel-suit has
decided to change his name to “Macßond,” and
to don a kilt and a sporran, in the belief that such
a course of action will give him a claim on 99 men
out of every 100 in the Inverell district.
Bismarck relates that Czar Nicholas once
had an affliction which required his back to be
rubbed. Said Nicholas, “I cannot trust a
Russian to rub my back, I must always have my
eye on him,” wherefore he obtained from King
Frederick William the loan of two Prussian
sergeants to do the work.
Any legitimate descendants of Sir Philip
Francis (“Junius” Francis) residing in Austra-
lasia, upon communicating with “ Price Waning,”
care of the Editor of The Bulletin, will be placed
in possession of information likely to prove of
considerable financial advantage to them. A son
of Sir Philip arrived in Sydney between 1840 and
1850.
A rumour goes that Mr. Chas. Potter, the
N.S.W. Government Printer (whose recent in-
crease of salary points, like his extended holiday,
to superannuation) will retire on his return from
Europe, and that he will be succeeded by Mr. S.
E. Lees, M.L.A., for the Nepean. Mr. Lees, by
the way, is the obliging individual who lately
printed an edition of “ Parkes’ poems.”
This is how the S.M. Herald heraldised
Premier Munro’s Convention banquet quotation
from Longfellow’s best-known poem. Munro
quoted four lines, but two were enough for the
Herald :
Let us, then, be up and doing, with a heart for anything
Still achieving, still pursuing, learn to labour and to win-
(Cheers.)
Under the circumstances, “ cheers ” is good.
The retirement of N.S.W. Under-Secretary
Eagar has revived the anecdote about his hustings
tiff with Sir John Robertson, then plain Jack.
“When Mr. Robertson and I were at school
together,” said Eagar, “he was known as Jack
Brag.” “ When Mr. Eagar and I were at school
together,” retorted Jack, when his speak-time
came, “he was so like a weak, cross-tempered,
helpless girl that the boys all nicknamed him
Betsy Eagar.”
Oswald Keatinge’s letter to his wife can
be construed in either of two ways : either that he
intended to commit suicide, or that he, finding
himself utterly crushed under such a sentence at
such an age, had determined to lie down and die.
It can only be construed as inevitably pointing to
suicide by people who don’t believe it possible for
an utterly broken man to give up the ghost after
the manner of the Marquesans, as described by
Robert Louis Stevenson,
There were extenuating circumstances
about Disraeli’s famous plagiarism of Thiers’
funeral speech on Marshal St, Oyr, in 1829,
for his eulogy of Wellington in 1852. Dizzy
brought it, m 1848, under the notice of Mr.
Smyth, a leader-writer on the Morning Chroni-
cle, and Smyth there reprinted it, or otherwise a
writer in the Globe, who exposed Disraeli’s pla-
giarism, would not have seen it. Smyth wrote to
the Times stating these facts.
H. P. Venables, many years secretary to
the Victorian Department of Education, died re-
cently in England. He was a genially-taciturn old
fossil, and was dismissed on Black Wednesday by
Berry and Co. The only interesting thing known
concerning the old man was that his department
once addressed as “ Stewart,” a proud citizen who
spelt his name “Stuart,” whereupon the offended
scion of royalty in writing back addressed Mr.
Venables as “ Mr. Vegetables.”
Colonel Mundy, writing in 1852 of Dr
Lang and his opposition to transportation, says :
The speeches of this gentleman and his colleagues—one
of whom, by the bye, was a retailer of rocking-horses and
radicalism in prose and verse according to the require-
ments of the market —teemed with vulgar personalities
against the Governor and his advisers.
If the Colonel happens to be still alive he will be
no less surprised than pleased to know how little
of radicalism there is in the whilom vendor of
rocking-horses. Also, how agreeable Sir Henry
tries to make himself to the Governors and
governoresses.
A wonderful old Australian of the Sir
George Grey stamp arrived in Sydney last week
on a short trip, in the person of Commander
Crawford Pasco, R.N., a septuagenarian, who has
all the vim and wholesomeness of a man of forty.
The veteran—a son, by the way, of Nelson’s flag-
lieutenant who hoisted the famous signal at
Trafalgar first visited Sydney with H.M.S.
Beagle in the “Thirties,” Darwin, the immortal
scientific investigator, being the naturalist. Cap-
tain Pasco’s personal knowledge of the Australias
embraces the men and affairs of the whole inter-
vening period.
A portrait in oils of the late Peter Lalor
has been painted to the order of his son, Dr. Lalor,
for presentation to Ballarat Art Gallery. The
artist, Mr. Tennyson Cole, had to work from a
photo, of the Eureka hero, whose loss of an arm
was unfortunately concealed by his pose for the
original sitting. As Melbourne Herald intelli-
gently explains—“ This position shows all the
strength of the man physically, but hides from
sight the missing arm.” Which reminds us of the
Irishman who wore a shade over the eye that had
been gouged out and grew a heavy moustache to
conceal the teeth that Pat Mulligan punched
down his throat. The Irishman in question is
merely a creature of the writer’s fancy, but he does
very well as an illustration.
The members of Adelaide Branch Austra-
lian Natives’ Association are considering a pro-
posal for adopting a political platform—still
retaining “God Save the Queen.”
It has just been discovered that the
shrapnell-shell supplied to the Australian military
departments by the dear old Mother Country is
jerry-built and often bursts at the gun-muzzle
even when propelled by a decreased charge of
powder.
“ Lorrie ” Harnett, the burly Sergeant-at-
Arma of the N.S.W. Legislative Assembly, visit-
ing Wagga lately, was introduced to the Riverine
Club as an hon. member, and was jocularly
addressed by his intimate friends as “Sergeant.”
Whereupon one member who knew not “Lorrie,”
expressed the opinion that it was “most derogatory
to the club, that a d d Sergeant of Police from
Narandera or elsewhere should be brought into our
place like this.”
Rev. Doctor Charles Strong, giving his
ideas regarding the amelioration of poverty in
Melbourne, before the Charities Commission,
favoured the erection of People’s Homes a la Pea-
body’s Buildings, at the expense of either the
municipalities or by private enterprise. He
opined that the majority of half-starved toilers
were being scandalously sweated by their land-
lords. This is altogether too attenuated. Lower
the rent-charge, and then the capitalist will
gracefully lower the wage-rate, and things will
there and then revert to the status quo ante, only
that the standard of comfort will have suffered a
sensible diminution. If Doctor Strong had advo-
cated a rise in the remuneration of labour, he
would have been worthy of some commendation,
but as it is, he has got hold of the wrong end of
the stick.
Dear Bulletin, —The book-fiend is bad
enough, but how about that terror to women and
children, the “Hindoo” hawker? While I was
absent from home one called; seeing no man
about, he told my wife that she must buy. She
had 17s. 3d., but his manner of speaking frightened
her so much that she spent all her money in rub-
bish : nor would he close his basket until she had
proved, by turning the purse inside-out, that she
had no more. Considering that the stabbing and
murdering capabilities of these cheeky niggers
have been so often demonstrated in this country,
it is no wonder they are so widely feared by
women. There is another side to the insolence of
these vagrants, to which I cannot specifically
allude. They must “clear” from Australia, as
they are in many ways worse than the Chinese ;
their present license is, I presume, accorded them
by reason of the fact that they are “our fellow-
citizens ” under the benign reign of the grasping
J. Bull.—E.B.
Harry’s Girl.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
One could see that her forehead bore traces of care,
And tears fell like rain on her cheek—
A cheek like a peach-blossom, blushing and fair,
Half hid in a tangle of golden-brown hair —
She had not done it up for a week,
For trouble had come most unpleasantly near,
The station was seized by the bank,
And Harry’d been super, at ninety a year,
Together, of course, with the “run of his shear,”
And a lease of the Bongabong tank.
How bright was the future, until there was cast
This shadow of doubt on their track.
How wearily, drearily, moments dragged past,
And she sighed as she said to her pillow at last:
“ Will those bankers give Harry the sack ? ”
How sad broke the morning, the dew-drops of
pearl
Were tears for the pair who must pack.
But, ah ! comes a horseman—her head’s in a whirl,
And she falls on his breast just the happiest girl,
For they’d not given Harry “ the sack.”
J.P.B.
The Land of the Crocodile.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Sir Thomas MHlwraith lives at a lovely
spot called “ Auchenflower,” a mile-and-a-half
from Brisbane on the Western railway. In the
days when the political life of Queensland was
embittered by the Steel Rails controversy, some
of the railway-guards referred—-among themselves
—to Auchenflower as the “ Steel Rails station.”
One evening a train was leaving the Brisbane
station. A member of Sir Thomas’s household
told the guard to “stop at Auchenflower.” In a
vacant moment the guard called out to the engine-
driver, in a voice heard all along the platform,
“ Stop at Steel Rails !” And the next mdrning
that guard received a polite but decisive intima-
tion that his valuable services were not absolutely
essential to the successful working of the
Queensland railways. And all the other guards
burned the word “ Auchenflower ” deeply into the
wooden tablets of their official memory.
* # #
Eighteen miles from Ipswich resides a
well-known settler, R. W. Le Grand, J.P., on
his estate of Wooyumboong. Le Grand is a
remarkable man to whom more extended refer-
ence will be made in a future chapter. At pre-
sent I have merely to mention that he has a
Teutonic neighbour whom we shall call Joseph
Coop. Coop came over to Le Grand to inform
him that his brother, who lived at Nijirk, in
America, had removed to Shakkeezahoo with all
his family. Their names brought Le Grand up
with a round turn. “ Veil! you knows not your
shography,” said Coop. But the brother's letter
being produced explained the conundrum by re-
vealing Nijirk and Shakkeezahoo as New York
and Chicago.
# * #
Another Teuton called in one day with a
wild look in his eye and a tomahawk in his right
hand. “ Goot mormng, Missa Le Grand ! De
beoples say you vas calls me von tarn schoundrel,
and never I likes it!” “ And what man dares to
say you are not ?” said Le Grand with a bland
smile. “ Ah, veil, ven you ’pologise like that,
never I minds it! Goot morning, Missa Le
Grand !” and he walked contentedly away with
the tomahawk.
# * *
Doubtless many Queensland students of
human nature have noticed, during the past five
years, the number of handsome women deliberately
sacrificed, or self-immolated, on the altar of Mam-
mon. The spectacle of Beauty and the Beast is
common enough anywhere on this planet, and
painfully conspicuous in Queensland simply by
reason of the small population. Every year
beautiful girls, urged by unscrupulous mothers or
their own overpowering vanity and ignoble ambi-
tion, are handed over to men old enough to be
their fathers, or to moneyed imbeciles or worthless
blackguards who possess wealth, or are the heirs
of wealthy fathers. These unhappy women are
doomed to a worse fate than those who slid down
the sloping arms of the Carthaginian Moloch into
the fiery furnace, or were given to the sea monster
to whom Andromeda was exposed to conciliate
Neptune. “We are creating,” says Carlyle, “an
aristocracy of the Money Bags; surely the basest
aristocracy yet known ! ” And yet, to join the
gaudy circle of soulless Mammon worshippers,
some of the fairest daughters of the people resign
themselves as wives to the embraces of notorious
fools, and equally notorious libertines! Is the
Australian mother who connives at these degrad-
ing sacrifices at all superior to the Indian matron
w r ho consigns her fairest daughter as a propitiatory
offering to the crocodile of the Ganges ?
# # #
Wherein, after all, lies the superiority—as
contra-distinguished from the status of the common
woman of the streets —of the moral condition of
the woman who sells herself, or is sold by her
parents, to some man or other, simply because he
happens to be rich ? It lies, dear friends, in this :
that the woman who sells herself for money to a
rich husband is sold wholesale, whereas the
“common” woman of the streets is sold retail.
Society regards wholesale trade as comparatively
respectable, whereas all retail businesses are low.
# # *
In one of the public offices up North is an
official who, under religious monomania, occa-
sionally believes himself to be the Saviour of
mankind. I remember seeing him some years ago
in the crowded gallery of the Legislative Council
during the delivery of the Governor’s Speech.
At the conclusion, in the midst of a solemn
silence, he exclaimed in a loud voice—“ Not a
single word about poor Jesus Christ!” Few people
know that this eccentric official has a handsome
and very estimable sister, who in her maiden
days was courted by a young man, who is now
one of the leading Australian statesmen. He
asked her to be his wife and she refused him, but
that is no reflection on anybody, as there are
many women who would refuse the best man in
this world. And ever since, that statesman’s
heart has been “darkened by her shadow,” and
the only consolation is the fact that he, at least,
escaped a brother-in-law who would have been a
permanent and unendurable calamity.
* # #
The next Queensland session will doubt-
less be one of the liveliest on record. If my
classical memory is correct, a gentleman named
Ephialtes was the solitary traitor at Ther-
mopylae. There are several worthy of that name
on the Ministerial Benches, but the Ministry are
are not at all anxious on their account. The fact
is that some of the Government supporters will
make any sacrifice to the dread of their con-
stituents and the fear of losing their £3OO per
annum. They will rise and make those saaly
prevalent political speeches embodying
The plain, ,lunt show of briefly-spoken seeming
Which hides the bloodless heart’s sole hardened
scheming ;
but when their seats and salary are in danger
they will descend from the serene heights of
cloud-capped bunkum, like kites that have lost
their tails, and fall all in a heap, with the usual
kerswosh ! on the Ministerial side of the House
during a party division. So that even if a general
election is anticipated this year by both sides
there is still a reasonable belief that it is not at all
likely to come off.
MAROOGALINE
A NEW REMEDY FOR IRISH DIFFI-
CULTIES.
A leading Dublin paper, having heard so much
said in both the Irish and English press in favor
of a wonderful oil, determined to investigate the
matter by personal interviews with people who
had used it, in order, to learn whether or not one
half that was said of its magical power was true,
or whether it was one of those gigantic advertising
schemes the magnitude of which in them modern
times fairly takes one’s breath away. Its represent-
ative interviewed a number of people in various
parts of Ireland who have been cured by the
application of the oil, and in every instance he
found that the published statements were true
and correct in every particular. Everywhere
people where enthusiastic in its praise.
A captain of the Royal Irish Fusiliers, who had
suffered long from rheumatism and sprained ankle,
said : “ The effect after three applications of St.
Jacobs Oil was simply marvellous; the pain at
once departed, and has never returned.”
A prominent hotel proprietor, after suffering
years from rheumatic gout, said : “It would be
hard to conceive of anything working a cure so
rapidly as St. Jacobs Oil did in my case.”
A sergeant of the Royal Irish Constabulary
told of a friend who had been a great sufferer for
years with rheumatism in the back, suffering
great pain. Using two bottles of St. Jacobs Oil,
the pain entirely disappeared, and never returned.
A young woman in Castlebar told the reporter
of the almost miraculous cure which her mother,
aged 70 years, had experienced from using the
invaluable St. Jacobs Oil. She had been almost
helpless for two years from rheumatism, and was
completely cured by using less than one bottle of
the oil.
A prominent surgeon in Tipperary related the
case of a parish priest who had for a long time
suffered the most intense agony from sciatica,
completely cured in three days by rubbing him-
self well with St. Jacobs Oil.
A very enthusiastic Irishman in Oort was met
with, shouting “Long live St. Jacobs Oil.” He
had suffered the most intense rheumatic pains for
years, and was cured by using the contents of one
bottle of the oil.
A cooper in Beale, County Kerry, Ireland,
related how his knee was twice its natural siz°,
stiff, and painful, and he was unable to stand ;
swelling and pain entirely disappeared after using
St. Jacobs Oil one week.
An old gentleman in Mallow, was cured in one
week after having commenced the use of St.
Jacobs Oil, of severe rheumatism of six years’
standing.
On returning to Dublin, the reporter called on
some of the leading chemists, for the purpose of
learning their views. Messrs. R. Simpson and
Co., of 16, Henry-street, the well-known chemists,
stated that St. Jacobs Oil was the most popular
remedy now being sold ; that it is no uncommon
thing for them to sell 1200 bottles of the oil in a
single week. That their last order was for 600
dozen, or 7200 bottles, and you can rest assured,
said Mr. Simpson, the senior partner, that no
medicine would have this enormous sale unless it
possessed actual merit.
PRACTICAL PHILANTHROPISTS.
THE blessings of peace and industry are
found in the advance of civilisation and
the development of resources—whether the latter
be of mental or mechanical evolution. They
who add to the conveniences of living, to the
education of the masses, accomplish something
more than a mere striving for riches —a fact
which cannot be gainsaid. Therefore the develop-
ment of an improved farming utensil is a bene-
faction to the community at large as well as a
source of profit to its originator. In connection
with this we may assume that any manufacture of
an article which tends to elevate the condition of
the masses bears the stamp of genuine merit, and
deserves attention other than that of mere com-
mercial interest. Accepting such reasoning for a
basis of fact, we believe none can gainsay the
claim of the Waterbury Watch Company to be
considered practical philanthropists, inasmuch as
by the production of an accurate, reliable and
timekeeping watch, at a price within the means
of every industrious person, they have already
educated large masses of people to a realising sense
of the value of time, and, besides, are daily and
hourly teaching youths and adults of both sexes
the necessity of punctuality, and the assurance of
possible and probable success in the future, when
they shall heed the passing moments, and utilise
them in accordance with the warning hands of
their faithful watches.

Female Suffrage: The Matter
Summed Up.
Dear Bulletin,— From the tendings of certain
social undercurrents it seems very probable that
before long an agitation will be inaugurated on a
large scale to allot to women similar political
rights to those at present possessed by men.
Dander these circumstances, therefore, it is as well
to consider whether, when the critical time comes,
such an extension of the voting power will be
advisable in the interests of humanity. Examin-
ing the relative proportions of the sexes, we find
that from time immemorial in all communities
there has existed approximately one man to one
woman, and that the percentage of males to
females in most populations is about equal.
That this-results from purely natural and not
artificial causes is obvious, and just as nature
oi’dains that the earth cannot bring into existence
more children than she can support, so will she
provide against any undue disproportion of the
two sexes. Assuming that the principle of One
Man One Vote obtain in all its purity, for every
male vote recorded there must accompany it
one female vote indirectly expressed through
the medium of. the male. Although man
has shown a decided inclination to legislate from
a class point of view, and to lay certain sections
of outside "classes under unfair disabilities, it
remains yet to be seen that he is willing to vote to
the prejudice of the opposite sex only, and it is
worthy of note that the wrongs from which the
females of the poorer classes suffer to-day are the
fruits of sinister operations directed not against
their sex alone, but against a whole class of people
comprising both males and females. A man is
not likely to vote against his own interests, and as
his comfort depends materially upon the happi-
ness of his indispensable female partner, it is cer-
tainly presumable that on all questions of general
policy the interests of both sexes will receive abso-
lutely mutual and impartial treatment. As men
must certainly have had mothers, and as the
majority possess, in addition, sisters, other female
blood relations, and, possibly, wives and
daughters, when a man goes to the poll to
record his vote it is hardly probable that
the known sentiments of his female connections
will not contribute largely towards fashioning his
political creeds, and, bearing in mind the powerful
influence which the • female exercises over the
male towards good or evil, the only apparent
danger in the present system of electoral dis-
pensation lies in the women having not too little
but too much social power.
* * #
Reviewing the few institutions in the Aus-
tralias whefein women have had the entire
administrative executive control it cannot be
declared that they have proved a remarkable
success! The patients at the women’s hospitals
complain that they have to put up with unneces-
sary humiliations and frivolous vexations which
would never occur in establishments directed by
men. As a servant, woman is facile princeps,
but as a master, she is a miserable
failure. No fault can be found with the
Victorian Civil Service employes as servants.
In fact, it is asserted that they are better clerks
than men would be. As that matter is not worth
arguing it may pass, the subject under enquiry
being the capacity of woman to conduct the
national affairs ab ovo ad infinitum. One of the
worst features about female education is the
sedulous cultivation of domestic hypocrisy. A
girl is taught to conceal her true feelings and
assume a mechanical puritanism which under-
mines her notions of what is true and false. As a
rule a woman will have a more sympathetic heart-
than man, but as her sympathy-wastes itself
chiefly on unworthy objects this advantage more
frequently than not proves rather a curse than a
blessing. To-day when priests, royalists and
other useless persons are on their final trial it
would hardly do to confer electoral privi-
leges on that sex by whose influence alone
the undesirable sections of society are per-
mitted to exist. The complexity of female
character renders her less efficient in action,
and the result of this is that her procedure is
invariably accompanied by tokens of irresolution,
vagary, and other signs of incapacity. An
emotionalist rather than a philosopher, an indivi-
dualist rather than an altruist, what else can be
expected but disaster if woman be granted com-
plete enfranchisement so long as her education be
conducted on the present ridiculous lines?
# * *
Physiologists assert that a marked deprecia-
tion is noticeable in the offspring of those farm-'
lies in which it has been the custom to educate
the females to the level of bluestockings. The
perfection of mental capacities in the mother is
gained at the cost of her physical attributes.
Further, it is very rarely that the brain virtues of
the mother will be reproduced in her offspring*
but her bodily vigour will invariably determine
the vigour of her children. With the father, the
case is diametrically the reverse. Accordingly, if
this be so, it is simple suicide to develop a high
standard of female education, since no possible
benefit can accrue to society thereby. A less con-
fined system of athleticism is what woman
requires, and men who are physically adapted for
the arduous exertions of politics or manual labour
or book-worming researches can be safely per-
mitted to discharge the external functions of
voting humanity, while woman internally directs
the show from her imperium in impeHo.
* * *
Surely no sane woman would desire for her
sex the privilege of competing with man on
the. hustings, in the halls of Parliament and of
Justice, in public offices, or in his everyday toil
on even terms ? When once the distinctive
features between the sexes are destroyed then
farewell to half of the salt of life. Woman's
world is not man’s world; they are separ-
ate kingdoms, which it would be nescient
folly to endeavour to assimilate. Half .the
attraction between the sexes lies in the op--
posing qualities possessed by each. Dissimi-
larity of characteristics proves often the strongest
bond of union between husband and wife. No one
would desire to see woman transformed into a
male female,” yet this is what apparently may
happen unless the present system of competitive
life gives place to co-operation, when women will
have no need to emulate the deeds of men to earn
a bare subsistence. For the present it is sufficient
that man should be the protector, and woman the
consoling angej, of daily life. By-and-bye, when
a healthier tone of female education develops a
more rational flow of thought* when the enjoy-
ment of material prosperity is secured to all under
a better system of social arrangements, then, with
a mind more virile and an organisation more effi-
ciently developed, it may safely be granted to
woman to perform her share at the ballot-box. As
matters stand to-day her claim to do so is open to
every objection.

The absconding ex-Town Clerk of Bathurst
gave himself up to Brisbane police last week.
He had shaven off his moustache and dyed his
hair, and might have remained free had he not
funked through becoming penniless and hungry.
The worst aspect of the case is that he has been
but a few months married.
Ex-Attorney-General Shiels, of Victoria,
who had been acting as emergency-man at the
Convention in Wrixon’s absence, discovered what
a huge tactical mistake he had made in not seizing
an opportunity to speak, when J. P. Abbott, on
Monday morning, insisted that he (S.) should
leave the Convention now that Wrixon had
arrived. It was urged on Shiels’ behalf that he
should be allowed to remain without voting, as he
was Premier Munro’s legal adviser and only
colleague, whereupon Abbott grimly retorted that
there were plenty of rooms in Parliament House
wherein Shiels could advise Munro. Exit Shiels.

The Bulletin wonders what Mr. Ardill,
the professional Sydney philanthropist, means to
do about the many charges made against him in
the columns of the press. No more direct chal-
lenges have, within our knowledge, ever been made
to any public man. Should Mr. Ardill fail to
take prompt measures to reply to the damning
accusations so openly levelled against him, it will,
we repeat, plainly be the duty of the public to
withdraw all support from the many institutions
over which he presides.
Parkes is out of it as the “draw ”, at the
Convention. He is billed as “ star,” but the real
star is Sir Geore-e Grey. When the ex-Knight of
Kawau spoke on Monday he was most flatteringly
received, and during his speech there was not a
sound save his voice to be heard. The delegates,
the public in the galleries, and the press alike
hung delightedly on every word, and S’r’nry was
undone. There is a big difference between the
utterances of Grey and Parkes —all the difference
that there is between cultured and soft-spoken yet
fervid and moving speech and flimsy, florid flum-
mery with the eccentricities of aspirate peculiar
to the Domain and the Queen’s statue—all the
difference that there is between a silver bell and a
kerosene-tin. Parkes is not the silver bell, And
the trouble with him just now is that he knows it.
