The “Great Pro-Consul."
SKETCHES FROM A LIFE OF ACTION.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
'V;
Sir George Grey had succumbed in the
struggle with the powers of Downing-street, but
An tarns-like sprang up the greater from his fall. It
was in defence of the people of New Zealand that
he had incurred the displeasure of the Crown and
aristocracy, and the vengeance taken by the
Colonial Office at once raised him to the highest
pinnacle of local popularity. Addresses were
showered upon him, and the people, as one man,
looked up to him as the champion of their rights
as members of a rising community. Other
Governors might be content to figure as puppets,
and to fill the part assigned by Bret Hart to
angels, by
“ Loafing around the Throne,”
but such a position was totally unsuited to Sir
George Grey’s character, and he was too manly
ever to have descended to the position.
Shortly after his recall, Sir George visited Eng-
land, and on his return to Kawau, devoted him-
self to literary pursuits. But in 1874 Air. Vogel’s
proposal to abolish the Provinces roused Sir
George to action. He looked upon the proposal
as equivalent to the destruction of the Constitu-
tion which he had taken so large a part in framing,
and was convinced that it would ultimately place
the land-legislation still rrore in the hands of
the land-grabbers and plutocrats. Under the
Provincial system, the land-laws of each province
were framed and administered by district govern--
ments, locally elected under a more liberal fran-
chise than that by which members of-the General
Assembly were returned. Moreover, this system
of local councils with semi-parliamentary powers
acted as a school for the instruction of the intelli-
gent in the knowledge of public affairs and
governmental duties, and had helped to make the
Maoriland Parliament itself a model for all Aus-
tralasian Governments.
Sir George, therefore, at once issued from his
retirement, and addressed an eloquent petition to
Mr. John Williamson, then Superintendent of the
Province of Auckland, urging that action should
be taken to prevent this wrong being done. This
petition was forwarded in due course to the Secre-
tary for the Colonies, Lord Carnarvon.
Mr. Williamson died soon after, and a deputa-
tion of Auckland electors at once waited on Sir
George Grey at Kawau, and begged him to allow
himself to be nominated as a candidate for the
Superintendency. He consented, and was elected
by acclamation, succeeding Mr. Williamson also
as. member for Auckland City West in the House
of Representatives.
Now commenced the grandest part of his career;
one which will fill the most brilliant pages of his
history when it comes to be written. It at once
became evident that a new power had entered the
political world. Possessing eloquence of the
highest order, he was capable of moving to burning
enthusiasm vast crowds, who hung upon his words
and followed him with the most implicit trust and
confidence.
Had the measure for the abolition of Provincial-
ism depended on outside opinion, its fate
was assured, for the mass of the people was
dead against it; but the House did not repre-
sent public opinion. It was elected for five years
under a limited franchise, and the districts were
so unequal that one member represented as many
as 1600 electors, while another represented 103
only—and so on.
In this unrepresentative House, Government,
Confident in its majority, succeeded in passing
the Bill, which became law in 1877.
Sir George now led an active agitation for par-
liamentary reform. Travelling all over the country
he axldressed in every centre crowded and en-
thusiastic meetings, and in these his glowing
oratory, the perfect independence of his position,
and his broad democratic sympathies gave him a
supreme hold over the people.
When Parliament met in 1877 after a dissolu-
tion, the effect of this campaign was at once
apparent, even under the blighting influence of a
restricted suffrage. Major (now Sir Harry)
Atkinson’s Ministry was defeated, and Sir George
Grey became Premier.
But, thanks to the constitution of the House,
the Premier’s majority was so small and so uncer-
tain that he could not carry any of the measures
of Reform for which he and his Ministry con-
tended. The landed party were also disgusted by
the imposition of a tax calculated on the unim-
proved value of land. And finally the rash and
irresponsible actions of Mr. Ballance, Colonial
Treasurer under the Grey administration, led to a
breach in the Ministerial ranks, Sir George being
at length compelled to insist on Mr. Ballance’s
withdrawal from the Ministry.
Under these troubles the Grey Ministry at
length succumbed. But though thus defeated as
a Minister, Grey continued as much a power as
ever, and even when not in office dragged the
Conservative party at his chariot wheels. His
successors in office found themselves compelled to
carry through all the measures of Parliamentary
Reform for which Sir George Grey continued as
vigorously as ever to contend. The duration of
Parliament was reduced from five years to three,
manhood suffrage was carried, electoral districts
were equalised ; and, finally, in 1889, Sir George
succeeded in carrying his great principle of One
Man One Vote — i.e.. that no elector is allowed to
have_ more than one vote at any Parliamentary
election. This carried, he retired from Parlia-
mentary life.
# # #
Sir George Grey also proved himself a
benefactor in other ways to at least two of the
nascent republics which he swayed as Governor.
At tire Cape ho founded a library which, in the
words of a leading English literary man in an
address on Libraries, given last year, would prove
a distinct loss to literature generally it by any
cause, such as fire, it should disappear. Not to
mention manuscripts, rare works, and first edi-
tions, this library contains the most valuable
collection of works on the philology of the
Southern Hemisphere in the whole world.
Sir George Grey, in talking of this Library
said : “ My idea was to get together all published
dictionaries, grammars, and Works in every
language and dialect of every country and Island
of the South Pacific, and, in fact, of the Southern
Hemisphere in order that students might there be
able to carry through the study of any language or
dialect that they might wish to acquire.
“ The British Museum was most anxious to
acquire this collection, and contended that it was
not fair that an out-of-the-way colony should
possess such a treasure. I offered to treat with
the Museum authorities for exchange of dupli-
catcd volumes in my collection for duplicated
volumes wanting in my library, and which they
possessed. They replied that an Act of Parlia-
rnent existed which prevented them from parting
with any volume which they had once acquired,
and there negotiations dropped.”
Sir George added that still greater displeasure
was manifested when it became known that the
most valued Dante manuscripts in the world,
containing notes and emendations in the great
poet’s own handwriting were in this library,
and it was actually proposed to get an Act
of Parliament passed with power to take one
of these manuscripts from the Cape and transfer
it to the British Museum ! “But,” said Sir
George, “better counsels prevailed, and the bill
was never introduced.”
Auckland, Maoriland, also, has been endowed
with a magnificent library, scarcely inferior to
that at the Cape, and also with a valuable gallery
of paintings, mostly by old masters; including a
Ratfaelle. Sir George Grey’s policy in respect to
these endowments may best be summed up in a
few words he let drop in connection with
some rare original manuscripts of the time of
Cromwell.
These consist of a treaty concluded by Richard
Cromwell in extension of one by his father with
the Hanseatic League, and a series of despatches
from Meadows, Secretary of the Council of State,
who was sent by Cromwell as ambassador to
Sweden in 1657 and 1658. Also a series of draft
reports in the hand of Thurloe.
Sir George said in respect of these : “ I was
told at the British Museum that there were none
of Meadows’ letters in the public records of the
United Kingdom, all having disappeared, and I
was earnestly pressed, on grounds of patriotism
and otherwise, to give these letters to swell the
Museum collection or the Record Office.
“I concluded, however, that there were in these
collections so many manuscripts of the highest
value; while documents of this nature were all but
unknown in the new countries springing into
nations under the impulse of British energy, that
it would be more equitable not to deprive New
Zealand of unpublished papers of such value.
“ Their influence might produce fruit, and call
into existence some historian in that new world at
the antipodes.”
We cannot close more appropriately than with
these words, which show the democratic bent of
tire Great Pro-Consul’s mind. From the time
that he was first called to rule over dependencies
of England, the one object of his rule was so to
direct the destinies of these dependencies as to
enable them worthily to take their place in history
as democratic nations.
In Liberalism, Maoriland now takes the lead ;
this is chiefly due to the noble example, bold
rule, and wise counsels of Sir George Grey.
The End.
In the happy Isle of Jersey, it has been
discovered that the libel law gives power to im-
prison a wife when her husband isn’t getatable.
A newspaper man there was ruined by the
damages in a libel suit, and as Jersey is an island
where they imprison a man indefinitely under
such conditions he wisely skipped. But the
aggrieved party hunted up an old statute bearing
pa the case, and cheerfully arrested the missing
publisher’s wife instead of himself. Jersey is a
nice place to federate with, taking it all round.
A Maoriland Idyll.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The curtains of night drop down
On valleys and forests still,
And a mist drives up o’er the plains
To the shepherd’s hut on the hill.
On the mighty Alps in the w r est
The moonbeams fall and glow—
Where the mountains pierce the cold, calm Heaver*
With spears of eternal snow.
Prom a crag on the mountain
X he shepherd looks on the night,
And longs that the angels may scatter
Some rays of soul-wanning light.
He can only bind his broken heart
With a hope of the Great To Be.
Por the shepherd’s angel Sweetheart sleeps
In a churchyard over the sea.
MAORI.
A. R. Fletcher Johnston, son of the late
Mr. Justice Johnston, of Christchurch, N.Z., has
succeeded the late William M‘Kinley, as law-
reporter for Melbourne Argus.
When Chinamen “peg out” at the Tas-
manian Tin Mines they are not interred European
fashion, but to save time and expense are simply
thrown out into the scrub like other refuse to
mingle their corruption with the usual surround-
ings of a Chinese camping-ground.
The lentcrfield (N.S.W.) poundkeeper is
a man of the strictest integrity. “Dooty afore
perks ” is his motto. The other day he found
his own cow astray, so he impounded her;
entered the fees against himself ; paid them over
to himself; made the impoundee shout for the
poundkeeper, and then entered the whole as
“ transaction closed.”
A Bathurst (N.S.W.) man trained to-
Sydney 3i dozen cabbages and 16 ducks, for sale 1
by auction. r lhe auctioneer’s “account sales’”
showed that after losing the value of the goods he-
(the consignor) had to pay 7s. 6d. out of his pocket;
to free himself from the hammer-man’s debt.
Commission, freight and other charges had ex-
ceeded the selling total by three half-crowns;.
After this, who will deny the N.Z. theory that
the transit question underlies the labour-question?
Dear Bulletin,- —The overpowering train
of thought in the “ handsome piece of Broken
Bill skite,” quoted by you the other day, recalls
the execration of the German Jew, who had been
taken in for half-a-crown by a fellow-dealer
in old clothes: “I wish I had a thousand
towns, and in every town a thousand streets, and
in every street a thousand houses, and in every
house a thousand rooms, and in every room a
thousand beds, and that in every one of these
beds you would have to lie a thousand years with
the most horrible toothache.” This metaphor
does not yet appear to be known in Broken Hill,
or it would, ere now, have been used to express
the local ideas about the permanency of the lode.
—Pinched Out.

CADBURY’S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-

of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds

One on the Jaw.
Dear Bulletin, —The chance notice of a para-
graph in one of the local papers the other day,
revived the memory of an incident nearly thirty
years old, of which 1 do not think any mention
has ever appeared in print. The paragraph merely
chronicled probate of the will of Elizabeth Bunt-
ing, widow, formerly an attendant at Kew Lunatic
Asylum, who died in November last, and it
attracted my attention by the desire expressed
in the will that her remains might be interred
in the grave of her late brother, John King
{who died in 1872), in the Melbourne Cemetery;
and for this purpose the sum of £5O was
■devoted by the will to cover the funeral ex-
penses. My story affords a quaint illustration
■of what may happen to a conscientious officer in
the discharge of his duty. John King was the
only survivor of that costly and ill-fated expedi-
tion led by Burks from Melbourne to the Gulf of
Carpentaria and back to Cooper’s Creek, where
Burke, Wills and Gray died of starvation in Aug.,
1861. King was rescued from amongst the blacks
six weeks afterwards by the party sent out under
Howitt, and the surveyor of that party, Edwin
J. Welch (whom you know) was sent down to
Melbourne in charge of King—who was in a very
weak state of health—from their depot camp at
Menindie, on the Darling River, with official
instruction “to proceed to Melbourne with all
available despatch, and hand J ohn King
over to the care of the Exploration Committee.”
There was great excitement in Melbourne over the
expected arrival, and when at last it was reported
that King had reached Woodend —then the ter-
minus of the railway—all the world and his wife
prepared to turn out and honour him with a re-
ception. A telegram was received from Welch
naming the hour of arrival, and the proper official
arrangements were made at Spencer-street railway
station, but the time passed and the train did not
come in. And then it was rumoured that
old Dr. Wills (father of one of the dead
explorers), acting on the instructions the
Governor, had stopped ■ the train at North
Melbourne station, and thence taken King home.
And the great large-hearted public howled its
discontent and tailed off in many directions, but
mostly to North Melbourne. I reached that
station just in time to hear Welch positively re-
fuse to give King up without a written order from
the Governor, or to allow him to leave the train at
that place. He said he had his instructions and
meant to sit there till they sent the train on. Old
Dr. Wills was in the carriage with Pond (of
Spiers and Pond), and Nash (Secretary for Rail-
ways) stood at the door arguing the point un-
availingly with Welch. The poor old Dr. was
under the combined influence of strong excite-
ment and something that was probably
red when he had last looked upon it.
Pond was engaged in the hopeless attempt
to get up a conversation with King, who
merely leant back in the corner of the carriage
and cried. “The line cannot be blocked any
longer,” said Nash; “the train must go cn to
Melbourne, and I shall send in a report to the
Minister.” “Report what you like, but send her
ahead,” came the reply ; and a few minutes after-
wards we drew up alongside the Spencer-street
platform, densely thronged with people, but all
arrangements tor the reception cancelled. How
they reached the old Government House in
William-street, packed on a “jingle,” through
that crowd has nothing to do with my story, but
John King and Welch were eventually safely
landed in one of the upstairs rooms and received
by the Gov.’s A.D.C., or secretary. King, in a
weak and fainting condition from over-excitement,
was placed on a sofa, and as a short interview
between Welch and Sir Henry Barkly was draw-
ing to a close a door on the opposite side of the
room opened, a tall, strongly-built and well-
developed female entered, followed by the old
Doctor in one of his most genial moods : —“That's
the man who refused to let me bring your brother
to you from North Melbourne, and if he dies you
will know whose fault it was.” “Are you the
man that had me brother in thethrain?” “I
am, madam, but— ” when a full-sized and
influential right-hander caught Welch on the
point of the jaw and upset him over
the end of the sofa. His Excellency
fled incontinently from the scene and dis-
appeared through the nearest door. Some
other official came in and helped to pick up the
debris, and the lady prostrated herself over the
partially inanimate body of her brother, and very
properly fainted. The excitement had run down.
Doctors Wilkie and Macadam arrived, and the
rescued man and his sister were driven to St.
Kilda by Sir William and Lady Don. Welch
used to say that he had to live on spoon-meat for
nearly a week after that meeting. Some of his
friends wanted him to summon the lady for
assault and subpoena the Governor as a witness,
but, whatever he may bo now, he was a loyal sub-
ject of the Crown in those days. Miss King after-
wards became Mrs. Bunting. If she hit Bunting
half as hard as she hit Welch, no wonder she was a
widow when she died.— An Old Melbourne
Fossil.
The Sorrows of a Sandwich Man.
His name was John Fosh ; his calling, sandwich-
man ; face grimy, eyes black and cavernous;
jaws lanterned, general appearance gaunt; frame
shivering with two coats on his back, afid a bundle
of something—shirts call it. Fosh was an old
soldier, and has had five years of odd jobbing—
window-cleaner, boardman, match-vendor, hawker,
and docker. He sticks to the boards when he can
get them. Fosh was dining on air and washing it
down with a lump of ice when I met him in the
Dark Arches formed by Charing Cross railway-
bridge. This is one of the favourite dining-rooms
of boardmen. If they have a herring and a chunk
of bread they bring it here. If- they have nothing,
like Fosh, they bring it here just the same, if
they have a penny to spare they buy a penn’orth
of pea-soup at the kitchen in Ham-yard. So I
understood Fosh to say.
“But, surely, Fosh, you have a penny over from
the day before ?”
“Not always. How is it when you don’t get no
boards for two days ? Where’s yer penny then ?
Where, indeed ?”
“ So some of you were run in the other day for
trespassing on the kerbstone ? ”
“ That’s so. And I’ve heard ’em say as they’ll
do away with us altogether. They told us this
morning that they’d pay no more fines.”
“ What does the law say ? ”
“The boardman must keep in the gutter.”
“ Then why don’t you ? ”
“ Why, the ’ansoras haims at us. They thinks
our toes is targits,” said Fosh grimly.
“ Then you take to the kerbstones ? ”
“ Ay, w'e takes to the kerbstones, and then the
pleeceman ’e comes up and warns us. And the
swells complain, and well, it’s the last job a
chap’ll take to.”
“ The last hope.”
“Ay. The very last.”
“ What pay do you get, Fosh ? ”
“ One-and-tuppence a day, from !) till 5.20.
And lucky them as gets a pair o’ boards. There’s
always a tight for ’em. I’ll tell you what it is.
There’s too many of us in this ’ere world.”
“ Well, how do you spend your oue-and-two-
pence?”
“Fourpence for a doss! Salvation Army,
Horseferry-road, a coffin bed and a leather blan-
ket ; but it’s warm enough, the room is, with
steam-pipes. At seven at night you goes in and
gets some coffee and a bit of bread. When you
goes out at seven in the morning you gets some
more coffee and a bit more bread. Them and the
doss is fourpence—and very good for the money.”
“ That’s only fourpence.”
“After we gets paid at five-thirty I goes to a
shop I knows and buys two-penn’orth of cooked
meat scraps. I take these to the shelter. That’s
supper. It’s not much of a sum. That’s six-
pence. Then there’s a penn’orth of pea-soup at
Ham-yard. They give you a good penn’orth there.
Sevenpence. Then there’s clothes, and a bit of
baccy runs away with some of the rest, and a bit
for a day when boards is scarce.”
“ About clothes, Fosh. How much for the outer
coat ?”
“A penny.”
“ Only a penny !” It was not half a bad gar-
ment.
“ I bought it from a mate. He had six of ’em.”
“The undercoat, Fosh ; how much ?”
“Threepence.”
“ And your shoes ?”
“Odd ones. Picked up in a barrow. Penny
the right. Twopence the left.”
“ Some of you go to the lodging-houses? ”
“That’s so. They charge fourpence there,
without bread and coffee. I don’t run to that.
Some goes to Limehouse. It’s only threepence
there
“What? They walk from the West-end to
Limehouse night and morning to save a penny ? ”
“ That’s so. To save a penny.”
“ No boards on Sundays ? ”
“ I sells matches. Buys ’em at sixpence a
dozen. Sells ’em at a shilling. Made eightpence
on Sunday.”
“ A bad winter this has been, Fosh! Why,
man, you’re crying.” . . A tear or two fell on
Fosh’s coat, and were icicles before I had turned
him inside out. No wonder Fosh found it cold
in his sixpenny suit. “ Tell you what it is, sir,”
picking off the frozen tear with his dirty finger-
nails—he was evidently ashamed of it—“ tell you
what it is, life’s like this ’ere weather. It’s a
frost.”
“ You’re in a bad way, Fosh. What are you by
trade ?”
“ Fourteen years in the army. Out in India all
the time. Invalided home. Never done no good
since.”
“ You’ve been five years trying to get work ?”
“ Five years. Fights for boards ; nights in the
open ; casual wards ; haymaking and hopping in
the summer; hawking; matches. I can’t go
glimming, I ain’t the cheek.”
“ Glimming?”
“Opening kerridge-doors, guvnor.”
Fosh is right.—“ There’s too many of us in this
’ere world. Life’s a frost.”
* * *
Fosh says there are thousands like him. —Pall
Mall.
In Defence of Jamieson and Cannibalism.
Dhau Bulletin, —As much opprobrium is heaped
on the memory of the late Mr. Jamieson, of
Stanley’s expedition, I should like to say a few
words in his defence. Cannibalism, in some parts
of Africa, is not a crime—it is merely a custom
rendered respectable by antiquity, and is quite as
capable of being defended, on humane grounds, as
many of our own laws and customs. For instance,
a countrywoman of Jamieson’s, named O’Brien,
was the other day at Sydney sentenced to
six months’ imprisonment for attempting to
commit suicide. Anyone who can see the justice of
such a sentence, and calmly acquiesce in the
carrying-out of so barbarous a law, need express
no indignation at Jamieson’s conduct while
amongst the cannibals. In patronising one of
their feasts, Jamieson did not offend the laws,
customs or sensibilities of the people with whom
he was then sojourning, and was probably actu-
ated by the purest motives in acting as he did.
The girl who was eaten was a captive from
another tribe, evidently retained as an article
of diet, to be used whenever other rations
ran short, and she, being conversant with
the customs of the country, probably viewed
herself in the same light, and was possibly grate-
ful to Jamieson for hurrying up her fate and
ending the state of suspense in which she must
have lived. There is no evidence to show that
Jamieson’s presence on the scene caused the
maiden any pain or embarrassment; she may have
often picked a spare-rib herself, and only regretted
her inability to attend the feast as a guest, instead
of as the piece de resistance. Jamieson, doubtless,
imagined his sketches would be received with
acclamation in England, showing, as they did,
a simple and inexpensive method of utilising sur-
plus population.
* * *
It may be urged by some folks that instead of
encouraging the cannibals to continue their usual
customs in his presence, Jamieson should have
explained to them the beauties of Christianity
and civilisation as practised in his own country :
he might have told them how the upper classes in
civilised communities manage to live on the lower
orders without actually eating them, and how
much more real enjoyment and luxury may be got
out of a girl by working her 15 hours a day in
field or factory during the time she is capable
of such exertion, and then leaving her to
die of starvation afterwards, than by their
primitive method of eating her while’ young.
Of course this system might, at first, appear too
hard on the victims, to commend itself to the
untutored cannibal minds. But, then, Jamieson
could have explained, that use would soon deaden
their sensibilities, and render them callous to the
sufferings, groans and sighs of the procurers of their
enjoyments, and that they would then be able to
carry on frivolous, expensive amusements quite un-
disturbed, although surrounded by victims expiring
under excruciating tortures and weighty burdens.
Mr. Jamieson may therefore have kept silence,
conscientiously believing their system superior—•
on humane grounds—to that prevailing in Britain.
There is a simplicity about the way in which these
Africans go for the object they have in view, that
arouses sympathy and compels us to admit that,
at least in one respect, they show a marked
superiority oyer us. There is no damned hypocrisy
about a cannibal.—Yours, etc.,
J. M.

How To Make Money.
A GREAT FUTURE INDUSTRY.
In the present day, when money-making, or the
question of how to obtain a comfortable living, is
the chief subject for .consideration witli most
people, all are on the alert to “ pick up a wrinkle ”
(to use the low vernacular) on this important
point. Many and various.are the schemes devised
for converting a shilling into half-a-crown, or, if
possible, half-a-sovereign, and with the great
majority the aim is to do so with the least amount
of risk and trouble. But experience has shown
that in the long run, perhaps, the soil, under a
proper system of treatment, affords the best
results in this direction, and, taken as a whole,
there are no more contented body of men than
the yeomen farmers. It is not our purpose, how-
ever, to descant here on the advantages of agri-
culture in the abstract, but to I'efcr to one branch
in particular of this class of industry, which, for
several reasons, has not, up to the present,
attained great dimensions in N.S.W. —viz., fruit-
culture. Curiously enough, with all the advan-
tages of our excellent climate and soil, the art of
fruit-growing has not established itself to any
extent in our midst, and by those who take an
interest in the matter diverse reasons are given as
the cause of this manifest want. Many people,
with a good deal of truth, assert that the best of
the land suited for fruit-growing is in the hands
of the large owners, while others again expatiate
upon the want of irrigation, and so on ad lib.
But it is a facI', 1 ', whether or no, that at present i
large quantities of fruit are imported which should
be produced in the colony. Beyond our orange
and vine culture, it may be said, indeed, as a
general thing, there is no systematic fruit-growing
in N.S.W. Without a doubt there are two
essentials to success in this connection, and
these are —adaptability of soil, and sufficient
moisture. To meet these requirements, and with
a view to fostering an industry which at present
languishes, a praiseworthy effort is being made by
the Land Company of Australasia that should
commend itself to all who take an interest in the
matter. The important discovery has been made
by the managing directors, of whom two—Messrs.
Edwards and Harris—may be considered experts,
that the soil on tho Ravensworth Estate, the
property of the Company, is in great
measure eminently suited for arboriculture,
and they have determined to give the
public the advantage of the fact. Inves-
tigation has proved that about 10,000 acres
of the estate, which is level country of a rich,
sandy loam, is irrigated by nature to such an
extent that without any artificial aid it contains
all desiderata for successful fruit-growing. The
laud is intersected by a network of streams, but
in addition to this water supply it also has been
found that below tho soil, which ranges in thick-
ness from Gft. to 10ft., there is an underlay of
shingly pebble and bluestonc boulders. The
effect of this formation is that the shingle bed acts
as a kind of subterraneous course for the water
which rises iu springs on the adjacent mountains,
and throughout the year there is a constant trick-
ling flow beneath the surface of fresh, pure liquid.
The result is that the soil, being of a sandy nature,
sucks up the moisture towards the roots, and even
iu the most tropical heat, w hen verdure elsewhere
is parched and limp, trees which have been
planted here are fresh and vigorous, and'they
grow with remarkable rapidity. Orchards
were planted to test the truth of tins
idea, immediately upon the fact becoming
knowui to the directors, and it is now establi- bed
beyond all question. An orchard of stone-fruit
trees which was planted seven years ago has given
ocular demonstration of tho fact, for although the
trees were planted 20 feet apart, the branches now
are touching each other, and the yields are very
large.
To enable would-be orchardists to obtain the
advantages of settlement on good soil, the directors
of the company have decided to cut up the more
suitable portions of the estate in sections and on
terms which should have the effe t of establishing
there a large and prosperous fruit-growing settle-
ment. The land will be subdivided into sections
of from 20 to 100 acres, and to assist selectois
cottages will be erected, and the preliminary steps
taken to make tho soil reproductive at the earliest
possible moment after selection. Large quantities
of cheap English money are now coming into the
coffers of the company, and it is proposed to
utilise a portion of this in the preparation of tho
estate for settlement. Already sonic orchards
have been planted and re sold, and it is at present
under the consideration of the Board to
lay out 500 acres in orchards, iu arcai to
suit large and small purchasers. One of tho
orchards which was purchased from the Company
originally, it may be mentioned, has recently been
re-sold, at a profit of several hundreds of pounds.
The advantage of the system proposed by the
Company is that selectors will find the land in
such a condition that they can almost immediately
obtain a profit, and experience lias shown that
where fruit can be grown successfully the profits
arc really very handsome, while the value of tho
property is increasing daily with the growth of
trees. Cases are plentiful m the colony where,
from six acres of orchard, no less than £5O an
acre per annum has been netted after paying all
expenses, and it is computed that < ns man alone,
can, with proper appliances, keep in good order
no less than 40 acres of land. Naturally, care is
required in the selection of the fruits to be grown,
but the experience of the directors enables them
to plant those trees which are most likely to
thrive in the different localities, and return a
good profit. It is worthy of note that the fruit
produced up to the present on the estate has been
disposed of almost entirely in the country districts,
and at prices higher than ruled at the time in
Sydney, thus proving that the metropolitan
market is not alone to be depended upon by
growers. As an additional impetus to the indus-
try, it is also proposed to erect on the estate
fruit-preserving establishments on co-operative
principles, by which means the settlers may not
only secure a market for their produce, but also
obtain a profit upon the manufacture of jams, &c.
The funds for this enterprise will be provided by
tho company, to bo afterwards repaid by the set-
tlers, who would thus obtain the whcMe thing in
their own hands. It is believed by experts that
the Ravensworth estate will ere long prove to be
the finest natural fruit-producing country in the
colony, and there is every reason to anticipate
that its future will be one of great prosperity.
The estate itself, which comprises G 5,000 acres, is
splendidly situated as regards the means of transit
either to Sydney or the various inland settlements.
It is adjacent to the main Northern line, 17 miles
beyond Singleton, and is 58 miles from New-
castle and 158 miles from Sydney. The estate,
which has a station of its own, has a railway
frontage of 16 miles, and every facility is thus
offered to intending selectors. Already over
5000 acres have been taken up, and applications
are being received for sections, which goes to
prove that the advantages offered by the company
are fully appreciated by those who are the best
able to value them.

Footprints in the Sand.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some human footprints have been discovered on
the sandstone formation iu a quarry near the
coast. They are about GO feet below the surface,
ami are apparently of very ancient date. —Daily
Paper. We rise to explain how these footprints
got there :
’i'was a dreary night by a dreary shore,
And slowly the murky tide did (low
With a dismal wail, and a washy roar,
Some fitty-five-huudred years ago;
And the winds flew by with a dolorous hum,
Like a worn-out harp or a muffled drum.
The skies were as dark as a hidden sin
O’er the dreamer who pondered there alone,
And sadly the inky seas rolled in.
As he sat, and cursed in an undertone;
Ho was an editor wild and thin,
And he grasped in his hand The Bulletin,
And he mused in hieroglyphics there,
For language wasn’f invented yet,
And so a citizen had to swear
In a sort of cuneiform alphabet ;
And when he couldn’t help but damn,
lie did it by means of a diagram.
For things were bad in that far-off day
About thirty-five hundred years 8.G.,
And the naked subscriber wouldn’t pay,
But always wanted his paper free ;
And a hairy contributor, long and thin,
Raised Cain when his paragraph didn’t go in.
And be thought of the undressed C.M.G.,
Who put on airs at that ancient date,
And passed resolutions beneath a tree,
A.nd grovelled, and wanted to “ federate,”
And who sat on his tail with a bilious eye
When a common democrat passed him by.
And the Primrose League that met once a week,
When the members clawed at each other’s hide,
In a slushy location beside a creek ;
And other troubles there were beside,
Including the unshod office bard
Who was tight in a barrel in the yard.
The primitive artist was on the spree
(A sort of old, aboriginal “ Hop.’ ),
He had eaten his uncle that day at three,
And put the dead relative’s boots in pop.
And a libel suit had been started too,
By an “eminent citizen ” painted blue.
And a “loyal subjecthad called that day
With a howl and a club, and said that he
Wanted the journal stopped right away,
Because of its awful disloyalty ;
And a pious man, who was feeling bad,
Had brained the printer, and withdrawn his “ad.’
And he thought of a certain man of fame,
Who—as paper wasn’t invented then,
Though finance was carried on just the same—
Had passed on him a stone P.N.,
An old red sandstone I O U,
Which was now considerably overdue.
Then, as the slow, dull tide rolled in,
With a dismal wail, and a hollow roar,
He dreamt that a spook with a mirthless grin
Was passing a plate upon the shore
And asking him to contribute
To an Himperial Hinstitoot.
And a spectral form came clad in bones,
And the tale of an ’Orrid Deed it read,
With details concerning the Hollow Groans
Of the victim, and how they smashed his head ;
(The crime, it seemed, was caused by booze
And the facts were all in the Evening News).
The voice of the birds in the sunless air
Seemed to yell forth a viewless cablegram,
Which stated that Battenberg’s “innards ” were
Inflamed, through eating unhealthy liarn.
And the world was “Pre-eminent in Grief”
Because of Bat.’s undigested beef.
Then the howl of some beast by a distant stream
Made him think, with a boding of awful woe,
That lie beard Parkes borrowing in a dream
About fifty-five hundred years ago,
Or else that unborn apost’c of light
Was kissing his Stella in the night.
Si the editor woke ’neath the murky sky,
Witli his knees supporting his weary head,
And his hat jammed down o’er his weary eye,
And asked why the world wasn’t all born dead;
And the footprints they found the other clay
Were his tracks, as he mournfully walked away.
TITUS SALT.
Some of the Queensland Mounted In-
fantry, stationed at Clermont, were sent to “ an
old hotel, very dirty, and kept by Chinese.”
They “ marched back to the stables where they
wore before victualled,” and started cooking for
themselves. They had evidently read The Bul-
letin’s articles on “Shduffing,” and declined
Chinese chew-mash. Nevertheless, The Bulletin
thinks “Shduffing” quite good enough for
creatures who hire themselves to be the guardian-
angels of blacklegs.
Dear Bulletin, — Your ‘’Half-Duck’
yarn in a recent issue recalls a somewhat similar
story. A Christmas party given by the “cove”
on a certain back-blocks station was attended by a
fine specimen of the young Australian “bushy,”
who, while all there at a muster, was quite at sea
in a ball-room. Noticing his backwardness, a
sprightly young lady from a neighbouring run
took a kindly interest in him, and even succeeded
in getting him to scramble through a quad-
rille. He fell desperately in love with her at
once, and the hostess kept up her end of the fun
so well that the cornstalk felt ho might make a
capture if ho nlaycd his cards cautiously. On
leaving for home the lady forgot her dancing-
shoes, and the hostess asked the young Lochinyar
from the plains if he would oblige by returning
them. “ Will a duck swim ?” was the reply, as the
delighted youth sprang on his horse and flew to
the home of his soul’s adored. The lady was sur-
prised to see him, but tendered a friendly
greeting, and then her enraptured admirer
blurted out; “ You forgot yer darncin’-shoes last
night, Miss, so I’ve fetched you the parcel.” His
enslaver thanked him with a smile that nearly
lifted him out of his knee-boots, but, on opening
the package, exclaimed: “ Why, there’s only one
shoe here!” “Yes,” stammered the blushing
“ bushy,” “ but I’ll bring yer th’ other one round
tomorrer; an’ look ’ere, Miss (most passionately),
“I wish to Gord yoii was a santipede, so as I
could bring you a shoe every day for the next six
months !”
His Godlike Raizon.
For all that Dan. O'Connor says,
Unth in and out of saizon,
Yo'll bet your bothom dollar, plaze,
He has “ a Godlike raizon.”
Ho loves our Royal Ladee’s name,
And won’t encourage traizon ;
He’s Oirisli, too, but all the same
Ho has a Godlike raizon.
A platform every blissed noight
His figure he displays on;
He worships girls wid all his moight—
He has a Godlike raizon.
Begorra ! wunst wp heard him shpake
About “the bed he lays on ; ”
Although he made a small mistake,
He had a Godlike raizon.
And when a filthy Dying Fowl
He didn’t fear to saize on,
It wasn’t law, but, by our sowl !
He had a Godlike raizon.
The most obtrusive characteristic of Mel-
bourne “authorities,” Civic or Governmental, is a
disposition to assume that no other city in the
world suffers from such fearful congestion of traffic,
extraordinary difficulties in general manage neut,
or so many petty matters requiring to be put
down, or put right by a bumptious bye-law. In
the malodorous retreats set apart for “ gentle-
men only,” at railway-s’tations, smoking is strictly
prohibited. The street cry of “MiUc Of” is a
punishable offence, and the ringing of a muffin-
bell is treason-felony, or something worse. The
peripatetic “sandwich-man” is against the rules,
and vehicles displaying advertisements are con-
trary to the ubiquitous regulations—except at
Municipal and Parliamentary election times, when
every cab in the land is pasted over with invita-
tions to vote for some Blowhard or Puffy who
helps to make the said regulations. Bullet-
headed policemen are specially encouraged to use
the “move on” clause for the annoyance of the
respectable portion of the community, whilst
street “ bookmakers ” and the dangerous classes
in general are left in peace to stand and spit upon
the weary wayfarer. There is no sense or system
in the bossing which Melbourne (not to mention
other Australian cities) folks meekly accept as
part of their birthright, nor is the iden-
tity of the power which suppresses a lot
of things — smoking under the piazzas of
the G.P.O., for example—quite clearly nnder-
standed of the people. Many prohibitions, ema-
nating from nowhere in particular, have been
casually painted on a board at an unknown
period and allowed to gradually establish prece-
dents. Consequently it is incumbent on the man
who walks abroad to think seriously and often of
the many things he mustn’t do in a public place,
and if his attention wander from that comprehen-
sive subject for ten minutes by the clock, he finds
himself ruthlessly grabbed for “insulting be-
haviour ” and knocked silly by a brand-new bye-
law. Perhaps the reason why Melbourne endures
all this oppression in silence is that the vanity of
its insanitary residents is tickled by the thought-
that it would be impossible to run such a densely-
populated region on the free-and-easy system
which is good enough for pettifogging places like
London, The rising generation, in particular, is
rapidly absorbing this idea, and if you drop
hints "to a high-spirited young native that the
Great Metropolis doesn’t mean the “Queen City
of the South,” he wants to fight it out to
a finish instead of inferring the matter to
arbitration. For this premature development
of patriotism the local “Radical” press is
accountable. The Herald makes a practice
of dosing its readers with instances of how’
“we are far ahead of London,” from the pens of
ardent enthusiasts who were born in Carlton, and
have seldom journeyed beyond sniffing distance
of the Yarra. Every alleged visitor of distinction
is requested to tell a Herald interviewer how
astounded he was at the enormousness of the
crowds, the turmoil of business, the unprecedented
“push” prevailing in “Our Wonderful City.”
To which the distinguished individual replies
that be was astounded, and bis condition of
mental bewilderment is promptly reported in
large capital. And now the Age has the cool
audacity, or gross ignorance, to say that “the
annoyance occasioned by pedestrians, who insist
on walking on the wrong side of the pathway, is
permitted here to an extent that would not be
tolerated in any first-class city of Europe or
America.” Does the Age mean to assert that
there are “ regulations ” for foot-passengers along
Cheapside ? There people suit their own
convenience in walking, as they do on Broad-
way, and their own convenience teaches
them to follow the stream of humanity, going
either side of the pavement. Nobody issues
orders or passes laws on the subject. If a man
attempts to push Ins way against either stream he
gets tired, or somebody else shoves him over, and
things go on proceeding’ as usual. The pedes-
trians of Marvellous Melbourne, however, are
supposed to need a special Act of Parliament to
control their lines of march. It is disgusting to
find a newspaper professing Liberalism in one
article, and advocating a further extension of
trumpery official interference in another. The
will of the people—be., of the majority—estab-
lishes a rule of the footway immediately a rule
becomes necessary, and when the Age speaks of
many towns having “regulators of the traffic,
whose sole function is to remind offending pro-
menadera ” where they ought to walk, that widely-
circulated publication is babbling in its sleep.
Nevertheless, the babble breathes the very spirit
of that peculiar stupidity (stupidity born of
vainglory in their fever-tainted collection of tall
building's and beastly back-yards) which makes the
many of Smellbourne glory in being overgoverned
by the few who are always trying to fossick out
something fresh to suppress. This qhildish belief
in the necessity for special traffic arrangements
on account of the city’s “ phenomenal increase of
population,” and so forth, seems to be growing on
the people, and will continue to grow whilst a
swarm of local mayors and several local journals
(the Argus being a decent exception) are forever
feeding it. One of these days the intelligent
foreigner will be flabbergasted by notices inform-
ing him that he mustn’t stop to talk, or think, or
‘ scratch his head in any of the principal streets
during business hours. And to these notices,
hanging from corner lamp-posts, the down trodden
Melbournian, bis face all aglow with exultation,
will point and ask—“Ain’t we going ahead at a
tremendous rate ?”
A Queensland Sanitorium.
There is many a beautiful site for a mansion
among the ranges at Toowoomba, and doubtless
the next ten years will see the landscape dotted
with fair residences, but there are few spots in
the world, search where you will, that surpass in
beauty that of the Blue Mountain Hotel, which is
fast becoming well-known over the length and
breadth of Australia, and which will ere long
claim and receive first place among health resorts.
To the visitor from the capital the climate of
Toowoomba is a pleasant revelation, but a stilb
further surprise awaits him if he wends his way
furthet into the mountains and cries excelsior,
for the Blue Mountain hostelry, though only
some four miles from Toowoomba, stands fully 500
feet higher than the City of the Range, and nearly
3000 feet above the level of the sea. Along a well-
'kept highway we spin merrily, catching here
and there a glimpse of many a pretty, quiet
scene in marked contrast to the wild natural
beauty of the hills and glens we are approaching.
Another mile or so and we reach the confines of
Mr. Holmes’ property (the Blue Mountain Estate)
and then turning sharply to the right, wh
descend a gentle declivity and find ourselves at
our destination, until now hidden under the brow
of the hill. Here the fresh free breeze of the
mountains catches us, and but little imagination
would make us fancy that wo were again in other
Highlands far away from these in which we find
burselves at Mr. "Holmes’ establishment, where
mine host on hospitable thoughts intent, awaits
to escort us through what is certainly one of
the most comfortable and best appointed hotels in
the colony. Of the size of the house one can form
no conception until a peregrination of the entire
building has been made, for it is built as it were
in sections, and thus presents that pleasant irregu-
larity dear to lovers of the homely and picturesque,
and possessing many a cosy nook, and sheltered
corner where loungers most do congregate. The
building into which our host leads us and which
contains the bar, billiard-room, dining-room,
reading-room, bacheldrs’-room —where is held
many a tobacco Parliament - and a host of rooms,
is that last erected, and a spacious establishment
does it appear as we glance down the long vista of
106 feet of corridor. .Further on is the older
part of the building which was originally Mr.
Holmes’ private residence. In all there are some
45 rooms, exclusive of public rooms, of which there
is ample accommodation. Throughout the entire
hotel, from the bar to the billiard-room, in library
and dining-room, in drawing-room and private
sitting room, the same good taste and the same
care for the comfort of the visitors is displayed,
and it would be difficult to find more luxuriously-
appointed rooms anywhere. It is evident that
the proprietor has spared neither trouble nor
expense in a praiseworthy .effort to please his
patrons, and we are sure that everyone who has
resided at the Blue Mountain Hotel will agree
with us in saying that he has succeeded. The
proprietor of the Blue Mountain Hotel has
objects in view which cannot possibly bring him
into collision with any other business man. He
caters for holidays-seekers, he finds his clients
among those who, leaving one town, do not seek
to find change of air in another, preferring the
seclusion and beauty of the Range resort. We
think these considerations should free the. pro-
prietor from all rivalry and insure him the good
offices of all All the time we have been spinning
this long dissertation our host has been standing
ready to usher us into a beautifully-appointed
parlour where everything is in the best of taste.
This, it seems, is the sacred oratory of Hymen.
Here honeymooners come to spend the interlude
in the play of life, the epoch in man’s history at
which bachelors scoff, ancient Benedicts sigh, and
of which the honeymooners themselves pretend
more or less successfully to be profoundly ignorant.
We suppose that the honeymoon is a time-honored
custo*i that cannot bo gainsaid or set aside, and
this being so—if young folks will marry—by all
means let them betake themselves under Mr.
Holmes’ hospitable roof. And now we seek the
front verandah facing the vast expanse of the
ranges. There down below in the hollow twists
the railway, like two gigantic twin snakes until it
creeps round a corner to re-appear again half a mile
further on, ultimately disappearing into a tunnel.
Down below in front and as far as the eye can
stretch lies the expanse of the Main Range, a
huge verdant plain ploughed up by nature, and
in which the furrows arc waving tree-tops. Here
and there a cloud obscures the dark-blue of the
sky, and instantly is it photographed on the
waving green-tinted and cverchanging foliage
beneath. Suddenly a cloud sweeps into view
and we see the tree-tops darken as though a giant
brush had changed their tints ; again the cloud
sweeps on and the shadow vanishes only to play
the truant artist in another place. Ear in the
distance is a great peak called Spicer's, and near it
a huge cleft which our host tells us is Cunning-
ham’s Gap, full of sad memories for those who
seek the via recta ; and the wonderful clearness of
the atmosphere is demonstrated by the ease with
which the large opposing range is scanned, for we
are told that the Gap is fully sixty miles distant.
No wonder those who have been here once come
back, for greater pleasure for those in whom
the artistic and poetic faculties are present could
not be imagined than to watch Nature in her most
beautiful aspect as seen from the corner of the
Range, and whether the visitor be on pleasure or
health intent he cannot resist the charm of the
climate, which has a strangely exhilarating effect.

A Lay of the Lash.
(FROM HAMILTON GAOL.)
We sing of Oorney Bourke, who was an insult to
the Law,
And a very vulgar moral from his story we will
draw ;
We cannot say for certain when his wickedness
began,
But it’s evident that Corney is a dreadful sort of
man.
With a taste for ardent spirits, as a young ’un he
was curs’d,
And he liked to beat his mother, very possibly, at
first;
He wouldn’t learn his lessons when a chubby,
growing lad
(We are taking it for granted ho was always pretty
bad).
He may have been an angel in his early salad
days,
Who talked of love and honour, like the gentlemen
in plays ;
Perhaps a fickle sweetheart set our Corney going
wrong—
Though it pleases us to fancy he was awful all
along.
But, anyway, no matter ; he took long ago to
crime,
When he wasn’t doing mischief he was mostly
“ doing time
That didn’t make him better, which is altogether
odd,
For there’s nothing so improving as a year or two
in “ cjuod.”
He became an “old offender,” and the name of
Corney Bourke
Was very much against him if he wanted honest
work ;
The “ peelers ” had to watch him when he chanced
to be at large,
And they never left off watching till they had him
back in charge.
There’s a law of evolution which must govern such
a case,
And a rising scale of charges for the prison human
race,
Thus the infamy of Corney evoluted more and
more,
And his newest crime was “ wusser ” than the one
he did before.
So it naturally happened that a learned judge
(salute !),
Who would scorn to give a flogging to a mean
four-1 eggei brute.
Endeavoured to put Corney on a higher moral
tack,
Through the grace of twenty lashes on his cold,
uncovered back.
A warder basted Corney till he hadn’t strength to
x move,
Then they put him, bleeding, cursing, on a pallet,
to improve ;
And the legal lesson taught him had the usual
effect
Of increasing Oorney’s virtue, and his proper self-
respect. _
But when his term was ended and from prison he
was sent,
The beast had quite forgotten that he wanted to
repent,
And the hymn, unohastened Corney used at even-
tide to sing
Was —“Now Iye been spread-eagled, I suppose I
oughter swing.”
His nature grew susceptible to changes in the
wind,
Which gave him nasty twinges where his body had
been skinned,
And ho felt a horrid impulse to go forth upon tin
basli,
Each time he was reminded of the “swishing ” of
the lash.
It came to pass in prison, in a good old dungeon-
cell,
That Corney had a comrade, and he thought he’d
give him ’ell;
The other chap was ailing, and could barely get
his breath—
So, what did Corney do—but kick and batter him
to death.
That other chap was old, and wicked Corney’s
aged, too,
And wo guess he simply killed him ’cause he’d
nothing else to do.
Some heroes slaughter people and get crosses,
medals, stars—
Old Corney. he’s got nothing but dishonourable
scars.
“ No motive for the murder,” says his Honour on
the Bench ;
It wasn’t done for money, nor for love of any
wench,
But we can find a motive for this bloody deed and
black,
In those “marks of flagellation ” on the wretched
fellow’s back.
A few years ago, at the ceremonious open-
ing of Kyneton (Vic.) District Court, the present
writer was much shocked by the action of a cer-
tain iconoclastic blacksmith. Up in the gallery,
where all onlookers, reporters, &c.,
was a rough-looking personage in his every-day
attire, two special features of which consisted in
his retaining his leather apron and wearing his
battered hat on his head regardless of our
warning looks. lie stood erect noting the
numerous official mummeries with supercilious
scorn. Very soon the judge entered the court-
house, and forthwith the crier commenced his
asinine preamble, which, as usual, ended with
“ Oyez oyez, God Save the Queen !” Then, in a
loud, vigorous voice, this iireverend stranger
belched forth “ . the Queen!” and
immediately sat down. For nearly a full minute
there was a dreadful silence, and all the occupants
of the aforesaid gallery shook from head to toe,
and fixed their eyes on the judge. The fact that
truth is often as strange as fiction was in this
instance strikingly exemplified. Pretending not
to have heard anything the judge called on the
first case, and so the incident passed officially
unnoticed.

The well-informed London editor has
struck a new horror. He has ascertained that
Australian lunatics are disposed of by shipping
them secretly and silently to Samoa, where they
arc turned loose upon the mountains to live upon
grass and Providence. Presumably, they are
landed at night, and, though no details are sup-
plied, imagination can fill in the horrid picture.
A long, low schooner, painted black, heaves-to in
the darkness, and drops the cook into four fathoms
of water. The sun is setting violently in the east,
and behind the vessel are the dull, dark leaden
wastes of the Pacific, while in front is a low,
desolate shore, diversified only by a mountain
02,000 feet high. On the sides of it are bears,
camels, and pannikins, and on the summit the
infuriated jam-tin lies low in its lair, while the
yells of the Wclsh-rabbit arc heard in the dis-
tance. Behind the mountain there is a burning
desert eighty miles wide, full of ostriches and
boa-constrictors. Tha unprincipled boss-pirate
then commands, in a hoarse, agitated voice,
that the hatches be taken off and the maniacs
sorted out on deck, and the purser brings up the
bill of lading for 800 tons of incurable naked mad-
men. He also reports that starboard idiot No.
1721 is slightly damaged by water, and that a
quantity of bulk lunatics in the lower deck have
shifted in the recent gale and been considerably
deteriorated, while two hypochondriacs have been
short-shipped. Next the derrick is rigged, or else
the monkey-wrench, and the imbeciles, and the
land-boomers who have gone off their respective
chumps, and the homicidal patients are swung
ashore by their hair, all except one who drops
overboard in the confusion. When the last of
them is landed the schooner sets sail once more,
while her cargo starts away inland and scares
the inhabitants into fits. This is the story
as it ought to have appeared, if the London
editor had possessed any vestige of descrip-
tive power, but, alas, it lacks the great element
of hard-boiled accuracy. If the horrid truth
must be told, no one knows exactly what
becomes of the Australian lunatics, and
no one asks. They are shipped to Queensland,
and landed up one of the rivers, at a little wharf
beside a large factory. They go in at one door of
the building, and yet the natives of the place say
that they never come out at the other. The men
who work in the establishment are all largo and
fierce-looking, with projecting teeth and immense
under-jaws ; and dull thuds are heard in the place
accompanied by shrieks. If anyone asks the cause
of this row they laugh a sepulchral laugh and say
that they were splitting firewood. The river is
full of .squashed hats stained with blood, which
drift wearily up and down with the tide, and there
is a deposit of gory hats for miles along either
shore. All the employes in that factory have
several hundred misfit shirts, collars, suits of
do'lies, and boots apiece, and they always
decline to state where they got them, but
anybody who has missed a deranged relative
can go up there and he will invariably find
somebody wearing a collar or a shirt with the
lost friend’s name upon it. About once a week a
vessel comes alongside the wharf, and is loaded
up with boxes labelled “ox-tongue.” Where it
goes nobody knows, but travellers Voyaging across
the Pacific often come across a lot of floating
cargo, which makes them think that a line-of-
battle ship, filled with ox-tongue, has sunk there-
abouts. This is all that is really known about
Australian lunatics. They go, and they never
come back, and nothing remains but the squashed
hats and the ox-tongue to build a tale upon. If
the London editor already alluded to will kindly
accept this explanation, we will feel grateful. It
is always a genuine pleasure to enlighten a London
editor.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1388.
To H. F. Kugelmanm, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, jvhich
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that' time took a great
quantag of -medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting atraifuntil
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last i began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in-my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter 1
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill 1 consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
■end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, anil
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty's
